# Structure Is Love

# Chapter 1

At 4:12 a.m., the apartment was dark except for the microwave clock and the strip of hallway light under Ravi's door. Anika was already awake. She had been jolting up all night to the radiator's bone-knock, palms slick on the blanket until her chest stopped bucking. Six months ago, predawn had meant the brass diya on the kitchen shelf, two breaths while Maya cupped a match around the wick, and one careful spoon of sugar in tea because school mornings should start sweet even when everything else was late. Now predawn meant compression shorts, laces double-knotted, and a training shirt hanging from the bedroom doorknob like a warning.

She lay still and tried to make her body heavier than the blanket. Her calves twitched anyway. The first bus to the rec center left before the sky lightened; miss it and they stood under the pharmacy sign in diesel wind, bleeding minutes they did not have. Ravi repeated that every morning in the same tone he used for utility due dates and train transfers. He had not said Maya's name in this kitchen once.

The floorboard outside her room gave a soft, familiar pop. Then another sound, quieter: ceramic against counter, then the long scrape of a metal spoon in a pot. He was standing in Maya's spot by the stove. Anika knew it before she saw him. Every morning since the funeral he stood exactly where Maya used to stand when she packed two lunches at once, talking to herself in a low running list: yogurt cup, napkin, extra spoon, bus card, do not forget oranges.

Anika sat up too fast, and black static slid over her eyes for a second. She waited it out, then pulled on her socks and the shirt that still smelled like last night's detergent and old rubber from the court bag. In the mirror over her dresser she looked nine, not eleven. The collarbones were too sharp. Her braid from yesterday had kinked into a loose rope. She did not fix it.

Ravi was at the sink when she walked in, one hand braced against the counter, the other rubbing the outside of his left ankle in quick circles. He did that before every shift and every training block, as if the old injury could be negotiated with. The kitchen smelled like instant coffee and curry leaves burned in yesterday's tempering pan. The steel tumbler with dented sides, Maya's tumbler, was on the drying rack. He had turned it upside down so water could drain out. He had not put it away.

"Banana," he said without turning. "Half now, half after warm-up. Salt water in the blue bottle. We leave in six minutes."

Anika opened the fruit bowl and stared at the bruised bananas. "I didn't sleep."

"I know."

"Then maybe we skip this morning." She peeled half the banana but did not eat it. "I can do homework before school."

He set his mug down. "You can do homework tonight. Shoes."

She kept her voice flat because if it rose it might crack. "We used to light the lamp before school."

For a moment he did not move. The spoon in the pot ticked once as steam pushed it. When he spoke, he kept his eyes on the stove.

"We're not doing lamps at four in the morning. We're doing work."

"It takes thirty seconds."

"Niks."

She hated that nickname from his mouth when it sounded like a command. "You don't even look at her photo anymore."

Now he turned. There were crescents of purple under his eyes, and stubble he missed along his jawline. His voice was low enough that she had to lean in to catch it.

"I'm looking at you," he said. "That is what I've got."

The words landed harder than if he had shouted. He reached for the folded paper on the table, the one with columns and times and abbreviations she was learning to decode: ghosting, multiball, lunges, serve targets. Next to Monday through Sunday he had drawn boxes for initials.

"You think this is about squash," he said. "It isn't. This is how we keep you moving. If you stop, that room in your head gets bigger. You've felt it."

He did not say grief. Your mother stuck behind his teeth. What he could not say was that he was terrified of losing the only person left in this apartment.

Anika bit into the banana. It was too soft at the center, and she almost gagged. She forced it down with one swallow of salt water that tasted metallic from the old bottle cap. Her throat burned.

"Shoes," Ravi said again, quieter.

She tied them. That was the turn. It was not agreement, exactly; it was surrender of one small morning thing.

They took the stairs because the building elevator had been dead since January, a handwritten OUT OF SERVICE sign curling at the tape corners. On the second-floor landing Mrs. Kline's tabby, Jasper, watched through the railing with a torn blue ribbon still looped around his neck from Christmas. Outside, late-winter air punched the sweat out of Anika's skin before they reached the bus stop.

Ravi checked his watch, then the bus app, then the street. He spoke in short units while they waited, but this morning she made him earn each one.

"Today's full block. No cuts."

"On two hours of sleep?" Anika kept her eyes on the wet asphalt. "That's how people puke before six."

"People who hide behind that line puke," he said. "You're not one of them."

She huffed once. "Fine. Three ladders, but if my chest locks up we switch to straight drives."

"No switches," Ravi said. "If your chest spikes, you call it before you freeze, you take forty seconds, and you restart the rep. That's the deal."

She dragged her thumbnail along the bottle label. "Forty real seconds? Not your fake coach seconds?"

His mouth twitched, not quite a smile. "Timer's on the floor where you can see it. Say your reset."

She stared at the wet streak of headlights on pavement. "Long exhale, then feet."

"Again."

"Long exhale, then feet. Then move."

"Good."

The Q66 hissed open. They rode in the back because the front heater blasted too hot. Anika rested her forehead against cold window glass and counted storefront shutters and dark laundromat signs. At a red light she saw her reflection ghosted over a pharmacy awning. Her face flickered with the reflection of the bus's route map, lines and arrows crossing her cheeks like somebody else planned her day for her.

The rec center looked closed from outside, a block of concrete with one buzzing security light over the side entrance. Ravi had keys from his volunteer coaching badge. Inside, the hallway smelled like bleach and old sweat. Their footsteps echoed. Somewhere in the building a pipe knocked three times in a row.

On Court 2, the HVAC started and stopped in irregular blasts, a metallic rattle that sounded like someone shaking a toolbox above the ceiling. The wall paint was cracked in one high corner where years of hot balls had chipped it. Anika loved this court and hated it in equal measure. Here, nothing stayed hidden for long: bad footwork, bad timing, bad lies to yourself.

Ravi set his phone on the floor by the tin and started the timer. Its cracked screen threw pale blue light on his knuckles.

"Warm-up. Eight minutes."

Anika jogged lines from sidewall to sidewall, from the T to the back corner, and from front-right nick to front-left nick. Her shoes made squeaks that overlapped with the HVAC rattle until she could not tell where one sound ended and the next began. Sweat started at the base of her neck a few minutes in. Her lungs opened a little as the pace settled, and by the clap at eight her shirt was already damp.

"Ghosting ladder one. Twelve corners each sequence."

She bent, hands on thighs, waiting for the dizziness to settle.

"Ready," she said.

He nodded at the floor markers. "Go."

She drove to the front-right drop and recovered to the T. She cut to the front-left drop and recovered again. She reached the mid-right volley, recovered, and then dug the back-left before resetting. She tracked the order by the numbers in her head, running one through twelve and then reversing it before she repeated the whole sequence. On the second sequence her right quad started to tremble near the knee. On the third, the back of her throat tasted sour.

"Lower hips," Ravi said.

She dropped lower. Her shoes skidded on a sweat patch and she caught herself on the wall with her palm. Plaster dust stuck to her skin.

"You keep reaching from the waist," he said. "Move first or you're going to jam your knee."

"I'm moving. The floor's slick."

"Then call it slick and reset. Don't turn panic into technique."

She shot him a look and pushed off hard.

"Faster than that."

She wanted to tell him that faster than that was where the panic started. She went anyway. Recovery step, lunge, push off, recover. Her braid smacked the back of her neck in a wet, rhythmic slap.

At rep nine she heard a high electronic chirp from the hallway and froze for half a beat. It was only the front desk scanner, somebody swiping in early for the weight room, but the sound was wrong and too familiar. It was the hospital monitor at 3:11 a.m., then 3:12, then flat line replaced by the soft rush of oxygen and a nurse saying her name like she was both a child and witness.

She missed the next corner completely.

"Reset," Ravi said.

She did not move.

"Anika. Reset."

Her chest pulled tight, each inhale short and useless. She shook her head once like she could clear it physically. The court wall blurred. On the whiteboard outside, somebody had written COMMUNITY HOURS in red marker, and for a second the word COMMUNITY looked like CHEMOTHERAPY because letters moved when she got like this.

Ravi stepped closer but stopped two feet away, as if an invisible line on the floor marked the limit of what he could do.

"Long exhale," he said.

She tried. It came out as a cough.

"Again."

"I can't."

"You can. Slow it."

She dragged air in through her nose, held two counts, pushed it out through clenched teeth. Again. Again. The fourth one caught, and the fifth one landed. Her heartbeat was still loud but no longer random.

Ravi's voice shifted back to drill tone, almost brisk. "You lost one rep. You don't lose two. Start at eight."

"Can we do drives instead?"

"No."

"Please."

"No cuts. Start at eight."

There it was, the wall between them. She wanted him to say her name the way Maya used to say it when panic hit at dentist appointments or crowded festivals, palm warm at the back of her neck, voice soft and stupidly confident: We've got this, just breathe with me. Ravi gave her numbers, not comfort. Ravi gave her structure shaped like mercy and called it enough.

She started at eight.

By the end of ladder one, her shirt was fully soaked and sticking to her spine. He gave her forty seconds of rest, not a second more.

"Ladder two," he said. "Add split step before every front-court lunge."

Anika stared at him. "That's extra reps."

"That's efficiency."

"It's more."

"Yes."

The answer was clean, almost polite. It made her angrier than if he had snapped.

Ladder two burned different. The split steps loaded her calves with each transition until both legs felt wired and unstable. On the first back-corner recovery she felt a sharp stitch under her left rib and bit the inside of her cheek hard enough to taste blood. She kept moving.

"Head up," Ravi said.

"I'm trying."

"Do it, don't narrate it."

She almost laughed at that, because narrating was all she had when things went sideways. She counted floor tiles instead. Twelve along the short line. Nine from service box to sidewall. She made the court a map and ran it like a person trying not to think.

Halfway through the second sequence the memory came anyway, not as an image this time but as smell. Antiseptic wipes and heated plastic tubing. The hospital room door that never stayed fully closed. Maya's skin after radiation, dry and warm and oddly sweet with medicated lotion. Anika's foot caught late, and her knee thumped the floor.

Ravi flinched. Just once. A small forward jerk he killed before it became a step.

"Up," he said.

Anika pushed herself to standing. Her palm left a damp print on the court. "That hurt."

"Where?"

"Left knee. I slammed it on the turn."

"Sharp or blunt?"

"Blunt now, but it spikes when I plant."

Ravi's jaw jumped once. "Then keep moving and tell me the second it goes sharp. I'm not guessing today."

She swallowed whatever answer was coming. He did not ask about the memory; the smell got filed under noise and left there. He tracked only mechanics, as if pain had categories and only some of them counted.

They finished ladder two three minutes behind his target time. Ravi did not comment on the delay. He wrote numbers in a small notebook with a pen that had a bus-depot logo on it, likely stolen from the dispatch desk, the ink smudged where his hand rested too hard.

"Water," he said. "One minute. Then ladder three."

Anika drank too fast. Salt water hit her empty stomach and sat there like a hot stone.

"I need longer," she said.

"You need consistency."

"I'm going to puke."

"Then puke and reset."

She looked at him, waiting for the softening that did not come.

"I'm serious," he said. "Your body can do more than your panic says it can."

"How do you know?"

He paused, and when he spoke his voice was rougher, less practiced. "Because I watched your mother do chemo on four hours of sleep and still pack your lunch. Because I watched her stand up when she couldn't feel her hands. That's how I know."

The words left no room to hide. They were the closest he had come to saying fear out loud. His eyes slid away from hers as soon as he finished, back to the timer, back to the drill sheet.

"Ladder three," he said.

She nodded because her throat had closed again.

She cleared the opening sequence clean.

Rep six of the next one pinched her vision at the edges, and before the final turn the hot stone in her stomach had climbed into her throat.

She barely made it to the side door before she vomited into the janitor sink in the corridor. What came up was banana, salt water, and acid, with strings of spit clinging to the rust stain by the drain. Her abs cramped so hard she had to brace one hand against the cinderblock wall. A fluorescent tube overhead buzzed and flickered. In the next room somebody dropped a weight plate with a clang that shocked through her ribs.

Ravi stood in the doorway and handed her a paper towel roll without comment. He did not touch her. He did not look disgusted either. He only stood there, rigid, waiting.

She rinsed her mouth at the cold tap until the acid taste dulled. Her face in the scratched mirror looked feverish and furious.

"We're done," she said, testing him.

"We're done when you choose to stop, not when your stomach makes the call."

"I just threw up in a janitor sink."

"And you're still standing. Good. Now we train the part after empty."

She wanted to say something cruel. The sentence crowded her teeth and stayed there. She spat into the sink one last time, wiped her mouth, and walked back onto Court 2 before he could tell her to.

That was the moment she noticed it: her chest was still raw from crying that almost happened, but the grief image had slid out of focus. The hospital room was gone. The scanner chirp was gone. In their place she cataloged pure bodily data: calf cramp on the left side, blister hot spot under right arch, and salt crust drying at the corner of her lips. If she kept the effort high enough, there was no space left for the other thing.

Ravi tossed a ball lightly from hand to hand, watching her stance.

"Finisher," he said. "Suicides. Five sets."

She squared up on the service line. "Five full?"

"Five full."

"You said three yesterday."

"Yesterday you did not finish ghosting."

"I almost passed out."

"Today you're still standing. Go."

The first sprint felt clean enough to fake control. On the next one, she turned late at the back wall. Soon her lungs felt like tearing cloth; she clipped the T with her shoe, stumbled, caught herself, and kept going until there was no Anika making choices, only movement stitched from command and refusal.

When she crossed the final line, she dropped to hands and knees. Sweat dripped from her nose and pooled on the lacquered floor. Her heart was a fist punching inside her sternum. She waited for the grief wave to crash in while she was vulnerable.

It did not come.

That absence scared her almost as much as the grief itself.

Ravi knelt beside her then, not touching, but close enough that she could smell coffee on his breath.

"Look at me," he said.

She did.

"You did the full block."

She nodded once.

"You wanted proof you could stay in it. That's proof."

Her voice was shredded. "It shut off."

He frowned. "What shut off?"

She searched for a word he would accept. "Noise."

He exhaled through his nose, slow. "Good. Use that."

Use that. It sounded like a tool in the bag with tape and extra grips. Maybe it was.

They cooled down with slow ghosting, no timer. Her legs wobbled on the stairs to the locker room. In the shower, hot water needled her skin until pink turned to red. She pressed her forehead to tile and felt the pulse in her gums. Her stomach was hollow and angry. She ate the second half of the banana anyway while standing under the hand dryer because there was no time.

On the bus to school, Ravi typed on his phone with two thumbs, probably dispatch messages for the morning shift. He did not look up when he spoke.

"You can sleep twenty minutes when we get home tonight. Not before homework."

Anika leaned against the window. "I wasn't planning to sleep in class."

"Plan harder."

"You make everything sound like a drill."

He pocketed the phone. "Because drills still reach you."

The line should have started a fight, but she was too tired to throw it back. At her stop, he handed her lunch container and a folded index card.

"Schedule for the week," he said. "Read at lunch. We sign tonight."

She unfolded it on the sidewalk. Black marker scored the card so hard the letters raised on the back: 4:30 court most weekdays, one recovery-and-footwork day in the middle, match play Saturday, film and mobility Sunday. Bottom line: NO MISSED MORNINGS WITHOUT MEDICAL REASON.

He tapped the card once. "Structure is love, Niks. Remember that."

He said it like a promise and a warning in the same breath. She pinched the card until the paper edge dented her skin.

Then he was gone toward the bus stop, shoulders squared against the wind, lunch he forgot still on the kitchen counter at home.

School started at 8:05, and by second period Anika could feel the dawn block hardening inside her legs. The classroom heat was turned too high, dry and stale, and the fluorescent hum stacked until every voice sounded wrapped in cloth. She kept her pencil moving through math warm-up and verb drills so no one saw the tremor in her hand.

Halfway through social studies, her chin dropped once toward her notebook. Ms. Alvarez called her name, and the room went quiet in the way middle-school rooms did when somebody got caught.

"Anika, stay after the bell."

When the class emptied, Ms. Alvarez closed the door and lowered her voice. "This is the third time this week you've almost fallen asleep in my room."

"I'm fine," Anika said. "I just got up early."

"Your hands are shaking." Ms. Alvarez nodded toward the pencil marks jagged across the worksheet. "If this keeps up, I have to loop in the counselor. I already emailed home once."

Anika's stomach dropped harder than it had during suicides. "Please don't do that. I can fix this this week."

"I already did," Ms. Alvarez said, not unkindly. "Thursday after school. You, me, and the counselor."

"Can we do Friday?" Anika asked too fast. "Thursday I have training, then a bus transfer, and if I miss the slot I get home after dark."

"This isn't a slot problem," Ms. Alvarez said. "This is you nodding off in class. Thursday."

By the time Anika got to lunch, tomato soup had skinned over in the cup, and her phone was vibrating under the table. Ravi: `Got teacher email. Don't miss bus. 3:18 Court 2.`

Across from her, Zoe stabbed at a tangle of cafeteria spaghetti and watched her over the steam.

"Was that your dad again?" Zoe asked.

"Yeah. Schedule check."

Zoe pushed her tray forward. "We're supposed to finish the migration poster today. You bailed yesterday."

"I can't stay," Anika said. "I've got court."

"You've got court every day." Zoe's laugh came out flat. "That's not busy. That's your dad running your pulse from his phone."

Anika's spoon clicked against the cup. "Drop it, Zoe."

"No. One question." Zoe leaned in. "If you told him no for one day, what happens?"

Anika looked down at the soup. "I said I can't."

Zoe held her stare for one more second, then stood, tray rattling. "Cool. Then don't ask me to keep covering for you with teachers."

She walked off before Anika could answer, chipped black polish flashing against the plastic tray edge.

At 3:24, Anika reached Court 2 with her backpack still on and her school ID lanyard slapping against her sternum. Ravi already had the timer running and the ball tin open.

"You're six minutes late," he said.

"Ms. Alvarez held me." She dropped her bag and pulled off her hoodie. "She emailed you."

"I know." He did not ask why; his jaw was already set. "Six-corner ghosting. Forty seconds on, twenty off. Six rounds."

The first two rounds burned the leftovers of morning fatigue out of her hips. By round four, the blister under her right arch split open and soaked her sock warm. She clipped the front wall, caught herself, and tasted copper.

"I need water," she said.

"After the round."

"I'm bleeding through the sock."

He handed her tape from his bag without stopping the timer. "Wrap it on the break. Keep moving."

She finished the round, taped the arch so tight it throbbed, and went straight into drive-volley lock. She restarted once on backhand volleys. There were no speeches and no collapse this time, only the mechanical grind of thirty more minutes until her shoulder shook and the ball finally started dying low off the front wall.

On the bus home, Ravi's phone buzzed twice with the school district number. He read the screen and swore under his breath.

"What?" Anika asked.

"Counselor wants both of us Thursday," he said. "They're calling it a fatigue concern."

She watched the rain bead on the window and smear into long gray lines. "You mad at me?"

"I'm mad at timing," he said. "If school starts documenting this, they can flag attendance, and every tournament day becomes a fight. We don't have room for another fight."

By the time she reached home, evening had flattened into that gray-blue hour when the apartment windows reflected the room instead of the street. The kitchen light was on. The smell of mustard seed and onion hung in the air, and a pot of rice sat covered on the stove. Ravi was at the table with the same folded schedule from the morning, now rewritten on clean lined paper in thick black strokes, as if harder pen pressure could keep the day from splitting open.

He had added checkboxes for morning pulse, post-session soreness, homework completion, and class alertness, and printed `EAT` in capital letters across the lunch slot. In the lower right corner he had drawn two signature lines: `Anika Iyer` and `Ravi Iyer.`

When she sat, he slid a steel tumbler of water toward her without meeting her eyes. The gesture was quick, almost tender, then gone.

"Eat first," he said.

They ate in near silence. Rice, dal, one spoon of pickle too sharp on Anika's scraped throat. Ravi asked about homework, the counselor email, and the alarm in the same clipped tone he used courtside, but his knee kept bouncing under the table hard enough to rattle the chair.

After dishes, he taped the schedule to the fridge with two magnets: one from the Queens Oncology fundraiser 5K Maya ran before she got sick, and one shaped like a cartoon bus from his depot holiday party. The paper hung slightly crooked and started to curl at the corners. He pressed it flat with both thumbs, then straightened it again.

"Read it out," he said.

Anika read each line aloud: times, blocks, school, mobility work, Saturday match play at 7:00 a.m., Sunday film review after laundry, no missed mornings without medical reason, counselor meeting Thursday, and no detours after last bell. By the last line, her own voice sounded like it belonged to someone older and meaner.

"Any changes?" he asked.

"Yeah," she said, before she could swallow it. "This is costing me people. Zoe told me I'm disappearing. My teacher thinks I'm too tired to stay in class. My foot bled through my sock and you still ran the timer. I need one morning to sleep, and I need one morning where we light the lamp and say Amma's name like she existed."

Ravi's face went still in a way that was worse than shouting. For three heartbeats he said nothing. Then he took the pen, crossed out `THU 4:30 A.M. COURT`, and wrote `THU 5:15 A.M. LIGHT + MOBILITY` in smaller block letters.

"That's the change," he said. "One morning. Not a collapse."

He set the pen down, looked at her directly this time, and tapped the line he had just added.

"But tomorrow and Friday stay. If school is watching, we show them structure, not chaos."

He held out the pen again. "Sign."

Anika signed her name, each letter careful because her hand was shaking. Ravi signed below hers in fast slanted script. He capped the pen and placed it on top of the fridge where they used to keep temple envelopes and spare birthday candles.

For a second they stood shoulder to shoulder looking at the paper, both breathing like they had just finished a rally neither one wanted to lose.

"4:12 alarm," Ravi said. "I'll wake you if you miss it."

"I won't miss it," Anika said.

She believed herself.

When she went to her room, she could feel tomorrow already waiting in her calves, taped to the fridge in black marker.


# Chapter 2

The schedule Ravi had taped to the fridge the night before was already curling at the corners, even after he had pressed it flat with both thumbs. Black marker blocks filled the page: court, shower, school, commute, court again. The only white space sat between 12:02 and 12:26, lunch period, and even that had `EAT` written across it in hard capital letters.

Both signatures still sat at the bottom margin, his fast slanted line under her careful letters, as if ink could hold the week together.

At 6:38 a.m., after ninety minutes of ghosting drills and one bus transfer in wet cold, Anika stood in the apartment kitchen in socks that smelled like damp rubber. The radiator clicked without warming the room. Ravi worked the stove with one hand and his phone with the other, leaning his hip against the counter to spare his stiff left ankle. Mustard seeds popped for his eggs; curry leaf smoke threaded into the steam from her instant oatmeal.

"You're three minutes behind," he said, still looking at the pan. "Eat while it's hot."

Anika sat, opened her math notebook, and forced down two spoonfuls. Her throat still tasted like iron from sprint repeats.

Ravi's phone buzzed. He had already built her afternoon in his calendar: bus to rec center, dynamic warm-up, feet-and-lunge circuit, thirty serve targets. He forwarded it anyway. Her own phone lit up on the table with his name and the same plan in short lines.

`Leave school gate 3:22.`
`Route Q49 or walk to 74 if late.`
`No detours.`
`Text when you're on bus.`

He finally glanced at her bowl. "You ate half yesterday and cramped."

"I'm eating."

He pointed his spoon at the oatmeal, then at the wall clock. "Finish it. You need fuel."

The words were practical. The tone was practical. The feeling in the room was not practical at all. It was the feeling of being watched while she chewed.

She got through three more spoonfuls and chased them with water because her stomach had started climbing into her throat. Ravi slid a banana toward her lunch bag and a folded napkin with almonds counted into a neat stack, twelve exactly.

"You look pale," he said. "Did you sleep?"

"Yeah."

"How many hours?"

"Six. Maybe six and a half."

He started to correct her estimate, then stopped. "Tonight you need seven. We can't keep wasting recovery."

He said `we` when he meant her body.

By the time she got to school, her fingers were still wrinkled from the too-hot shower she had used to scrub off court smell. A clean hoodie did not help. Rubber ball, bleach mop water, and old sweat clung to the fabric like a second skin.

In homeroom, fluorescent lights drilled straight through her molars. Social studies started with a worksheet about city government. She wrote her name, watched the letters drag across the page, and stalled at question one: Explain the role of public oversight in municipal decision-making.

Ms. DeMarco moved between desks with iced coffee in one hand and hall passes in the other. She taught with a soft Queens rasp and twisted a fox-shaped ring on her thumb whenever she was trying not to snap at kids.

"Morning, team," she said. "Ten minutes silent work, then we'll discuss. Heads up, no phones out."

Anika pressed her pencil harder so the graphite left grooves she could feel through the paper. She answered two questions by memory, one by guessing, and then lost three minutes in a blink that did not feel like a blink. Her chin dropped. The pencil slipped from her fingers and carved a dark line across the worksheet and onto her wrist.

A hand tapped her desk once.

"Anika."

She jerked up so fast her chair screeched. Jerome in the back snorted. Zoe, two rows left, turned in her seat with one eyebrow raised.

Ms. DeMarco crouched beside the desk instead of calling her out across the room. It made everything worse.

"Walk with me for a second," the teacher murmured.

The hallway was empty except for a custodian rolling a trash bin toward the stairwell. Anika stood against cinderblock while Ms. DeMarco checked the worksheet and then checked her face.

"You fell asleep mid-sentence," Ms. DeMarco said quietly. "Are you sick?"

Anika wiped graphite off her wrist with her sleeve. "No."

"Did you eat breakfast?"

"Yeah."

"Are you getting enough sleep at home?"

Anika kept her eyes on the chipped blue stripe painted across the wall. "I'm fine. I just woke up early."

Ms. DeMarco waited, like patience might pull a truer answer out of the first one. When nothing came, she twisted the fox ring once and exhaled.

"You don't have to tell me everything," she said. "But you just fell asleep on a worksheet. If this keeps happening, I call home and loop in the counselor. I can't ignore it."

Anika's stomach folded tighter. "Please don't call my dad. I'm okay."

"I'm sending a quick email now so we can get ahead of it. You're one of my strongest kids, and this isn't your usual."

The email already existed in Anika's head before Ms. DeMarco finished the sentence: Concern about fatigue in class. She could see Ravi opening it between dispatch calls, jaw tightening, turning worry into rules.

"I said I'm fine," Anika said. The second time came out flatter.

Ms. DeMarco did not argue. "Go splash water on your face, then come back in."

In the bathroom, Anika cupped cold tap water against her skin until it stung. Her eyes looked puffy, purple moons sat under both, and damp roots still clung to her scalp from the morning shower. She held her breath and counted to twelve the way she counted ghost reps. At eleven, her phone buzzed in her hoodie pocket even though it should have been off.

Dad: `How was first period?`

She stared.

She typed: `Fine.`

Three dots appeared almost immediately.

Dad: `Teacher said you nodded off.`
Dad: `Send lunch photo before bell.`
Dad: `If you don't reply, I call the main office.`

Her lie felt soft for one second, then set like plaster. `Fine` had been one word. It hardened fast.

When she got back to class, Ms. DeMarco drew a flowchart on the board. "Oversight isn't punishment," she said. "In theory, oversight protects people when one person has too much unchecked control."

Anika copied the sentence into the margin because it sounded like something she should remember, even if she did not know where to put it yet.

By second-period math, hunger had turned mean. It bit, vanished, then bit again under her ribs. She ate the almonds during independent practice, one at a time, hiding each bite behind her hand. The worksheet held fractions; her brain kept substituting route numbers and court times. 3:22 gate. 4:05 court. 4:12 first drill.

At 10:47 another message arrived while classes changed.

Dad: `Main office copied me on the wellness note.`
Dad: `If anyone asks, say you had insomnia.`
Dad: `Do not mention 4:12 drills.`

She read the last line twice. It was the first time he had written the concealment out plain.

In science, the room smelled like vinegar and old aquarium water. Mr. Patel asked for volunteers. Zoe raised her hand before the instructions were finished because Zoe always liked anything that might explode. While Mr. Patel measured baking soda, Zoe leaned back and whispered without moving her lips.

"You look dead," Zoe said. "Like Victorian dead, not regular dead."

Anika snorted once despite herself. "Thanks."

"I'm serious. You okay?"

"Yeah."

Zoe squinted. "That was a fast yeah."

Anika pulled her science notebook closer and pointed at the board as if acid-base reactions had suddenly become urgent. The demo foamed over the beaker lip. Kids cheered. Mr. Patel laughed and cursed when fizz hit his sleeve.

Under the noise, her phone buzzed again.

Dad: `Counselor request came through.`
Dad: `I told them no intervention needed.`
Dad: `Do not contradict me at school.`

Zoe caught the movement and lifted both eyebrows. "You're texting in Patel's class? Bold."

"It's just my dad."

"Everything okay at home?"

The wording was casual. The way Zoe asked was not casual at all.

Anika tucked the phone away. "Yeah. He's just being annoying."

Zoe nodded, but she kept watching.

Lunch started with rain tapping the cafeteria windows and a pizza line snaking past the vending machines. Anika took the standard tray: square slice, milk carton, bruised pear. She was not hungry until food sat in front of her. Then she was starving and nauseated at the same time.

Zoe dropped into the seat across from her, backpack still on one shoulder. "My mom said I can go to Astoria Park if I finish homework first. You wanna come? Me, you, maybe Dani if she stops fake-flirting with eighth graders."

Anika peeled the pear sticker and stuck it to the table edge. "I can't. I've got practice."

"You always have practice."

"It's Tuesday."

"So? Tuesdays still have sky."

Anika tried to smile. Zoe drummed chipped black nails against her milk carton.

"I'm not asking you to skip your whole life," Zoe said. "Just one hour. We hit the deli, we get fries, we do math on a bench, we pretend we're normal middle school idiots for sixty minutes."

The thought of fries made Anika's stomach lurch, half desire, half acid.

Her phone buzzed.

Dad: `Lunch pic now.`
Dad: `Finish tray or warm-up becomes suicides.`
Dad: `Leave gate 3:22. Rain slows buses.`

She angled the tray, snapped a photo before Zoe could see, and sent it.

Zoe watched her thumb move and leaned back. "Wow. You have parole-officer texts."

"Shut up."

"I'm not joking, Niks."

Anika bristled at the nickname and the tenderness inside it. "It's not like that. He's tracking practice, not my whole life."

"Then what's it like?"

Anika bit pizza crust and chewed too long so she would not have to answer. She could feel Ms. DeMarco's email hanging over the day like a second fluorescent light. She could feel Ravi building rules from every adult who noticed.

"It's temporary," she said finally. "My sleep got messed up. That's all."

"From what?"

"From waking up at 4:12 and getting lunch audits by text."

"That's not temporary, Niks."

Anika set down the crust. "I can't do this every lunch, Zoe."

"Do what."

"The home questions. The dad questions. Don't ask me again at school."

A ketchup packet burst at the next table, red fan on dirty tile. A lunch aide shouted for paper towels. Under the cafeteria roar, Zoe lowered her voice.

"Is this about your mom?"

Anika's throat closed on dry crust. She reached for milk that was already gone.

"I'm fine," she said, crushing the carton until the seam popped and milk dampened her knuckles. "It's sleep. It's practice."

Zoe slumped forward. "You keep saying that like you're reading from a card."

"I mean it, Zoe. Don't do this at school," Anika said, and heard how dead she sounded.

The words left chalk on her tongue.

Zoe sat back. Hurt flashed across her face, then annoyance, then the ghost of a joke that never arrived. "Okay," she said. "Cool. Be fine."

She opened her sketchbook and started drawing the cafeteria salt shaker with violent little strokes. Anika watched graphite carve shadow and forgot to ask about Zoe's art magnet open house, the one Zoe had mentioned twice this week.

Her screen lit again.

Dad: `Did you finish tray? yes/no.`
Dad: `I need the same answer if school follows up.`

She typed `yes` even though half the pizza was still there.

Dad: `Good.`
Dad: `Pack at 3:18.`

The afternoon dragged and jumped. In English she missed two inferencing questions because she read the same paragraph four times and kept seeing route numbers instead of words. In art, paint thinner gave her a headache. At 2:41, Ms. DeMarco appeared at the classroom door and asked to see her in the hallway again.

"I heard back from your dad," Ms. DeMarco said quietly. "He says this is a new training routine and he's monitoring your food, sleep, and movement."

Anika's pulse stuttered. "He told you that?"

"He told me enough to worry me."

Anika stared at the locker vents. Rain rattled the stairwell windows.

Ms. DeMarco kept her voice low. "Hear me. When a grown-up controls your food, your time, and who you see with threats, that's coercive control. Calling it coaching doesn't make it okay."

The phrase hit harder than any of Ravi's texts had. She had felt the shape of it all day and never named it.

"I'm not trying to blow up your life," Ms. DeMarco went on. "But you keep falling asleep in class and freezing every time that phone lights up. If that keeps up, I file before dismissal. That means counselor first, then assistant principal, and maybe ACS screening. I'm not threatening you. I'm telling you what happens next."

The institutional consequence was spelled out in hallway light.

Anika gripped her sleeve seam until thread cut into her finger. "Please don't file anything today. I'll fix it."

"You can't fix this alone," Ms. DeMarco said. "And you shouldn't have to."

"Just give me one day."

Ms. DeMarco studied her for a long beat, then nodded once. "One day. Tomorrow, first period, we check again. If you nod off in my room, I file."

Anika nodded.

As she turned back toward class, Ms. DeMarco added, "You're eleven. You're allowed to act eleven. Remember that."

The line followed her inside and sat there like an impossible assignment.

At 3:11 the warning chime rang. Backpacks zipped. Chairs scraped. Hallway noise surged toward stairwells. Zoe caught Anika at her locker, breathless, holding two bus cards like a magic trick.

"I topped this one for you," Zoe said. "Grandma gave me ten bucks for helping with laundry. Don't fight me. We're going to the park, we split fries, we do math on a bench, and we act normal for one hour."

Anika froze with one sneaker half-tied. The lace bit into her fingers. Her pulse kept thudding in her throat like the warning chime had moved under her skin.

"I can't take your card."

"You can. It's ten dollars, not a kidney."

"I've got to go."

The words came out thin, almost polite.

Zoe stepped in front of the locker before Anika could close it. "Practice can wait one day."

"No, it can't."

"Why? Is your dad gonna send a SWAT team?"

Her phone buzzed once, then again, then a third time.

Dad: `3:18. Pack now.`
Dad: `Where are you?`
Dad: `Gate photo in one minute.`

Each vibration jumped through her pocket seam and into her jaw. She did not show Zoe. She shoved books into her backpack and missed the zipper track the first time.

Zoe's voice lost its joke layer. "Tell me what's going on."

"My dad hears a late minute like it's a smoke alarm. That's what's going on."

"That's bullshit."

"If I'm late, he cuts warm-up and adds suicides."

"So that's normal now? Your dad runs your day by text and I'm supposed to shut up?"

Ms. DeMarco's phrase from the hallway flashed hard and clean in her head: coercive control. Hearing Zoe say it in friend voice made Anika want to peel out of her own skin.

The hallway thinned as kids poured toward the stairs. Rain hammered the stairwell glass. Anika could feel each second like a countdown beep.

"I've got training," she said. "He's already asking for gate proof."

"You haven't explained anything."

"I don't have time to miss this bus and pay for it on court."

"Right. You've got time for ladders and ghosting and your dad's control texts, but you don't have one hour for me."

She could already see Court 3 in her head: orange cones, Ravi's watch, the look he wore when minutes became punishment.

Anika yanked the zipper free where it had caught fabric.

"Zoe."

"What."

"I've got ninety seconds to make the stop."

Zoe laughed once, sharp. "You mean friendship? Yeah. I noticed."

Her chest pinched so hard she almost said the true sentence: I'm scared of what happens if I'm late.

That landed harder than morning drills. Anika wanted to say she was sorry. She wanted to say please wait. She wanted to say she did not know how to hold grief and school and Ravi's fear and hunger and still sound like herself. What came out was the smallest, safest sentence.

"I'm fine."

Zoe stared at her for a long second, then folded the extra bus card in half and shoved it back into her pocket.

"Have fun being fine," she said, and turned for the stairs without looking back.

Anika stood by the locker bank while the corridor emptied. Another vibration crawled up her pocket seam.

Dad: `Miss this bus and we add fourteen suicides.`
Dad: `Move.`

She moved.

Outside, rain slid down the back of her neck. She pulled up her hood and ran the half block to the Q49 stop, backpack pounding between her shoulder blades. The bus arrived crowded and breathing diesel. She wedged into the aisle, one hand on a wet pole, and sent a gate photo through the fogged window.

Dad: `Good.`
Dad: `Sip water now.`

She obeyed automatically, unscrewing the bottle with numb fingers. Water tasted like plastic and old metal. At the next light, two girls from school crossed in front of the bus sharing earbuds, laughing hard enough that one had to grab the other's sleeve to stay upright. Anika watched them until the doors hissed shut.

She opened her notes app. It started as logistics and slipped toward confession:

`social studies asleep 8:19`
`Ms D said coercive control`
`welfare report if tomorrow same`
`Zoe's face when she said Be fine`
`lunch finished (not true)`
`gate 3:24`

The list sat there like evidence she had typed against herself.

Rain smeared the bus window until Queens looked waterlogged and unreal. Then the rec center pushed through the blur: chain-link fence, wet concrete, windows bleeding dull yellow. Rubber-ball echo leaked through the doors before she touched the handle.

Ravi stood by the vending machine in depot uniform, reflective stripes at his cuffs, eyes on the clock. He checked her wet hoodie, then her phone screen, then his own.

"Gate photo says 3:24," he said. "Schedule says 3:22."

"Zoe stopped me at my locker."

"Then you keep walking next time." He turned toward Court 3. "Warm-up is gone. Fourteen suicides for the delay, then ladder."

"Dad, the bus was--"

"No. You don't lose minutes and negotiate."

Court 3 smelled like wet rubber and old mop water. Two middle-school boys on the next court went quiet when Ravi set orange cones on the service line and back wall. The phrase Ms. DeMarco had used in the hallway came back hard: coercive control. Here it looked like stopwatch math, three late minutes turned into fourteen suicides in front of witnesses. He clicked his stopwatch and pointed.

"Touch the line every turn. Hand down so I can see it."

Anika dropped her backpack by the glass and started running. Baseline to short line, backpedal, baseline again. On six, her calves knotted. On nine, acid climbed the back of her throat. On eleven, her right palm slapped varnish a fraction late.

"That one didn't touch," Ravi said. "Again."

She ran it again with her hand flat. Then one more because she stumbled at the wall and he kept the watch moving.

By fourteen, her lungs were tearing at air and black dots swam in the corners of her vision. Kids from the next session filed past the glass pretending not to look. A seventh-grade girl she recognized from Saturday clinics did look, then looked away so fast her ponytail snapped.

When Ravi finally said stop, Anika bent over the trash can and vomited thin milk and bile while her hamstrings trembled. Her chest burned. Spit hung from her lip and hit the rim.

Ravi wrote `+14` on the printout clipped to his board. "Now we start," he said. "No overthinking."

The phrase landed like a lock turning.

Anika wiped her mouth with the back of her wrist, straightened, and stepped back onto the service box because she did not know what else to do with a body that had already been made into proof.


# Chapter 3

The next afternoon, less than twenty-four hours after he had stood on Court 3 saying no overthinking, Ravi brought home boxes that still smelled like oranges and detergent, flattened them on the kitchen floor, and stood each one up like he was building lanes for traffic. The apartment was quiet in the way it got quiet only after morning drills, after school, after the two buses and one transfer that left Anika with rubber-ball echo still ticking in her ears. It was late enough that the light coming through the narrow kitchen window had gone from white to the thin gray of late winter, and the radiator hissed without warming anything above ankle height.

He wrote in black marker on each box flap: `DONATE`, `KEEP`, `TRASH`, `MAYBE`. Then he crossed out `MAYBE` so hard the cardboard tore and wrote `DONATE 2` over it.

"We can finish this before five," he said. "Pickup window is four-thirty to six. If we stack by the door by four, we won't miss them. Tomorrow we'll do the hall closet."

Anika was still in her school sweater, one sleeve damp where she'd wiped sweat off her neck on the bus. Her calves buzzed from ghosting drills at dawn. She watched him tear tape with his teeth and smooth each strip down with the side of his thumb, fast and exact, as if bad tape would cost him minutes he did not have. He shifted weight off his left ankle when he turned, a hitch she only noticed when he thought she was not looking.

"Start with kitchen shelves," he said. "No overthinking. We have to clear space."

He said it like an instruction at court: no overthinking. Move your feet. Get to the ball. She went to the first cabinet anyway and froze with her hand on the knob.

The top shelf held three stainless-steel canisters with dented lids, one plastic jar of tamarind paste crusted at the rim, and two tea tins Maya had reused for cumin and fenugreek long after the labels peeled off. The smell hit before she touched anything. It carried stale curry leaf in metal, cardamom dust, and overboiled tea that had lived in the walls for years. Behind that was something floral and dry that should have been impossible six months after the funeral and still arrived like a slap: jasmine hair oil and the cheap hospital hand soap Maya brought home on her skin.

Anika took down one canister, then put it back, then took it down again.

Ravi had already filled half a box with duplicate mixing bowls and extra mugs from the top rack. "Anything chipped goes," he said. "Anything we haven't used in months goes."

"We use those," she said, pointing at the tea strainer with the bent handle.

"We have two." He held one up. "See?"

"The bent one doesn't drip."

He looked at her like he was deciding whether this was true, then dropped both strainers into `DONATE` and reached for the next shelf. The metal clicked against a glass jar. She flinched hard enough that he paused.

"Niks."

She hated when he used the short name in command voice. "What?"

"Don't do this all night. Pick and move."

She set the canister down on the counter and opened it. Masoor dal sat like red lenses against steel. Maya's handwriting on white masking tape around the side read `masoor / quick` in dark blue pen, slanted right, the tail of the `q` cutting under the word like an underline. The letters made something in Anika's chest pull tight and sharp.

She was nine again and standing on a chair at this same counter while Maya tapped a spoon against the rim of a pot. Not now, little engineer, Maya had said, smiling without looking up, because Anika kept asking if boiling changed protein. Count to sixty before you stir. Not forty. Sixty. She had smelled onions and mustard seed and the bitter edge of burned garlic because Maya answered a text and forgot to lower the flame. Anika had laughed. Maya had muttered, "Damn it, burned garlic again," soft and annoyed at herself, then fed her the rescue batch out of the pot with a spoon too hot for a kid's mouth.

The memory landed full-body, not like thinking but like being dropped back into old weather.

Ravi was still moving. He opened and shut drawers. He made one pile for forks and another for cracked containers. Plastic lids rained on the counter. He worked by categories, not by object. She worked by object and did not move.

"This one stays," she said, touching the tape label.

"One canister set stays," he said. "Not all six."

"There aren't six."

"Three here, three in the pantry."

"Then keep four."

"Keep three."

"Why three?"

"Because we're two people, not six."

The sentence sat there, too plain, too finished. They were two people, and that was all he would allow the room to hold. She counted backward from twenty because her face was getting hot and she did not want to cry in front of open cabinets and old cumin.

"If we stack the heavy ones in the bottom box," she said, voice flat, "the pickup guys can carry faster and the handles won't rip."

It was not an answer. It was logistics. She heard herself do it and could not stop.

Ravi nodded once, relieved to have procedure back. "Good. Do that."

They worked thirty more minutes in mismatched rhythm. Ravi's side of the counter cleared to wood grain. Anika's side became careful little islands: one chipped measuring cup with turmeric shadow in the numbers, one rolling pin with a hairline crack near the handle, one packet of cloves expired two years ago, one cloth bag of dried red chiles Maya had hidden behind rice because they were too spicy for Ravi.

At one point she opened the cutlery drawer and found a stack of folded paper napkins with Maya's grocery list written on top. `onion (small)`, `curd`, `sanitary pads`, `AA battery`, `orange if cheap`. The words were compressed, practical, no extra loops, written in the margin of an old pharmacy receipt. She held the paper to her face like that could bring smell back. It smelled like drawer dust and cumin.

"Trash that," Ravi said without looking. "It's old."

"It's writing."

"It's a list."

"It's her writing."

He stopped taping. The silence after tape noise felt violent.

"Anika." He put both hands on the counter and leaned in, voice ironed flat, thumbs pressing pale half-moons into the laminate. "If we make every piece of paper sacred, we'll drown in this apartment."

"I'm not drowning."

"You are. You just call it something else."

She stared at the list until the `g` in `orange` blurred. He reached for it. She folded it in half and slid it into her sweater pocket before he could touch it.

His jaw moved once to the side, the way it did when he was swallowing words. "Fine," he said. "One list. Keep moving."

By three-thirty, they had six boxes by the entryway and one bag of garbage tied at the neck with a knot too tight for bare hands. Ravi made tea without asking if she wanted any, too much milk, too much sugar, the way he made it when he was trying to keep his hands from shaking. He stood at the sink while it boiled over and did not curse, only turned the flame down and scraped the sticky line off the burner with a spoon.

"After tea, bedroom closet," he said. "That's the hard one. Better to do it now."

Anika wrapped both hands around the steel tumbler and watched steam stripe the kitchen window. He had called it the hard one, as if there had been an easy one.

The bedroom closet door stuck in winter, swollen from damp, and Ravi had to shoulder it open hard enough that the track groaned and the hangers inside rattled. Cold air from the back wall slid out across Anika's shins. Then the smell followed: naphthalene from mothballs, sandalwood from old drawers, the faint clean-sour trace of detergent Maya used because it was cheap and came in giant powder bags, not pods. Saris hung in a narrow block at the back, each one in a clear plastic cover Maya had slit at the top so the fabric could breathe. On the floor sat two canvas bins of folded sweaters and one shoe box full of scarves.

Ravi brought in three empty bags from the supermarket and opened them with quick snaps. "Winter coats we keep. Work clothes maybe we donate. Shoes mostly donate."

"Mostly?"

"One or two for memory. We're not running a museum."

Anika crouched at the sweater bin and lifted a navy cardigan she had seen every morning for years, usually with a pen clipped upside down to the collar because Maya forgot where she put pens. The cardigan smelled like old wool and Vicks and the lavender packets Priya had mailed last summer. She pressed the cuff to her upper lip, eyes closed.

Memory came again, concrete and cruel: Maya at the table at midnight, cardigan on over hospital scrubs, trying to help Anika memorize science vocabulary while fighting nausea that made her hold her breath between words. "Evaporation," Maya had said, tapping the flashcard with one finger. "It means something leaves and still belongs to the water cycle. That can happen."

Anika had rolled her eyes because she was ten and angry at everything. "That's not how belonging works."

"It is tonight," Maya had said, then thrown up in the sink and come back five minutes later with mint toothpaste still on her tongue, pretending nothing had happened.

Anika folded the cardigan and set it in the keep pile.

Ravi glanced over. "One cardigan, okay."

She nodded, then picked up a green salwar kameez with one missing button and held it long enough that he noticed.

"Donate that," he said.

"Why?"

"Because it's torn and you won't wear it."

"I could fix it."

"You don't sew."

"I can learn."

"Not this week."

His tempo was faster now, voice clipped around time markers. He checked his phone every few minutes for the pickup message as if the driver were a proctor and late fees might follow if he missed the slot. He folded without looking at labels, by weight and shape only. Every clean fold looked like an erasure.

Anika moved to the shoe box of scarves. Beneath two cotton scarves lay Maya's old work ID lanyard with the hospital logo half rubbed off. She touched the plastic badge sleeve and felt her throat close. There was no badge inside, just a faded transit card and one movie ticket stub from a night Anika had not gone because she had fever and Maya stayed home with her while Ravi went alone and came back with popcorn in a paper cup. The stub was for a Tamil movie Maya had called terrible and secretly loved.

"Can I keep this?" Anika held up the lanyard.

Ravi did not turn around. "What is it?"

"Her lanyard."

"Keep. Small things are fine."

Small things are fine. The rule sounded practical until she realized it meant he had decided what size grief was allowed.

In the top closet shelf sat two shoeboxes of papers and one plastic pouch with jewelry: glass bangles wrapped in newspaper, one chain with a tiny Om pendant, one digital wristwatch with a black resin strap scuffed white at the edges.

Anika saw the watch and did not breathe.

Maya wore it every day, not because it was good but because it survived bleach and bus poles and sink water. She used the timer for cooking and for medication alarms during treatment. In the last months she had stopped wearing rings because her fingers swelled, but the watch stayed on until the hospice nurse removed it and set it on the dresser next to a half cup of cold tea.

Ravi reached for the jewelry pouch first. "These we can put away. Later."

"Can I look?"

"Quickly."

She opened the pouch. The watch was warm from the room, not from skin, and that made grief feel stupid and physical all at once. She turned it over. The back was scratched deep near the battery cover. She remembered the exact scratch: Maya had caught the watch on the metal edge of the bus farebox and laughed because blood came before pain and she bled onto the dispatch forms Ravi had printed for his own shift.

"Put it in keep," Anika said, too quickly.

Ravi's hand hovered. "We can decide later."

"Why later?"

"Because we're not doing one-item arguments. We're finishing."

"It's a watch."

"Exactly. A watch."

She put it back in the pouch because his eyes were on her and she needed him to look away.

They moved to papers: receipts, test reports, warranty cards, temple donation slips, and a stack of old school forms with emergency contact lines that still had Maya's number in neat print. Ravi sorted by future legal risk. He kept tax forms, trashed expired insurance cards, saved anything with signatures, and set old novels aside for the building lobby shelf.

Anika found a spiral notebook half full. The cover showed sunflowers gone dull with kitchen grease fingerprints. First pages: grocery budgets in Maya's hand; columns of prices from two different stores; bus fare totals; one page of recipes measured in handfuls and caution notes for Anika in parentheses: `less chili for her`, `soak rice 20 mins if you remember`. Midway through, the handwriting changed from lists to short entries, not dates, just fragments: `A vomited before math test + still wanted to go`, `R forgot lunch again`, `today pain 6 but manageable`, `owe Leela $40`.

Anika closed the notebook before Ravi could see the line about pain.

"What is that?" he asked.

"Nothing. Old notebook."

"If it's bills, keep. If it's random, donate."

"It's half used."

"Then donate."

He did not ask what was written. She hated him for that and understood him at the same time: he was moving through land mines by refusing to look down.

"Why are you running this like a fire drill?" she said.

"Because pickup's at six, and if we stall now these bags sit here another week."

"So that's it? We box it, tape it, done? Like she was clutter?"

He turned, finally full on, one hand still gripping a stack of insurance envelopes. "Don't put words in my mouth."

"You wake me up at four-thirty and time every set. Now you're timing her sweaters."

"I'm timing the stairs, the driver, and my shift tomorrow."

"For who?"

His face changed on that question; his mouth flattened and his grip bent the insurance envelopes. For one beat she saw the exhausted man from the kitchen sink, not the coach voice. Then he put the envelopes down and went back to command.

"We're done talking," he said. "Take this bag to the door."

Anika planted her feet. "No. That notebook stays with me, and you stop calling it random."

Ravi's jaw worked. "If pickup misses, they charge us again and these six bags sit in the hall till Friday. I can't eat another fee and lose tomorrow's shift. Take the bag."

"Then say that," she said. "Say it's pickup fees and hallway space. Don't act like I'm making drama out of paper."

"I'm saying it," he snapped. "Now move."

"You started it when you called her notebook random."

"I called that pile unsorted. Bills go left. Clothes go right. Trash goes out. That's the only way this gets finished."

The line came out like one of his depot checklists, fast and joyless.

"Says who?" she said.

"Says the door we can't open all the way right now. Six bags are already in the hall. Driver texted forty minutes."

There it was: money and space, the true gods in their kitchen. He took a breath and tried for measured. "One box. You get one memory box, tin size. It stays on this shelf till Sunday when Priya's here."

"Then stop shoving the watch under scarves. Put it face up in the tin."

"Fine," he snapped. "I'll put it face up. Happy?"

She was not. The fight had shifted and she did not know whether she had won anything except a sharper version of the same wall.

Ravi grabbed the small plastic tin marked `KEEP`, dumped the jewelry pouch into it, and dropped Maya's watch in last. The watch hit metal with a hard bright click that made Anika's shoulders jump.

"One box wasn't a debate line," he said. "It's this. We seal it now and reopen Sunday when Priya's here."

He held the tape gun out handle-first. "Seal it."

Anika did not take it. "If that watch leaves this apartment, I don't get on the morning bus."

His jaw flexed once. "It doesn't leave. The tin stays on this shelf. Sunday we decide."

She took the tape gun from his hand and pulled one strip too fast, slicing her thumb on the tin lip before she slammed the tape down across the lid. She added a second strip crosswise like she could pin the whole argument shut.

"Pickup text came," he said after a moment, voice flatter. "Forty minutes. Move."

He handed her the linen bag and turned back to the top shelf.

Anika took the bag into the hallway where the light was always one stop too dim, dropped it by the door, and stood with her palms on the peeling wall until the urge to scream thinned into a sharp buzz behind her teeth.

When she came back in, Ravi was kneeling by the tin again. "Goddamn tape, stay down," he said as the corner lifted back up under his thumb. He peeled it back to reset the lid, the watch resting on the shelf edge while he pulled a fresh strip.

"Can you carry these pillowcases?" he asked without looking up.

"Yeah."

She took the pillowcases with both arms. On the turn toward the hallway she let one slip, crouched to pick it up, and with her left hand took the watch from the shelf in one practiced scoop, palm closing so hard the buckle pin dug skin. She slid it into the inside pocket of her hoodie before she stood.

Ravi kept talking behind her about weight distribution in the donation bags. "Books separate from clothes. If they mix, the bottom tears."

"Okay," she said, heart kicking too hard.

She carried linens to the door, then came back twice for coats and towels, once for kitchen towels stiff with old starch. Each return tightened the skin between her shoulders until it felt stapled. Did he notice the empty edge on the shelf? Did his eyes track her pockets? His gaze snagged on her hoodie and she nearly dropped the bundle, then realized he was staring past her at a torn bag seam.

The half-used sunflower notebook was still on the dresser where she'd left it between insurance forms. Ravi was in the hall lifting a box and saying, "Come on, don't rip now," at the tape seam. Anika slid the notebook under her sweater against her stomach, cold spiral wire pressing skin, and pulled her shirt down.

When she bent to lift a second box, the notebook edge scratched her ribs. The sting felt like proof.

At four-fifty, two donation workers knocked and moved through the apartment fast, practiced and polite in the way of people who had seen a hundred versions of this room and this family. One of them wore mismatched gloves, one gray, one black, and apologized every time a box bumped the wall. The other had a tiny silver cross pinned upside down on her jacket zipper and kept saying "Got it, got it" under her breath as she carried heavier loads than her partner. Ravi over-thanked them. Anika stayed out of the doorway and counted each box leaving: one, two, three, four, five, six, seven.

When the last bag went, the apartment looked larger and poorer at the same time.

Cupboards showed raw empty shelves where spice tins had hidden water stains. Closet rod exposed a run of bare metal between Ravi's shirts and the one cardigan Anika had saved. The floor near the dresser had a pale rectangle where a storage bin used to sit. Even sound changed. Ravi set down a mug and the clink bounced farther.

"Eat," he said at seven, setting rice and reheated dal on the table. He had made too much and salted it late; she could taste the fix. "You've got school tomorrow. We still train in the morning."

"How many sets?"

"Standard Monday. Ghosting plus serves. Maybe light movement after school if homework is done."

"How light?"

"We'll see your legs."

She nodded like this was normal bargaining and forced down bites while the hidden notebook warmed against her skin. The watch in her hoodie pocket dug into her hip whenever she shifted in the chair.

Ravi did dishes without speaking. Water ran. Plates clicked. The radiator knocked twice like someone at the door. He left her lunch container drying upside down and went to the living room with his phone and a legal pad, writing numbers in columns that looked like dispatch schedules, overtime shifts, and rent dates. She did not ask.

In her room, Anika locked the door even though the latch stuck and did not fully catch. She took the watch out first.

The watch had a black strap, scratches at the buckle, and one faint crack across the plastic face that only showed if she tilted it under light. She pressed the side button. The display blinked to life, weak and green: `19:43`. The seconds ticked. The battery was not dead.

She put it on her wrist.

It was too loose. Maya's wrist had been narrower by the end, then wider on steroid weeks, then narrow again. Anika threaded the pin through the second-to-last hole and shook her hand. The watch slid once, settled against bone, and stayed.

She sat on the floor with her back against the bed frame and pulled out the sunflower notebook. First half held Maya's lists and fragments. Last third had blank pages. The spiral wire bit her thumb where kitchen grease had stiffened the cover; turmeric and drawer dust lifted only when she cracked it open.

She opened to a fresh page and wrote the date, then crossed it out because she did not want to start like school. She tried again:

`after donation day`

That looked too dramatic. She scratched that out too. Third try:

`MON`

The third try felt better. It was neutral.

She made columns the way math teachers wanted: narrow for numbers, wide for notes.

`food`
`training`
`pain (0-10)`
`grief spike (0-10)`
`output`

She stared at the headings until they stopped looking stupid. Then she filled the first line from memory.

`banana half + tea (5:05) / ghosting 12 x full-court, serves 80 / pain 6 at shin, 4 chest / grief 2 during drills / output: better split step on last four sets`

Second line:

`school lunch: skipped half sandwich / stairs hurt after 6th period / pain 5 / grief 1 / output n/a`

She paused, pen over paper, then wrote the event she had been avoiding naming.

`kitchen + closet sorting (3:10-5:00) / no training / pain 3 legs, 8 throat / grief 9 when smelled cardigan + saw handwriting / output: none`

She added one more line under output before she could talk herself out of it.

`when pain is in muscles, I can move better. when pain is in chest/throat, timing goes bad unless I lock on the back-wall seam and breathe through my teeth.`

The sentence made her nauseous with accuracy.

She flipped back through Maya's pages. Grocery arithmetic. Short notes about bus delays. A list of medicines where one refill had a star next to it and `ask discount` written in the margin. On one page Maya had tracked her own pain for nine days in small numbers at the top corner, no explanation. `5 6 7 8 7 7 9 8 8.` Anika put her finger on the `9` and held it there until ink smudged.

From the living room, Ravi's voice rose as he argued with someone on the phone about shift coverage. "I said I can take Friday if Patel swaps Wednesday. No, not double nights. I can't do both. I've got a kid. Stop dumping that dead route on me every week."

He sounded like himself at work: clipped, bargaining, polite until he was not. Anika imagined him in the depot office under fluorescent lights, ankle swelling in his left shoe, drinking burnt coffee and pretending fatigue was a scheduling problem instead of grief.

She wrote another line.

`Dad says move fast or drown.`

Then, below it:

`Fast is his float.`

She stared at that one longer than the others. It felt too close to mercy. She crossed out `float` and wrote `weapon`. She crossed out `weapon` and wrote `air tank`.

The revision felt better. It tasted metallic and useful, like biting a lip to stay upright.

A knock came, two quick taps with no waiting. "Lights out in fifteen," Ravi said through the door. "And leave your phone in the kitchen tonight."

"I don't sleep with it anyway."

"Good."

His steps moved away. She waited until the floorboards stopped complaining, then tore the page corner with the crossed-out words and swallowed the strip dry before she could think about how weird that was.

She slid the notebook under the mattress near the wall, then changed her mind and pulled it back out. Too obvious. Ravi changed sheets on Sundays and hated lumps in the mattress. She moved it again, this time into the old geometry kit box under her desk, beneath protractor sets she never used. On top she placed two broken mechanical pencils and a packet of erasers so it looked like junk.

The watch stayed on her wrist.

At ten, the apartment settled into night noises: radiator hiss, upstairs toilet flush, one distant siren crossing Northern Boulevard, Ravi coughing once in the living room before his bedroom door shut. Anika lay on her side facing the wall, watch face against pillow, feeling each second tick through fabric. She tried to slow her breathing to match it and failed.

Memory came in fragments that refused order. She saw Maya at the stove, tapping the spoon twice before tasting. She saw Maya in clinic shoes, unlacing one-handed while she asked about math homework. She saw Maya in the hospital bed saying, with lips dry and cracked, "You don't have to be good all the time, kanna," and then asking for water before Anika could answer.

Anika squeezed her eyes shut until color flashed behind them. She dug her thumb into the watch strap until the buckle pin bit skin and named what she could feel: radiator hiss, rough pillow seam, the dry pull in her throat.

Then she remembered the donation workers carrying bags down the stairs, careful not to scrape walls. She imagined those bags opened in another apartment, her mother's cardigan on someone else's shoulders, spice tins in another kitchen, list pages in the bottom of recycling. A panic wave rose so fast she sat up and pressed both palms to the mattress.

She wanted to run suicides in the hallway until her lungs burned and her mind narrowed to feet, turn, feet, turn. She wanted to wake Ravi and scream that he had let strangers carry away things that still knew Maya's hands.

Instead she got out of bed, took the notebook from the geometry box, and wrote in the dark using the green glow from the watch as a guide.

`22:18`
`urge to run: 8/10`
`rule: if chest > 6, no decisions.`

She set the notebook down, put her back against the wall, and did wall-sits in socks on cold floor, thighs shaking while the watch timer beeped softly each minute. On the next hold she bit the inside of her cheek hard enough to taste blood. By the last hold the shaking blurred into heat and the panic slid one notch lower.

The final beep answered with a hallway floorboard creak. Ravi's steps stopped outside her door.

"Niks?" he said through the wood. "Why are you up?"

"Leg cramp," she said, still against the wall, breath chopped. "I'm stretching."

A pause. "Keep it quiet and get back in bed."

"Yeah."

His steps moved off. She waited until the radiator hiss swallowed the sound, then wrote one more line with her hand shaking.

`after wall-sits: urge dropped to 4. method works if I shut up and finish the set.`

She replaced the notebook in the geometry kit box and went back to bed with quadriceps buzzing. Through the wall she could hear Ravi's television low, some sports panel arguing over players she did not care about. Every few minutes his cough cut through the commentators, dry and restrained, the cough of someone trying not to wake a child.

At 4:25 the watch alarm vibrated against her wrist before it beeped. She killed the sound instantly. For one second she did not know where she was and reached toward the other side of the bed for a body that had not been there in months.

Then the apartment, the wall, the winter dark, and the watch all snapped back into place.

She stood, pulled on training shorts under sweatpants, and opened the notebook to last night's page.

`AM rule: if grief spikes, eyes on back wall and hold pace. no extra speed.`

She underlined `hold pace` twice, tore a narrow strip from the page, and tucked it into her sock above the left ankle where it scratched when she moved.

Before closing the notebook, she added one more line.

`watch stays on. if he asks, I say it's mine.`

In the hallway mirror she tugged her sweatshirt cuff over the watch by reflex, then rolled it back until the black strap showed. Her face looked older in the mirror's warped edge, not because of bone or skin but because she had started carrying a secret weight and the body knew before the mind did.

Ravi was already in the kitchen tying his shoes, one lace frayed white near the knot. He had laid out two bananas, one steel bottle of water, and a packet of salt crackers on the counter in strict parallel lines. He glanced at her wrist and held there a beat too long before he looked at her eyes.

"You took it out of the tin," he said.

Anika reached for the banana. "Yeah."

"After I said Sunday."

"I wasn't waiting till Sunday."

His mouth tightened. He checked the time on his phone, then set it down harder than he needed to. "Bus in nine, so we're not doing a full fight. Listen carefully. New rule: if you keep something of your mom's, you say it to my face. No more pocketing things behind my back."

She peeled the banana too fast and split it down one side. "I'm keeping it."

"Then keep it in the open." He pushed the steel bottle toward her. "If I catch one more secret like that, closet key stays with me till Priya's here. Eat half now, half after warm-up. Move."

"Okay."

She swallowed without tasting and followed him down the stairs. At the landing he slowed for one step because of his ankle, hand briefly touching the rail. He caught her seeing it and straightened.

Outside, wet air slicked her teeth and settled cold on the back of her neck. Trucks were already throwing grit off Roosevelt Avenue. The air carried wet concrete and old exhaust. They walked fast to the stop, not talking. Ravi kept one arm angled behind her when trucks rushed past, an old protective habit he never mentioned.

At the shelter, while he checked transit updates, Anika slid her cuff down and pressed her thumb over the watch face until the seconds disappeared under skin. She felt the edges of the case, the place where Maya's wrist had worn the plastic smooth, and did not cry.

When the bus came, they climbed in and stood near the back door where there was space for lunges if the route stalled. Ravi began reviewing the morning set out loud, short imperative phrases timed to each streetlight: "First twenty minutes footwork. Then serves. No drifting. Don't chase power early."

Anika nodded at each one, the dutiful athlete, the manageable daughter.

Inside her sweatshirt cuff, the watch warmed against her pulse. In the geometry kit box under her desk, the notebook waited with columns and lies and true things mixed together. The chapter of the house they had cleared yesterday did not feel closed. It felt like an incision with stitches that had not taken.

She touched the pen mark still faint on her finger from the night entry and looked out the smeared bus window as Queens slid by in dark storefronts and shutter grates. She counted passing intersections until counting became breath.

At the rec center doors, Ravi held one open and said, "Cuff up. If you're wearing it, don't hide it. After drills, straight home and we finish this."

She rolled her sleeve back so the black strap showed, the loose watch face knocking once against her wrist bone, and went in.


# Chapter 4

By Friday evening, after days of predawn bus rides and cuff-up drills, when Anika double-knotted her shoes, the old HVAC above Court Two had started its metal cough, three hard rattles and one long whine, like somebody dragging a chair across tin. The sound bounced inside the public rec center and settled in her teeth. She checked Maya's watch out of habit, then corrected herself because it was not a habit yet, not really. She had started wearing it only this week, and the strap still sat wrong on her wrist bone unless she pushed it up with her thumb.

6:43 p.m.

Ravi stood by the cinder-block wall with a clipboard he did not need. He had printed a match sheet from work and clipped it to the board even though the ladder organizer had his own forms. He liked paper that could be held. He liked columns. He liked an object that did not answer back. A smear of bus-depot grease stained the side of his left thumb, and he kept rubbing it with his right index finger as if he could erase it before anyone noticed.

"You warm up through the middle," he said. "Don't chase early winners. Make her hit one more ball every rally. She wants you rushing. Don't give it."

Anika nodded once.

"Run it back."

"Middle first. One more ball."

"And your serve routine."

"Four bounces, breath out, eyes up."

He looked at the watch on her wrist, then at her face, and he did not mention Maya. He never mentioned Maya before matches. It was one of his rules that was not written anywhere.

Across the glass, the ladder board listed names in dry-erase marker. ANIKA IYER sat under RUNG 7. Tonight she played for RUNG 6. Above her name was LEAH SETHI, fourteen, Forest Hills, current holder. Anika knew Leah from two junior events where they had not played each other. Leah had the academy posture that looked taught, shoulders down and neck long, even when she bent to tie a shoe.

Leah arrived with a navy duffel and a little brother trailing her, maybe six, still in a school uniform hoodie, carrying a lunchbox covered in cartoon sharks. Before she even looked at the court, she crouched and told him, "Don't trade the clementine this time. You hear me? That's mine for after." The boy saluted with two fingers and sat on the bleachers, swinging his legs hard enough to knock his heels against the metal seat.

Leah's warmup jacket was zipped to her chin despite the heat. A frayed friendship bracelet wrapped twice around her racket throat; she unwound it and rewound it before every stretch, the same exact motion, as if she were tightening a screw. When she finally glanced at Anika through the glass, her mouth tipped sideways in a smile that was almost friendly and not at all friendly.

"You're the kid," Leah said when they met at the door for the coin toss. "Rung seven kid."

"I'm Anika," Anika said.

"Yeah. I know. Everybody knows." Leah bounced on her heels once. "You hit forever. That's what people keep saying."
