The marker, Mr. Salazar, flipped the coin with a thumbnail scarred white across the knuckle. He was retired NYPD and volunteered twice a week, and he always smelled faintly of fennel seeds and mint. "Call it."

"Tails," Anika said.

Leah won the toss anyway and chose to receive.

As they knocked up, Leah fed high, soft balls to the back left and watched Anika run them down. They were not hard enough to score, just hard enough to make her move extra steps before the match started. Anika sent the ball back to length and kept her face blank. She could feel Ravi at the side wall, not visible from this angle but present as pressure, like another line painted on the floor that she had to stay inside.

The first game opened with a twenty-six-shot rally. Leah went early to the front right, then feathered a counter-drop that died crooked because of the warped floorboard near the tin. The ball took an ugly second bounce. Anika still got there and lifted it high. Leah sighed theatrically, like she had been assigned tedious homework, and reset from the T.

That was the pattern. Leah made a show of patience whenever she made Anika run. She rolled her neck. She checked her nails between points. She asked for the ball to be wiped when there was no sweat on it yet. She let Anika serve, then held up one finger to the marker as if to say wait, wait, I'm still not ready. The tiny delays worked because they were legal and because they made everyone notice time.

Leah caught Anika's eye at 4-4 and said, quiet enough that only the two of them could hear, "You look twelve."

"I'm eleven," Anika said before she could stop herself.

"Wow." Leah whistled once. "Then this is your bedtime match."

Anika served hard to the backhand and took the next rally in eighteen shots by refusing the front court until Leah blinked first. She did not look at Leah after the winner. At the service box she tapped the ball into the same worn patch: one, two, three, four. On four she emptied her lungs and lifted her eyes to the front wall before she struck. The routine made a fence around her.

She took the first game 11-8.

Between games Ravi leaned in from the door and passed her the bottle without asking. "Good. Keep length. Don't talk."

"She keeps slowing it," Anika said.

"Then you speed your feet, not your shot choice."

"I know."

His jaw flexed once. "Then do it."

The second game started fast and turned sour in the middle. Leah stepped across Anika's line twice and asked for lets that were never lets. Mr. Salazar gave one. Ravi muttered something sharp under his breath that ended in "ridiculous," and then he went still when Mr. Salazar glanced toward him.

With the game at 7-6, Leah clipped a ball that sprayed chalk dust from the side wall. She looked at Anika while she twirled her racket handle and said, "Coach says if I keep you running, you'll fold by game four. Easy."

The words landed before Anika had language for them. Fold by game four sounded like a weakness listed on a scouting sheet with her name on it.

She stared at Leah's mouth and missed the score call.

"Seven-all," Mr. Salazar repeated.

"You can hear fine, right?" Leah asked. "That beep got you? Knew it. After your mom, you've had way too much hospital noise."

Something under Anika's sternum tightened so hard she thought she might hiccup. She did not answer. She served short by accident, got punished straight down the wall, and did not chase because she knew she would not reach it.

Tied at 9-9, she made a choice that felt petty and lifesaving: stop trying to hurt the ball, hit ugly high length to Leah's backhand, and make her turn one extra time before every attack. The rallies got slower, meaner, and Anika dragged the game back to 10-all.

Deuce dragged into the longest rally of the night so far, thirty-nine shots by Mr. Salazar's count, and Anika felt her forearm begin to burn from the elbow down. Leah won it with a low counter-drop that scraped the tin and rolled dead. Leah did not celebrate. She just lifted one shoulder and walked backward to receive, as if to say this outcome was administrative, already filed.

Leah took the game 12-10.

Ravi met Anika at the door, voice low and clipped. "You gave her your ears. Stop doing that."

"She talked." Anika's throat felt lined with chalk.

"Everybody talks. Your job is to be deaf."

"You can't just be deaf."

"You can if you want to win."

She drank and swallowed twice because the first swallow came back up with acid.

The third game opened with Leah serving body-lob and then pinning Anika in the backhand corner until her calves trembled. The ball on Court One hit glass with a report like a hand slapping tile. Somebody in the bleachers laughed too loud. A little kid dropped a metal bottle and it rolled the length of the aisle, clanging against each stair edge. Sound piled on sound.

Down 3-5, Anika over-hit three balls in four rallies. She knew it while the ball was still leaving her strings. Her timing had gone half a beat early, the same way it went in class when she tried to write notes and listen at the same time.

She decided to trade pace for height, lifting deep lobs to buy half-breaths and reset her feet instead of swinging harder. It worked for two rallies. Then Leah camped on the T, volleyed the shorter lifts, and turned those bought seconds into punishment.

Between rallies she said nothing now. She did not need to. She just held the T with her chin high and forced Anika to run around her. At game ball, Leah asked Mr. Salazar to repeat the score even though she had clearly heard it.

"Ten-six," he said.

"Thanks." Leah smiled at Anika. "Just checking she can count when she's tired."

Leah closed it 11-6 on a serve that nicked the side wall and died in the back, putting Anika down two games to one.

Ravi crouched by the court door for the first time, bringing his face level with hers. Sweat darkened the collar of his jacket. "Listen to me. She's trying to make this social. Don't let her. Pin her deep left, deep left, then step in only if she floats one."

Anika nodded.

"Run the pattern."

"Deep left twice before I go short."

"And if she talks?"

"I call the score and hit."

He tapped the clipboard with two fingers, the same quick tap he used when checking bus arrivals. "Good. Breathe out on bounce three and stay on your toes between rallies."

He said it like there were only two buckets and everything in the world fit in one of them: execution and noise.

By the fourth game, sweat had soaked through Anika's shirt spine. Her fingers were pruning inside the grip. She started towing each breath in by force. The ball looked normal until it did not. Sometimes it came off Leah's racket with a faint blur around it, as if the fluorescent lights were slightly out of phase.

Leah kept up the body games. On every point she won, she walked straight past Anika's shoulder, too close, not touching but close enough for heat. On every point she lost, she tied and retied her ponytail without need. She asked for the ball changed at 5-5. Mr. Salazar refused. She laughed and said, "Worth a try," then stared at Anika while she spun the old ball in her palm.

The electronic scoreboard on the outer wall chirped at 7-7 to confirm another court's result. One short beep came first, then another, then a sustained alarm because somebody had entered the score wrong and the machine did not like mismatches.

The pitch hit Anika behind the eyes.

For half a second the court glass turned into hospital glass. The side door became the ICU sliding door with the rubber seal that sighed open too slowly. The chirp became the monitor in Maya's room after midnight, first regular, then quick, then angry. She saw the green line stair-step and flatten and jump again. She saw a nurse with a ponytail ask, "Can you step outside for a minute?" while Ravi argued about one more minute, one more minute, please. She smelled chlorhexidine and warmed plastic tubing. She remembered the paper gown against her own forearm when she leaned over the bed and Maya's fingers were too dry to hold on.

"Anika." Mr. Salazar's voice cut through. "Play."

The ball was already in motion. Leah had served and come forward. Anika reacted late, clipped the frame, and sent the shot high enough to survive. The rally stretched to eight shots, then twelve, then seventeen.

Her chest tried to lock. She forced a breath into the bottom of it and kept moving. Leah went short to the front left. Anika reached it and drove straight. Leah volleyed crosscourt. Anika's feet slid on sweat and recovered. The ball came back to the middle. She refused the cheap drop, hit length, hit length again, and waited until Leah overreached on a boastful volley. The opening appeared for half a blink. Anika punched the ball low into the backhand nick.

She hit a winner.

The score moved to 8-7.

She did not fist-pump. She did not look at Ravi. She walked to the service box and did the routine with hands that were no longer steady.

On bounce two, her hand twitched and nearly lost the ball. She caught it, reset, and forced four clean bounces before she let her breath go and brought her eyes up to serve.

Leah finally broke the silence at 9-8. "You're still hearing that beep?" she asked, smiling as if they were sharing a joke. "You just blanked out for a sec."

Anika bounced the ball and said, "Score?"

"Nine-eight," Mr. Salazar said.

"Thanks," Anika said, not to Leah.

She served tight to the back left, took the next rally with patient length, then guessed right on Leah's crosscourt and cut it off early for 10-8.

Leah saved one game ball with a perfect drop that died on the second bounce. On the next rally Anika forced a boast and caught the volley into open space for 11-9.

The match was level at two games all.

Ravi's voice came through the open top panel of the door before she reached him. "One game. One. You're fitter than her tonight."

"I'm dizzy," Anika said.

He put the bottle in her hand. "You're dizzy because your heart rate is high. That's not an injury. Breathe low. One game."

She wanted to ask for one minute alone in the corner by the vending machine where no one could look at her. She wanted to press her forehead to cool metal and stop hearing the scoreboard beep replaying under everything else. Instead she drank because he was watching the bottle level and she could feel him counting her swallows.

The fifth game became ugly in the way long matches become ugly. Good technique bent around survival. Leah's shoulders stayed pretty, but her movement slowed by one fraction on deep corners. Anika's legs felt full of wet sand, and her right quad twitched every time she pushed off. The rallies got longer because neither could put the ball away clean.

At 5-5, Anika made herself loosen her grip and play for corners instead of trying to finish early. The choice cost pride and saved oxygen. One deep exchange drew a loose boast she could punish, and she edged ahead before Ravi barked from outside, "Middle!"

Two points later, with it 6-6, Leah hit a hard body shot that clipped Anika's hip and dropped. "Sorry," Leah said automatically, then added, "You okay, kid?"

"I'm fine," Anika said.

She was not fine. The edges of the court had started to pulse with her heartbeat.

Mr. Salazar looked at Ravi and lifted a warning finger. Ravi shut his mouth but not his eyes.

Leah took the next two points with brutal patience, forcing Anika front-right and then lifting over her head again and again until her lungs stung. 9-8 Leah.

On game point at 10-9, Leah wiped her face with her wrist and said, not cruel this time, just factual, "You're tough. I'll give you that."

It was the first honest thing she had offered all match. It made Anika angrier than the taunts.

Anika answered by refusing the first opening and making Leah play one more full diagonal, then another. She erased one game ball, then another, until they were back at 11-11, both breathing through open mouths.

They started 11-11 with a weak serve from Anika that should have been punished. Leah stepped in and chose a drop too fine, clipping tin by a millimeter. They got a let and replayed the point.

Anika bent forward with her hands on her knees, inhaled once, twice, and lifted her head before Mr. Salazar could call time.

She served deep. Leah countered deep. They moved each other corner to corner without risk for twenty shots, then thirty. Panic tried to crawl up her throat; she bit the inside of her cheek, tasted copper, and kept her knees loose.

Stay in this rally.

Stay low.

Stay here.

At shot thirty-six, Leah's length landed short by half a foot. Anika stepped across and drove to deep backhand. Leah scraped it up for shot thirty-seven. Anika volleyed to the opposite corner for shot thirty-eight. Leah got there late and sent up a defensive lob that hung in the center too long.

Anika could have hit a winner to the front. She saw it and refused it. She played safe length once more, deep and heavy, to pin Leah behind her.

Shot thirty-nine came back high.

Leah tried a boast from a bad body position. The ball sat up in the middle. Anika stepped in and killed it straight. The ball cracked low, kissed the sidewall nick, and died, giving Anika 12-11 and match ball.

Leah delayed for the first time without pretending. She walked to the back wall, touched the bracelet on her racket throat, and exhaled through her teeth. When she turned, she looked older than fourteen and also exactly fourteen, all edges and effort.

The scoreboard chirp flashed through her skull again on the first bounce. She palmed the ball, started over, dug her thumbnail into the seam until the rubber squeaked, breathed out between her teeth, kept her eyes up, and served.

She served hard to the body. Leah got a racket on it but popped it loose. The rally went short, only eight shots, and each one carried the weight of fifty. Leah attacked first with a short ball that sat high. Anika lunged, dropped, recovered, and watched Leah scramble forward. Leah reached the ball and lifted it too crosscourt. Anika was already there. She punched a firm straight drive past Leah's shoulder.

Leah stretched, missed by inches, and slapped the floor once with her palm in frustration as the ball bounced dead.

She won it 13-11, three games to two.

Anika had a split second of silence after match ball, the kind that arrives before the room decides how to react. Then the bleachers clapped. Somebody whistled. The little brother with the shark lunchbox yelled, "Come on, Leah," too late and too loudly and then looked embarrassed.

Leah stood, breathed, and offered her hand through the sweat. "Good match," she said. No smile now.

"Good match," Anika said.

Leah added, quieter, "You didn't crack."

Anika did not know what answer belonged there. She squeezed once and let go.

She turned toward the door where Ravi was already stepping in, face lit with a pride so bright it almost looked like relief. "That's how you finish," he said, loud enough for the bleachers. "That's discipline."

The floor tipped under her feet.

She tried to swallow and got only saliva and acid. The smell of rubber and old sweat sharpened into something metallic. Her stomach folded hard, once, and she clamped her jaw shut because people were watching and she had just won and winners did not throw up on glass courts like babies.

The second heave hit harder than the first.

She made it three steps toward the side door before her body took the decision away. She dropped to one knee by the trash can and vomited in a hot, violent stream that splashed the rim, then the concrete, then her own left shoe. The force left strings of spit hanging from her lip. She coughed and retched again until there was nothing left but bitter foam and a burn in her throat that felt flayed.

The gym went quiet in pockets. A few people looked away out of politeness. A few did not.

Ravi crouched beside her immediately, one hand on her back between the shoulder blades, rubbing circles that were almost tender and almost impatient. "Breathe through your nose," he said. "Slow. Slow."

Mr. Salazar appeared with paper towels and set them beside the can. "Kid might need the medic station."

"She's okay," Ravi said before Anika could speak. "She overreached and still closed. That's all."

Mr. Salazar did not move. "She told you she was dizzy before game five. That's not nothing."

He thumbed his radio and spoke toward the desk without taking his eyes off Anika. "Front desk, Court Two. Minor athlete, post-match emesis with reported dizziness. Bring first-aid kit and incident log."

Anika spat into the can and wiped her mouth with the back of her wrist. Her hand shook so hard she had to use both hands to twist the water bottle open.

Leah hovered two steps away, towel around her neck, uncertain whether to stay. Her little brother had stopped swinging his legs. He watched Anika with the alert, scared stare children get when an adult body does something sudden and ugly.

"You want my electrolyte tabs?" Leah asked. "I've got lemon ones."

Before Anika answered, Ravi said, "We have what we need. Thank you."

Leah did not step back. "You should still take one. Those help."

Anika managed, "I'm okay," though the words came out scraped.

Her body called her a liar. A dry heave folded her over the can until her ribs spasmed and her eyes watered. Nothing came up except bitter spit and threads of bile. She could hear Leah's little brother breathing through his mouth, fast and scared.

She tried to wipe her chin and missed. The world kept tilting by a few degrees, correcting, then tilting again. The chirp from the scoreboard lived behind her left eye, a tiny alarm that would not dismiss.

The man in the park-district jacket came off the bleachers and stopped clapping. "What happened in there besides the vomiting?"

Leah swallowed. "I talked during points. She talked too. It got ugly."

"About what?" he asked.

Leah stared at the floorboards. "About whatever got a reaction."

Mr. Salazar's jaw shifted. "It included comments about family bereavement and hospital noise. It'll be documented and reviewed."

Ravi took the paper towels and wiped the side of Anika's shoe as if cleaning mud after practice. "Look at me," he said.

She lifted her head.

"You won a five-game attritional match against an older player who tried to get in your head. You kept your length when your legs were cooked. You did exactly what we trained."

Her stomach cramped again. She pressed her fist below her ribs until it passed.

"Do you hear me?" he asked.

"Yeah."

"Tell me what held."

She swallowed. "I kept it deep and finished."

"And?"

"I still got back to the T."

"Exactly." He stood and addressed nobody and everybody at once, voice carrying toward the bleachers and the organizer's table. "This is what separates kids who have talent from kids who have spine. She played through dizziness and still executed."

The front-desk manager, Ms. Okafor, arrived with a red first-aid tote and a yellow carbon incident pad. She set a folding chair beside Anika and pointed to it. "Sit, please."

Ravi turned toward her. "We're doing a short cool-down and heading home."

"No, you're not," Ms. Okafor said, calm as a locked door. "She's a minor with dizziness plus emesis. Policy is twenty minutes monitored rest, fluids, orientation check, and guardian signature before release. If you leave early, I document unsafe departure and notify the ladder committee."

Ravi's mouth thinned. "She won the match."

Mr. Salazar answered before Ms. Okafor did. "Winning doesn't clear symptoms."

Anika sat on the folding chair with sour taste coating her tongue while Ms. Okafor clipped a pulse ox to her finger, counted respirations, and asked her name, date, and what court she was on. Her insides felt hollowed out, scraped clean with a spoon. She wanted someone to ask if she was scared when the beep hit. She wanted someone to say the words hospital monitor out loud, to prove the flash had been real and not weakness. Nobody asked that exact thing, but nobody pretended nothing had happened either.

Ms. Okafor handed her a paper cup. "Small sips. If that comes back up, tell me right away."

They did not do Ravi's ten-minute cool-down walk. They sat under the EXIT sign while two new players took Court Two and thudded balls against the front wall where she had just knelt. Mr. Salazar timed the interval on his phone and wrote two entries in the incident log: post-match vomiting with dizziness, and opponent conduct review pending for comments about family bereavement and hospital noise.

At minute twenty, Ms. Okafor had Anika stand, walk six steps, turn, and walk back. "You're steady enough to go," she said, then looked at Ravi until he met her eyes. "No extra training tonight. If she vomits again, gets a headache, or seems confused, you take her to urgent care."

Ravi signed the yellow carbon form hard enough to tear the paper and folded the copy into his clipboard.

By the time they reached the bus stop, the usual bus had already come and gone.

Ravi stared at the route board, then said, quieter, "I know you felt bad out there."

She waited.

"But this is competitive sport," he continued. "No one gives points for feeling bad. They only remember the result."

There it was, the rule beneath all the other rules. He said it in his bus-depot voice, matter-of-fact, same tone he used for route changes and delay reports. It was not cruel. It was not kind. It was simply true in the way he believed truth worked: hard surfaces, no cushions.

She glanced down at Maya's watch. A smear of vomit had dried near the buckle. She rubbed it off with her thumb until the metal shone again.

"I remember," she said.

Ravi nodded, satisfied, and checked his clipboard though there was nothing left to check except the folded warning.

When the next bus finally hissed to the curb, the night had gone sharp with late-winter cold. Steam rose from a street grate and drifted around their ankles. Ravi texted someone from the ladder committee the final score: 11-8, 10-12, 6-11, 11-9, 13-11. He typed quickly, then showed Anika the screen before hitting send, as if numbers could pin the match in place and keep it from changing shape.

She watched the message go. Her body still felt raw and emptied, and she pressed her thumbnail into the groove of Maya's watchstrap until the edge bit skin and the beep receded.

On the ride home she sat by the window and kept worrying the strap groove with her thumbnail so she would not drift back to the ICU beep. At seventy-fourth Street a woman boarded carrying grocery bags that smelled like cilantro and wet cardboard. At eighty-second, a man in a restaurant apron fell asleep standing up, forehead against the pole. It was a normal night on a normal bus with normal bodies.

Ravi stared straight ahead and said, without looking at her, "You move to rung six tomorrow morning."

"Okay."

"We start at five-thirty. Recovery jog, then ghosting. Short session."

Anika rested her head against the cold glass. "Okay."

The bus lurched, and acid rose in her throat again. She swallowed it back and pressed her temple harder into the cold glass.

When they climbed the apartment stairs, Ravi's old left ankle stiffened on the second landing, same as always. He caught the rail and hid the wince a beat too late. At their door he fumbled the key once, then got it on the second try.

Inside, the apartment smelled faintly of curry leaf and detergent. The kitchen light had been left on over the sink. Ravi set his clipboard on the table, set her racket beside it, and said, "Shower. Then half a banana, salt, and sleep."

She went to the bathroom, peeled off her shirt, and saw where the body shot had already purpled her hip. She touched the bruise and flinched.

In the mirror her face looked older and smaller at the same time. There was dried spit in the corner of her mouth she had missed. She cleaned it with warm water and stood there another minute listening to the pipes click inside the wall.

Winning had not made the monitor sound disappear.

Winning had turned it into training data.

She brushed her teeth, spit until the taste was almost gone, and came back to the kitchen in clean pajamas. Ravi had cut half a banana into exact coins and sprinkled salt like medicine. He slid the plate across without comment.

She ate. The salt stung her cracked lip. He watched the clock over her shoulder and wrote tomorrow's schedule on a torn envelope in block letters.

5:30 RUN + MOBILITY
6:00 GHOSTING (20)
6:30 BREAKFAST

He tore a strip of tape with his teeth and fixed the envelope by the fridge light switch, where she would see it at dawn.

Anika looked at the list and then at her own left hand, which still shook a little when she held it in the light. She tucked the hand under the table so he would not see.

"Good night," Ravi said.

"Night."

In bed she lay on her side and listened to the building settle: radiator hiss, distant TV laughter through the wall, pipes knocking twice. Every so often one of those sounds landed on the same frequency as the scoreboard chirp and her stomach clenched in answer.

She put two fingers on Maya's watch where it rested on the crate beside her mattress, not wearing it now, only tracing the cool plastic edge until it warmed under her skin.

Before dawn, rain tapped the fire escape in thin starts and stops.

Sometime after the radiator hiss thinned to a whisper, she finally slept.


# Chapter 5

Rain had started before dawn and did not commit to it. It stayed in the air like spit, too fine to dodge, enough to make the bus windows sweat. Anika stood over the heater grate with her bag against her shins, watching traffic slide and stop and slide again on Northern Boulevard, every red light eating into the warm-up block Ravi had written on last night's torn envelope and taped by the fridge. Maya's old digital watch sat loose on her wrist and clicked each minute with a tiny plastic jump. 5:22. 5:23. 5:24.

When they got off at the rec center, the sidewalk shone like dark fish skin. Ravi already had his keycard out, already moving fast enough that she had to half-jog behind him under the awning.

"You're two minutes under where we need to be," he said without looking back. "We trim dynamic stretch, keep racket prep. Qualifier volume starts now."

The lobby smelled of bleach and wet coats. Beyond the desk, the courts were lit in hard white strips, each glass wall ghosting a second copy of whoever moved inside it. The HVAC coughed awake and settled into its metallic rattle, louder than the rain. Anika dropped her bag beside Court 3 and started rope.

First fifty jumps, her legs felt wooden and obedient. On jump sixty-two, the right shin gave a small bright warning, like somebody snapping a pencil inside the bone. She clipped the rope, reset, went again. The second warning came earlier, at thirty-eight. She kept her face flat and looked at the service line like it had offended her.

Ravi stood outside the glass with his phone timer and a pen tucked over his ear, already drafting the morning in rows: ghosting, lunge matrix, feeds, retrievals, interval sprints. He tapped the glass once and held up four fingers. Four rounds.

She switched to ghosting. Front-left, front-right, back-left, back-right. Split step, push, recover. On the first set she could keep the pain in a narrow lane, a hot stripe along the inner edge of her right shin that vanished if she landed exactly on the ball of the foot. On the second set it thickened, climbed toward her knee, and bit every time she decelerated. The court floor felt half a centimeter farther away than usual. She kept missing it.

"Lower center," Ravi called through the door gap. "You're standing up out of the lunge."

"I know," she said.

"Don't know it. Fix it."

She dropped lower. In the front-right corner, the pain sharpened into a clean point that made her vision blink once, white at the edges. She came up too fast and pretended she had meant to reset her grip.

Ravi stepped in, took the racket from her, and placed her right foot with two fingers at the toe. "You can't cheat the distance. If you shorten here, you get punished later. Again."

His hand was warm through her shoe. His own left ankle made a faint hitch when he stepped back, the old stiffness he never named unless it was to call it laziness in his younger self. She had learned his body the way you learned court lines: where it would hold, where it would not.

She lunged again, driving into the front-right corner three times in a row.

By rep eight, each push-off knocked her breath off count; inhale came late, exhale leaked out as a hiss. By rep twelve the throb had teeth. Even standing still, she felt a low buzz in the bone, as if the drill kept striking after she stopped moving.

Ravi checked the timer. "Thirty seconds. Get the reps."

Anika dragged a breath through her teeth and gave him three quick lunges on the left side first, pretending she had misheard the call, buying herself two breaths before she had to load the right leg again. She landed soft and silent. She took the next right lunge shallow and pulled out early.

"Full depth," Ravi said.

She made the next one long enough to touch the floor with her knuckles. Pain shot up so fast she had to clamp her teeth to keep from making noise. She stood and walked it off in a small circle as if she was tracking an imaginary ball trajectory.

Ravi frowned. "What's that limp?"

"My sock twisted," she said. "It's rubbing."

He looked at her shoe. "Then fix it and stop wasting reps."

She crouched, tugged nothing, and rose before he could kneel beside her and touch the leg himself. Her watch read 5:46. She had twenty-four minutes until shower and bus transfer. If she lost those, she would lose first period, and Ms. Donnelly would email home again.

The rain had soaked into the court building, into everyone's hair and shoes, and now damp climbed the inside of her calf sleeve. Damp days always made the shin angrier. She knew that before she knew the word tibia.

Ravi fed balls from mid-court. Straight drives, quick recoveries, no chatter. Every third feed he forced her forward, low, then flicked behind her to make her pivot on the right leg. She could do it if she landed exactly under her center and did not let the heel kiss down. She could do it if she let the front-left corner be ugly. She could do it if she did not think about the point of pain, because thinking about it made it expand.

At 6:03, she misjudged a low ball and banged her racket frame against the tin. The crack echoed, thin and humiliating.

Ravi opened the door. "Focus."

"I'm focused."

His eyes narrowed at the snap in her voice. "Then show it. Qualifier draw closes in three weeks. Nobody gives extra slots because your foot feels weird."

She bit back the first thing in her throat and said, "Got it."

By the time they finished feeds and moved to court suicides, the right shin burned with each turn. Start line to short line and back, then to half-court and back, then full and back. She ran with the right foot slightly turned out, ugly but less sharp. On the third ladder Ravi stepped to the side door to take a call from the bus depot. She heard his voice flatten into work tone: route shortage, swap sheet, night shift maybe.

She straightened her foot and gave him six clean lengths before he returned.

When he came back, she was breathing through her mouth and trying to look like only breathing through her mouth.

"Good," he said. "That is the pace. Remember it."

The praise landed in her chest the way cold water landed in a hot pan, violent and brief. She lifted her racket a few inches in answer, because even that felt safer than shifting weight onto the right leg.

They made the 7 train by forty seconds. She stood the whole ride, not because there were no seats, but because bending the knee to sit made the shin pulse harder when she stood again.

At school, the hallway floor had fresh wax and smelled like lemons trying to be cleaner than they were. Anika kept to the wall so nobody would bump her from the right side. Her backpack felt heavier than its actual math book and lunch box weight, because every stair made her recalibrate where to place the foot.

Second period science, Ms. Donnelly asked her to pass out lab sheets. Halfway down the aisle, her right leg misfired and the room tilted left. She set the stack on Ben Cho's desk too fast and held the back of a chair for one breath too long before her balance came back.

"Anika?" Ms. Donnelly said.

"I'm good." She resumed moving, handing out papers with her left hand because her right fingers had gone a little numb.

By lunch she had started counting steps between classes in groups of sixteen, a way to keep panic from sloshing over the sides. Stairwell to cafeteria: sixty-four. At the landing by the trophy case, two seventh graders gave her the careful look people used on cracked glass; she smiled so they would keep walking. Cafeteria to English: one hundred twelve. By the top of the second-floor stairs, her mouth tasted like pennies and she smiled at Mr. Patel before he could ask. English to nurse after he spotted her slowing on the landing: forty-eight, with a rest on step twenty-nine she called a shoe retie.

Nurse Eileen Park's office was too warm and smelled faintly of peppermint gum and vinyl exam-table paper. A small fan turned in the corner even though the heater was running. On the windowsill, three succulents leaned toward a square of pale sun, and one had a strip of medical tape around the pot where it had cracked. A Yankees mug held tongue depressors. On the wall behind her desk, a photo of a teenage boy in a marching-band uniform was pinned beside a handwritten grocery list: oat milk, cumin, batteries.

"Sit," Nurse Park said, already rolling away from her computer. She wore bright green clogs with little tomatoes printed on them, the kind of shoes people bought because they stood all day. "Mr. Patel says you've got a limp."

"It's not really a limp."

"That answer usually means yes." She offered a half smile and pulled on gloves. "Where?"

Anika tapped the outside of her shin first, then corrected to the inside when Nurse Park waited.

"How long?"

"Since this morning."

Nurse Park lifted an eyebrow. "Try again without trying to get back to class fast."

Anika fixed on the floor tile between her shoes, the one with a chipped corner shaped like a tooth. "A few days. Maybe after practice last week."

"Give me numbers I can use. Right now, sitting still."

"Three."

Saying it out loud made the pain feel less negotiable.

"Walking flat stays at three unless somebody clips my right side. Stairs push it to four."

"On court?"

Anika picked at a loose thread on her sleeve and pictured Ravi's face if she said the high number first. "Front-right lunges hit five. If I shorten depth and land quiet, it's more like four."

Nurse Park's gaze sharpened. "You're already editing movement to bargain with pain. That's not random soreness."

Nurse Park made a noise in her throat that was not disbelief and not agreement, just filing. She palpated along the tibia with two careful thumbs. At the second press point, hot static burst across Anika's ankle and stole her breath; she grabbed the table edge before her knee could dip.

"Sorry," Nurse Park said quietly. "I know."

She turned her laptop around and clicked through a chart. "Localized bony tenderness plus increased pain with impact in a kid who trains hard. I want imaging. Today if your family can manage it, tomorrow at the latest."

"It's just sore." Anika heard herself and added too quickly, "We had extra ladder drills because qualifiers are coming."

"I heard you." Nurse Park's voice stayed even, almost gentle, which made refusing her harder. "I'm not saying your season is over. I'm saying we need to rule out a stress reaction before it becomes a fracture. Those are different problems if you catch them early."

She set a printed referral form on the desk and wrote in block letters: NO RUNNING/JUMPING UNTIL CLEARED. PE EXCUSED.

Anika watched the marker move and felt heat rise up her neck. "Can you not write that part?"

"I can, and I did." Nurse Park capped the marker with a firm click. "You get one body. I don't care if you're trying out for the moon."

Anika stared at the paper. No running. No jumping. The words looked like a prank somebody meaner than Zoe would pull.

"I need to call your guardian," Nurse Park said. "Policy for a possible significant sports injury."

"My dad's at work."

"Then I'll leave a message and send this through the parent portal."

"He hates portal messages."

Nurse Park gave her a look that said many parents hated many things, and none of that changed bones. She picked up the phone.

Ravi did not answer. Nurse Park left a voicemail in her clipped professional cadence, then sent the school note and tucked two copies into a yellow envelope with ANIKA IYER in blue ink.

Before she handed it over, she tapped the corner of the envelope once. "I know you're worried about missing training. My son plays trumpet. If he misses one rehearsal, he thinks his life is over. It's not. Bodies heal on their own clock, not ours."

Anika forced out a clipped "Okay" because compliance was the price of being released, then slid off the table and tested one step. The leg ached even before weight settled.

"No gym, no after-school conditioning," Nurse Park said. "And if your pain spikes tonight, urgent care. Don't wait."

"Okay."

The lie came out smooth from overuse.

She left the office with the envelope in her binder and spent the rest of the day calculating how fast she could get home, ice for fifteen minutes, and be changed before Ravi checked the planner app. By final bell the sky had turned the color of old dishwater. The sidewalks steamed where the morning rain met late sun.

On the bus back to Jackson Heights, she opened the envelope under her backpack and read the note twice. The second time she folded it smaller until it fit inside the pocket of Maya's notebook, between a page of interval times and a grocery list in Maya's rounded handwriting from months ago. cilantro, ginger, dish soap. Anika touched the word cilantro with her thumb, then shut the notebook before the bus hit her stop.

Ravi was in the kitchen when she got in, tie loosened, depot lanyard still around his neck. Mustard seeds popped in hot oil in the pan. Curry leaf smoke stung the back of her throat. He was chopping onions too fast, the knife blade making hard staccato taps on the cutting board.

"You're late by eleven," he said. "Transfer delay?"

"Train held at Queensboro."

"Eat a banana now. We leave in forty. Jonah gave us Court 2 and 3 overlap. Good slot."

She set her bag down carefully, keeping her right leg straight. "I had to go to the nurse today."

The knife stopped mid-air. "Why?"

"Shin pain."

Ravi turned off the burner and faced her. For one breath she saw the fear before he arranged it into irritation. "How bad?"

"Not bad-bad. Just sore."

"Which side?"

"Right."

He crouched, hand already reaching, and she had to force herself not to flinch before he touched. He pressed along the bone, quick, practical, less gentle than Nurse Park, then looked up.

"Tender," he said.

"Yeah."

"What did she say?"

Anika pulled one folded school note from her pocket and held it out. Ravi read it once, jaw tightening at the all-caps restriction line.

"Imaging," he said flatly. "Temporary rest."

"She said maybe stress reaction."

"School nurses write maximum caution because they have to." He refolded the paper smaller than she had, then slid it under the half-packed folder by his lanyard instead of leaving it out. "We run this through sports med, not through a portal warning."

"She said no running or jumping until cleared."

"Cleared by imaging," Ravi said. "We're not pretending this note is the final verdict."

He moved to the fridge and pulled down the calendar. Blue ink for school deadlines, black for training blocks, red stars for tournament dates. He crossed out that night's suicides, wrote YELLOW PLAN over the next three days, then tapped the red star in three weeks.

"If volume drops this week, your match legs disappear. Then you go into qualifiers half-ready. Then you lose early and everybody says you peaked at borough. You want that?"

The answer sat ready in her throat: no, because losing felt like drowning in public. No, because she had just learned pain could be translated into praise if she survived it. No, because he looked less broken when there was a plan pinned to a magnet.

"No," she said.

"Exactly. We're not stupid. We're strategic." He softened the tone a fraction. "Yellow plan until scan: no road work, no bonus courts, no front-corner repeats if your gait slips. You drift, we stop."

"What do I tell Jonah?" she asked.

"You tell him nurse flagged soreness and we're waiting on the appointment. You don't hand him that note and invite a shutdown before we even get scanned."

Anika tracked the folder by the microwave, imagined Nurse Park's face in this kitchen, and said, "Okay."

"Say it straight."

"Okay. I'll run the yellow plan."

Ravi exhaled hard through his nose, relief disguised as annoyance. "Good. Change. Bring both sleeves."

At the rec center that evening, Court 2 smelled of damp rubber and old dust. Jonah Feld was already there with a bucket of balls and his timing clipboard, the chronic strip of white athletic tape wrapped around his right wrist. He wore the same faded navy cap he always wore, brim salt-marked from sweat. A half-finished black coffee sat on the bench beside a paperback mystery with a transit map sticking out as a bookmark.

"You look cooked," Jonah said when Anika stepped inside.

"Long day," she said.

"Everyone has a long day." He bounced a ball once and watched how she moved to pick it up. His eyes narrowed. "What's the hitch on your right side?"

Ravi answered from behind the glass before she could. "School nurse flagged soreness and wrote a restriction note. We're managing load until imaging."

Jonah kept his gaze on Anika. "That what happened?"

Anika tightened her grip on the racket. "It's fine."

"Fine is not a metric." Jonah walked to the front-right corner. "Give me six lunges, controlled."

She did three clean and three compromised. On lunge five, her right leg went slack for a split second; her back foot skidded and her knee caved inward.

Jonah set down the ball. "Stop."

Ravi opened the door. "She can continue. It settles after warm-up."

"No," Jonah said, still calm. "Not with that load pattern."

"We have qualifiers in three weeks."

"I can count weeks." Jonah's tone stayed dry, almost bored, which made Ravi angrier. "I can also count how many times she's off-center on that leg. She's protecting the tibia."

Ravi stepped onto the court, palms up, argument posture. "Then we build around it. Back-court drives, no front-corner lunges, no jump work."

"That is still impact."

"Everything is impact."

"Exactly." Jonah pointed toward the door with his clipboard. "Then you reduce it before it chooses reduction for you."

Anika stood between them, sweat cooling under her shirt, pulse thudding in her shin. If she spoke, one of them would use her words like a weapon. She kept her mouth shut.

Ravi cut his hand toward her. "Tell him it's manageable."

She said, "It grabs on push-off and turns sharp in front-right. I can still finish the block."

Jonah snorted once. "That's not manageable. That's you negotiating with a bone."

"I can finish today's block," she said quickly. "I just need a minute."

Ravi latched onto it. "There. She can finish."

Jonah picked up his coffee, took a sip, and set it down with care. "I'm not running a kid through a possible bone stress injury because a calendar looks scary. We do a technical session only for twenty-five minutes. We do no suicides, no extra courts, and no heroics." His focus snapped back to Anika. "If pain climbs, you stop. That's not a suggestion."

Ravi's laugh was one dry syllable. "Heroics? She's training."

"Call it what you want," Jonah said. "I'm done arguing labels."

For twenty-five minutes he fed soft-length balls and had her work pattern recognition from mid-court without deep corners. Even then, pain threaded every right-foot plant, a metal wire pulled tighter each rally. Jonah saw it. Ravi saw it and pretended he did not, tapping splits on his phone, muttering target times under his breath like prayer.

When the timer beeped, Jonah held up his palm. "Done."

Ravi reached for another ball from the bucket. Jonah stepped in front of the feed line and shook his head once.

"Session's closed," Jonah said.

Ravi's jaw worked. "You're overcorrecting because of one school note."

"Maybe," Jonah said. "I'd rather overcorrect now than explain a fracture later."

He began collecting balls, each one thudding into the bucket with a hollow pop. Then he walked to the wall tablet by the court door and typed with one finger. On the evening board, Court 3 vanished from Ravi's reservation line.

"I logged technical-only and removed your second-court block until imaging," Jonah said without turning around. "Front desk gets the same note."

"You're putting this in the system?" Ravi said.

"Yes. If you want it reversed, send a written override with your name on it."

Ravi stood very still for three seconds, then turned to Anika. "Shower. We'll do mobility at home."

She turned and went to the locker room, where the mirror above the sink had a crack running through its corner like lightning. She peeled off her right calf sleeve. The skin over her shin was faintly swollen, tight and shiny where her finger pressed and left a pale dent.

She ran cold water over a paper towel and wrapped it around the leg until the ache dulled from knife-edge to hammer beat. In the next row, two older girls from the basketball program argued about prom dresses and laughed so hard one of them snorted. Their life sounded illegal in the best way, a place where people said stupid things and nobody measured them in rep counts.

When she came out, Ravi had already packed most of the gear.

"We're still behind this week," he said quietly while they walked to the bus stop. "Don't let Jonah's caution become your excuse."

"It's not."

"Good."

The bus was late and crowded, damp coats pressed shoulder to shoulder. Anika stood with one hand on the overhead bar and counted stoplights to keep from noticing each jolt. Ravi scrolled through tournament forums on his phone, jaw set. At one point he murmured, almost to himself, "If we lose this window, we can't buy another one."

She pretended she had not heard.

At home he made her sit on the couch with an ice pack duct-taped around her shin while he answered depot emails and revised the week schedule. "No pavement runs," he said. "Court movement only. We front-load weekends."

"Okay."

"Walk to the sink and back."

He was watching her face, not her leg, checking whether the week he had built was still standing.

She crossed ten kitchen tiles and came back before the shin could wobble.

"Better," he said. "See? Already settling."

It was not settling. The ice had numbed the surface while the deeper ache stayed bright and mean. But one clean lap was useful evidence. A clean lap got people off your case.

Before brushing his teeth, Ravi looped a bike cable through both of her main racket bags and locked them to the kitchen radiator. "No court tonight," he said. "Give me your word."

"You have it."

He gave one curt exhale like a deal had been signed and left the key on his ring in his pocket.

By ten-thirty Ravi was asleep at the kitchen table with his glasses tilted and one hand still on the calculator app. Anika watched him for a full minute from the doorway, the lines around his mouth deeper than they had been last year, his hair grayer at the temples than the photos on the fridge. Grief had hollowed him and then filled the hole with schedules.

She should have gone to bed.

Instead she dug out an old aluminum backup racket from under her bed, tied her school sneakers again, slid the front door open without letting it click, and took the late bus back to the rec center with her hoodie pulled over the racket throat so she would look like any kid hauling homework. The custodian at the desk knew her by now, knew whose daughter she was. He glanced at the screen, frowned at Jonah's technical-only flag, and waved her through when she said, "Mobility work, no match play." The court availability board showed Court 4 empty for one short block before adult league.

Inside Court 4, the air was colder than Court 2, the overhead light slightly dimmer on one side. The metal rattle from the HVAC made the glass hum. She set her watch timer for twelve minutes and chose suicides because numbers never argued back.

On the first ladder, she ran short line, half-court, full, and back, and the pain started as a thin burn and climbed each turn.

Sweat chilled between her shoulder blades, and she pictured Ravi's key still hooked to his pocket seam.

On the second ladder, the shin stopped feeling like a warning and started feeling like glass dust under skin. By half-court it had become white static, and on the hard pivot she had to stutter-step to keep the leg from buckling.

She told herself it was fear, not tissue. She told herself pain lied. She told herself Nurse Park had to scare kids by job description and Jonah had the luxury of caution because he did not sit at their kitchen table when bills came.

On the third ladder, the right shin flashed so hard on the full-court turn that her foot did not push; it collapsed. She caught herself on one hand, scraped her palm against painted floor, and tasted bile in her mouth.

She stayed crouched for three breaths, listening to the ball thump from the next court where grown men shouted split and let and stroke like legal terms in a language she was not old enough to speak.

She shoved herself upright and tried to restart. On the first turn, a dry click fired deep in the shin, her toes went prickly and numb, and the leg folded again before she reached the short line.

She killed the timer at 7:41 and stood against the wall, breathing through her teeth while the numbness ebbed into a heavier throb.

By the time the adult league players knocked to claim the court, her right leg trembled when she stopped. She bent to pick up her bag and had to use the wall for balance. The man at the door, maybe forty, racket tucked under his arm, looked at her swollen shin and said, not unkindly, "You okay, kid?"

"Yeah," she said. "Cramps."

He gave a noncommittal grunt, like he didn't believe her and didn't plan to contest jurisdiction.

At the desk, the custodian handed her an incident clipboard because minors who got hurt on court had to be logged. She wrote her name, wrote slipped during conditioning, and checked DECLINED EMS before he could call Ravi.

The ride home felt longer than the map said it should be. At every transfer she had to choose between taking stairs fast and arriving on time or taking them sideways and arriving late enough for questions. She chose time.

In the apartment, the kitchen light was still on. Ravi had moved from table to couch, one arm over his face. The ice pack sat melted in the sink.

Anika showered with one foot out of the spray because hot water made the shin throb harder. In her room she opened Maya's notebook to the current week page. She had drawn columns after chapter three and kept them strict: date, session, load, response, notes.

Monday AM session included ghosting, lunge matrix, feeds, and suicides, and the response line read: right shin lit hot by rep twelve; right foot turning out to survive pivots.

She remembered biting the inside of her cheek after rep twelve so Ravi would not hear a sound.

Monday PM session stayed technical, and she wrote: wire-tight ache on every right-foot plant.

She could still feel Jonah's eyes on her footwork, waiting for honesty she had not given him.

Late PM session included unauthorized suicides, and she wrote: near buckle, numb toes, visible swelling.

She stared at the entries until they blurred. Then she uncapped her pen and dragged a line through each one, slow and careful so the page would not tear.

Monday AM response became: settled once warm.

That line looked like a forged signature, close enough to pass.

Monday PM response became: manageable.

Late PM included mobility only.

In the notes column she replaced near buckle with managed with form correction.

She also rewrote the incident line in her head before sleep could catch it: no collapse, no numb toes, no form that could surface later.

The edits looked almost neat, as if there had never been harsher lines under them. She took the second nurse note from the notebook pocket, unfolded it, and read NO RUNNING/JUMPING UNTIL CLEARED one more time. Then she folded it into a rectangle small as a tea tag and tucked it inside the back cover where the binding had come loose.

From the living room came Ravi's sleep cough, dry and stubborn. Anika closed the notebook, pressed her palm flat over it, and listened until the apartment settled into pipes and distant traffic and the soft electrical buzz of the refrigerator. On the counter, a half-packed folder sat under his depot lanyard with pay stubs, tournament papers, and the first folded restriction note clipped together for the morning.

Her shin kept pulsing anyway, steady as a second heartbeat.


# Chapter 6

The subway lurched into Lexington and threw Anika half a step into her father's shoulder. He caught her with the side of his hand, eyes still on the stack of papers balanced on his forearm. The top sheet had a gray header and a bar code and the kind of tiny print adults pretended they understood the first time through.

The folder that had sat half-packed under his depot lanyard the night before was now arranged in strict layers: pay stubs clipped together, rent ledger, utility statements, Anika's school verification letter, tournament invitation, and a section he called "medical support docs" in a voice that tried to make it sound like any other category. On the train, every stop shook one corner loose. He kept pressing the pages flat with his palm, like he was calming a fever.

Anika stood with one hand on the sticky pole and watched his thumbnail drag a dark line through photocopier toner. She could smell damp denim and the sour metal breath from the tunnel. Her right shin gave her the thin hot warning it always gave now when she stood too long and kept still, but she did not shift weight. If she shifted, he would notice.

At 59th Street they climbed out and crossed into air that bit through her sweatshirt. Midtown was bright in the way that felt expensive: clean glass, brushed steel, door handles polished so hard the city looked fake in them. The private club sat half a block from a bakery that sold nine-dollar croissants the size of her face. Ravi glanced at the chalkboard menu, then away.

Inside the club lobby, everything was quiet enough that her sneaker squeak sounded like she had done it on purpose. A woman in a navy blazer gave them visitor tags and pointed toward administration. The carpet swallowed their footsteps. Anika kept hearing Queens rec-center echoes in her head anyway, that rubber smack and HVAC rattle that never stopped. Here, even the air-conditioning sounded educated.

The administrative office was behind a frosted-glass door stenciled with JUNIOR DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM. Ravi opened the folder before they sat down, checking contents again even though he had already checked on the train, on the sidewalk, and once in the elevator. His left ankle stiffened when he pivoted, a small hitch she had known since kindergarten, from his old playing injury he never admitted hurt.

A woman came out from the back carrying two paper cups and a stapler in her teeth. She was maybe in her thirties, with a mustard cardigan that had one cuff darkened by fountain-pen ink. Her black hair was twisted into a clip shaped like a tiny shark. She set the cups down, removed the stapler, and gave them a quick smile that looked practiced but not cold.

"Ravi and Anika Iyer? I'm Nora Patel. Sorry about the wait. Scanner died on me again." She tapped her monitor with two fingers. A sticky note on the side read CALL MAA DENTIST in block letters, and each time the screen lagged she rubbed the knuckle of her ring finger like it hurt.

Ravi stood too fast. "No problem. We have everything. I think."

"Good. Let's get you through intake." She led them to a cubicle with low walls and one plastic fern. "This appointment is required for all hardship requests. Since she's a minor, you sign, she co-signs athlete acknowledgment, and we need household income, debt obligations, and supporting statements." She said it in one breath, then looked at Anika. "You can ask questions whenever. Kids should know what we're filing in their name."

Ravi set the folder down between them before Anika could answer.

Nora clicked through tabs and slid a packet across the desk. HARDHIP FEE WAIVER ADDENDUM A. The first page had boxes for tournament level, travel burden, and guardian employment status. The second page was all numbers. Monthly net income. Housing cost. Utilities. Transportation. Existing debt over $500. Medical debt. Delinquency status. Collections risk.

Anika stared at the columns and had the same feeling she got when Jonah set up ghosting drills with too many cones and called it simple. There were too many lines to fail at.

Nora asked for IDs first, then pay stubs. Ravi had them ready, paper-clipped to a yellow sticky note marked FEB/MAR. She typed while he spoke.

The keys in Nora's keyboard came in bursts, a dry plastic rattle that reminded Anika of balls skidding off side-wall nick and dying.

"Bus depot dispatch, full-time," he said. "Net around thirty-nine hundred. Overtime jumps around. Some app-driving nights."

Anika kept her eyes on the form so she would not look at his face. When he said app driving nights, she pictured his phone glowing at 1:10 a.m. through the bathroom door.

"How much does the app driving add, roughly?"

"Six hundred on a bad month, maybe a thousand if I burn my weekends."

Nora entered it. "Any direct support from a co-guardian?"

Ravi's jaw moved once before he answered. "No. My wife passed last year."

Anika watched the muscle in his cheek jump and knew he had practiced saying that sentence like a line from a script he hated.

"I'm sorry." Nora's voice lowered and became less procedural for exactly two seconds. Then she clicked forward. "I still need every debt over five hundred."

The condolence hit and vanished so fast Anika almost wondered if she had imagined it.

"Standard household stuff," Ravi said. "Utilities, nothing unusual."

"Mark utilities and move on," Ravi said. "We're not here to put on a poverty show."

Nora did not look up. "I'm not asking for a show. Thin paperwork gets denied, and then your daughter pays full fees."

He slid over a white envelope and two folded notices. Nora flattened them. "City Housing Utility Arrears, eighteen-sixty. Status: past due." She entered it without comment and reached for the next stack. "Medical debt statements, please."

Anika felt her right shin pulse twice, sharp and hot, the way it did at the start of a hard drill. Her body never waited for permission to react.

Ravi hesitated, then pulled the gray packet from the bottom of the folder. The corner caught on the table and a page slipped free. It spun once and landed face-up by Anika's wrist.

QUEENS ONCOLOGY BILLING SERVICES. ACCOUNT HOLDER: RAVI IYER. PATIENT: MAYA IYER. CURRENT BALANCE: $38,400.00. PAST DUE PORTION: $6,400.00. DAYS DELINQUENT: 93. NEXT ACTION: COLLECTIONS REVIEW.

For one second she read only MAYA IYER and saw her mother's hand on a hospital rail, skin waxy under fluorescent light. Then the numbers snapped into focus and stayed there.

Under the total there were line items in smaller print, each one a month from the end of Maya's life turned into a price tag. Inpatient oncology admission: $14,200. Infusion services: $9,640. Emergency fever management: $8,950. Pharmacy adjuncts: $2,110. She recognized the words and not the numbers, then recognized the numbers and felt her throat close.

Nora did not pretend she had not seen Anika reading. She spoke directly to her, careful and matter-of-fact. "These are gross balances after insurance adjustments, not one-time due today. We document them to justify fee relief."

Ravi reached for the loose page. "She doesn't need line-by-line detail."

"She just saw it, and she's co-signing this application," Nora said. Not sharp. Just true.

"She's eleven."

"Then she gets numbers, not rumors," Nora said.

The scanner coughed and flashed red. Nora stood, smacked the side panel with the heel of her hand, and sat back down. "Sorry. This machine needs prayer and violence." She gave Anika a quick apology smile, then returned to the form. "Current payment plan is eight hundred a month, right?"

"Eight hundred," Ravi said. "We're on a plan."

Nora glanced at the statement. "Late three cycles, ninety-three days delinquent, no external transfer yet. Is that accurate?"

"Yeah."

"I need more than 'yeah' for the file," Nora said. "If collections hits before Friday, transport support freezes. Tell me exactly where this stands."

"No transfer yet," Ravi said. "They call, I pay before cutoff."

Nora marked a box and circled one line on the addendum. "For hardship approval, I need a brief explanation of delinquency and expected correction timeline. Two to four sentences is fine."

Ravi took the pen and wrote fast, pressing so hard the nib scratched. Anika leaned enough to read sideways.

Household income reduced after spouse death. Overtime used to maintain payment plan but irregular due to child tournament travel and school schedule. Request temporary waiver and payment-plan stabilization period.

He did not write that he had sold Maya's gold chain. He did not write that she had heard him take app-driving calls at 1:10 a.m. in the bathroom with the fan on so she would not hear him taking strangers from Midtown bars to LaGuardia.

Nora collected the pages and pulled another form from her drawer. "Athlete acknowledgment. Anika, this says you understand fee-waiver status can change and you'll report financial updates. Just sign here and initial this line."

The pen was heavy and warm from Ravi's hand. She wrote A N I K A, each letter separate, then looked at the sentence above her signature again.

I certify that this application reflects current household financial hardship, including debts and delinquent obligations.

She pressed her initials into the margin hard enough to dent the next page.

Nora stamped everything RECEIVED with a square red stamp and stacked the documents into a fresh folder. "You'll get a decision in five business days. If approved, tournament registration fees are reduced and equipment subsidy stays active for this quarter." She glanced at Ravi. "You may also qualify for emergency transport support for Manhattan trips, but I need one more thing for that."

"What?"

"Proof that oncology account has not entered external collections yet. A current portal screenshot is enough." She handed him a checklist card. "Send it before Friday and I can attach it to this file."

Ravi took the card like it weighed more than paper. "We'll send it."

Nora looked at Anika again, this time less like an administrator and more like a person who had seen too many families learn numbers in public. "You did your part today," she said quietly. "That's not nothing."

Anika nodded because if she opened her mouth, she was not sure which voice would come out.

They walked back through the polished lobby. In the revolving door, Ravi's folder slipped and one envelope flashed red where the stamp hit it in block capitals: FINAL NOTICE. He shoved it down before the door finished turning.

Outside, traffic shoved sound against the glass towers and made a blunt roar. Ravi steered her toward the subway entrance and did not ask if she was hungry. She followed half a step behind, watching his shoulder blades through his jacket. His posture had changed from appointment posture to transit posture: head down, objective clear, no detours.

At the corner, she said, "How much is left?"

"We handled the form." He did not slow.

"I saw the number."

"Those statements always look worse than they seem."

"It's thirty-eight thousand."

He hit the walk button with his thumb hard enough to make it click twice. "It's not a cash-now number."

"Then what is it?"

Ravi exhaled through his nose, eyes still on traffic. "It's a payment plan. It's adult paperwork. You don't need to carry it."

She waited until they were on the platform because the train noise gave her cover. "How do we pay it?"

"Same way we have been. Work. Planning." He kept his voice low and fast. "Don't spin out."

"What if it goes to collections?"

He turned then, finally looking straight at her. His face had gone flat in the way it did before a match when she asked for reassurance and got instructions.

"It won't if I keep up," he said. "And I will."

The train screamed into the station. He stepped in first and moved to the door area where there were fewer seats and fewer conversations. She stood beside him, knuckles white on the pole.

Across from them, a kid in a prep-school blazer slept on his mother's shoulder, mouth open, earbuds leaking tinny drums. When the train slammed into a switch, his teeth clipped his lip. He jerked awake with a small shocked cry, and his mother pressed a napkin to the blood, whispering into his hair until he settled.

Anika stopped counting stops and watched her own reflection jump in the window glass.

Then the numbers pushed back in anyway. One fee waiver might shave two hundred off a tournament. Transit support might save another eighty. Thirty-eight thousand four hundred still sat there like a wall, so she broke it into things her body understood: sets, weekends, drills she could turn into money if she kept winning.

At forty dollars a set, she would need nine hundred and sixty sets. At two hundred a weekend, almost four years.

Ravi's phone lit in his hand. UNKNOWN 866. He killed the call before the second ring and shoved the phone deeper into his jacket pocket. Anika swallowed the question she was about to ask and pressed the light button on Maya's watch instead. 1:18 p.m. Numbers glowed green, indifferent.

At Queensboro Plaza, Ravi finally spoke again. "You have school reading tonight and mobility work. Don't let this become a thing in your head."

It already was a thing in her head. It had shape and color and interest rates.

By the time they got home, the apartment smelled like burnt mustard seed from the pan Ravi forgot to lower before the train. He scraped the blackened bits into the sink and started over without comment. The kitchen window was open a crack and spring rain pushed mist onto the sill.

Anika put her bag down by the table where Maya used to sort grocery receipts into neat stacks: produce, transit, medicine, things that could wait. The spot was empty now except for today's forms and Ravi's keys.

He made tea with too much ginger and set one mug in front of her. "Drink. Then homework."

She sat but did not touch it. "What does collections review mean?"

Ravi kept his back to her, stirring dal with short angry circles. "It means they take another look."

"Is that when they call all day?"

"Sometimes. Today was five calls before lunch, and Friday's eight-hundred payment is the cutoff before referral fees."

"Who called?"

He turned the stove down and faced her. "Anika."

His voice was warning, not answer. "If I answer, you don't turn this into a training target. Promise me."

"I promise. Who called?"

He braced both palms on the counter. "Yes. Meridian Recovery. They leave the same script every time: 'This is an attempt to collect a debt, and any information obtained will be used for that purpose.'"

The words made her stomach drop harder than the dollar amount had.

"How many calls?"

"Five today. Two yesterday." He rubbed his forehead with two fingers. "If I miss Friday, they kick it to external collections."

"How do you not miss it?"

"I move other pieces." His laugh came out thin and mean. "I pushed ConEd to next week and took the Saturday overnight dispatch shift. That's how."

"Eight hundred every month is a lot." The words came out flatter than she meant. "And you still have rent and everything."

"Yes," he said. "I know how numbers work."

She flinched at the edge in his tone. He saw it, hit the counter with the heel of his hand, and said, "Fuck. I'm doing it again."
The word cracked across the tile. Her shoulders jumped up toward her ears.

"Look," he said, softer but still tight. "I'm not mad at you. I'm mad at this whole circus. I should've redacted that page before today."

"I can help," she said.

"By being eleven?" It came out too fast, then he closed his eyes like he wanted to pull the sentence back. "No. By doing your school and training and sleeping. That's your lane."

"Winning helps, right?"

Ravi set the spoon down. "Winning helps with program stuff. Maybe. But it isn't a debt strategy."

"It can be."

"No." He met her eyes, voice back in command register. "Don't build fairy tales out of pressure. You play because you play. I pay bills because I pay bills."

She heard the lie in the split between those two sentences. He had built his own fairy tale months ago and called it discipline.

He plated rice and dal and pushed the plate toward her. "Eat while it's hot."

She kept her hands in her lap. Steam curled off the rice and faded. When she finally lifted the fork, she took two bites, then set it down.

Ravi watched her leave most of the plate untouched, jaw ticking, and scraped his own half-finished dinner into the sink, eyes on the drain.

His phone buzzed across the counter. Unknown 866 number. The preview line read FINAL NOTICE: ACCOUNT SUBJECT TO COLLECTION TRANSFER. He turned the screen face down and let it ring out.

Anika stared at the darkened phone until the vibration stopped. The apartment went so quiet she could hear rain hitting the fire escape.

After dishes, Ravi took a work call in the hallway, voice low. She caught fragments through the thin wall.

"I can take Saturday shift... yes, overnight... no, leave my Monday dispatch as is... I need the differential."

When he came back in, he was already in old sweatpants, rubbing his left ankle like it had locked up. "Mobility band work," he said. "Ten minutes. I'll time it."

She did the routine on the living-room rug while he watched the clock, then checked off a box on the whiteboard near the fridge. He had drawn the week's plan in black marker: school, training, recovery, commute blocks. Every box closed, every arrow straight.

At nine-thirty he told her lights out and went back to the kitchen table with his laptop and the folder. She brushed her teeth, changed, and shut her bedroom door until only a strip of hallway light cut under it.

She waited.

Ravi's chair scraped, paused, then scraped again. Paper shuffled. The laptop notification pinged. She pictured him on the billing portal, typing passwords, clicking payment-plan options, deciding what not to pay this week so he could pay what scared him most.

Anika sat at her desk and pulled Maya's half-used notebook from under her math binder. The cover still had a faded sticker from a lab-supply conference and a coffee ring that had gone the color of old tea. She opened to the section she kept hidden behind taped-in worksheets.

The existing page had today's facts in her tight block letters.

In the morning I went to school.

In the afternoon we had the waiver appointment.

In the evening I did mobility work.

My shin pain score was 4/10 while standing and 2/10 after band work.

From the kitchen came Ravi's debt-collector voice, low and careful. "No, don't move it to external before Friday. I said Friday." A cabinet door slammed hard enough to rattle the glasses. She closed the notebook on her fingers until the cardboard edge bit skin.

When she opened it again, she turned to a fresh page and wrote the date. Then she drew a line down the center and titled the columns PUBLIC PLAN and PRIVATE PLAN.

Under PUBLIC PLAN she copied tomorrow's schedule from the whiteboard exactly, so if Ravi saw the page he would see compliance.

Under PRIVATE PLAN she wrote what she had already decided on the train.
Tuesday and Thursday: add forty minutes of footwork after assigned drills. Do not mention it.

She wrote:
Wednesday: run stairs in the building after homework, twelve flights six times.

A hot wire of pain jumped down her right shin. She pressed her thumb into the tender ridge until the pulse eased, then struck out the stairs line and replaced it.
Wednesday: hallway ghosting and split-step holds after homework, door locked, towel at threshold to kill ball noise.

Saturday: thirty minutes of solo ghosting before Jonah arrives.
Daily: cut breakfast sugar, skip school snacks, save stipend money in an envelope.

She paused and listened. Ravi coughed in the kitchen. Chair scrape. Silence.

She added one more line.

Target: win enough this year to keep the oncology account out of collections.

The sentence sat in the center of the page like a dare. She did not cross it out.

Her watch gave a single beep at 10:00 p.m. She covered it with her palm so the sound would not carry.

From the hallway, Ravi knocked lightly and opened the door before she answered. "Lights out," he said. He saw the notebook and assumed homework. "You done with reading?"

"Almost." She slid the notebook half an inch under her textbook.

He nodded, eyes on her face like he was checking for fever. "Big day. Sleep matters."

"Okay."

He started to close the door, then stopped. "About earlier. I snapped. I'm sorry."

She looked at him and said the line that would end the conversation fastest. "It's fine."

"It's not your job to fix adult messes," he said.

She kept her expression neutral, the one she used when teachers asked if everything was all right at home. "I know."

He stood there another second, wanting something he could not phrase, then shut the door.

Anika waited until his footsteps moved away and the kitchen faucet slammed on, then off. She pulled the notebook back out.

Instead of adding another full row, she tore a narrow strip from the edge of scrap paper and wrote tomorrow's extra work in abbreviations only she used: HG 40, SSH 12x6, G30. She folded it three times and tucked it behind Maya's watch face where the cracked strap met the casing, a hiding place Ravi would never check because he never touched that watch.

Then she slid under the blanket, shin throbbing with a soft electrical pulse, and listened to the apartment settle around her: pipes knocking, rain tapping the sill, Ravi shifting in the kitchen before everything went still.

She reached under her pillow for Maya's watch, clicked into alarm mode, and set a second wake-up thirty-eight minutes earlier than the whiteboard schedule for tomorrow.

Before she buckled the watch back on, she pressed the folded strip flatter under the cracked strap until the edge carved a raw crescent into the skin at her wrist.

When she finally slept, her calves kept twitching like they were still climbing stairs, and her hand stayed wrapped around the watch face while her wrist throbbed in the same hard rhythm as her shin.


# Chapter 7

By the time Anika got off the Q66 and crossed Northern Boulevard, the 4:40 wake-up and full school day had already cinched her chest tight from running the evening through her head.

The folded strip from last night was still tucked behind Maya's cracked watch strap, the edge rubbing her wrist each time the bus jerked.

If she stayed at Zoe's apartment until nine, she would miss the footwork block. If she cut out early, she could still tap the ball in the hallway between the bedrooms, if Dad was out on app-driving and did not hear it.

Then she remembered she had promised Zoe she would stay for cake, and cake never happened early.

The plastic gift bag handle sawed a red line into her fingers. Inside was a sketchbook with thick paper, the best one she could afford at the pharmacy after school, plus a six-pack of neon pens that clicked when she shook the bag. The cashier had offered a glitter card for another three dollars. Anika had said no and watched the woman put it back on the spinning wire rack without looking at her.

Zoe's building smelled like laundry steam and old frying oil. On the second-floor landing, the number two had fallen off one apartment door and somebody had drawn it back with black marker, a crooked swan neck of ink. Music thudded through the wall before Anika knocked: bass from a pop song, a burst of shrieking laughter, then the flat digital gunfire of a video game.

Zoe opened the door on the second knock, already grinning, chipped black nail polish flashing as she grabbed Anika's wrist and hauled her in.

"You made it. Holy shit, I thought your dad was gonna invent a training emergency."

"I said I'd come."

"You also said that last month." Zoe shut the door with her heel and softened her tone so quickly it almost counted as kindness. "Come on. Shoes by the rack before my mom yells."

The apartment was hot with bodies and oven heat. Eight kids from school were packed into a living room that should have held five. Someone had draped silver streamers over the curtain rod. Two younger cousins chased each other under the coffee table, screaming in high falsetto every time their knees hit the rug. A boy from math class, Malik, sat cross-legged by the TV with three bottle caps lined up on the floor in front of him like tiny medals; he collected them from every soda he ever drank and rubbed the edges with his thumb when he was thinking.

On the dining table, four pizza boxes sweated grease into the cardboard. Anika's eyes snagged there and stayed.

There were two plain slices, three pepperoni slices, and one pepper-and-onion slice that nobody had touched. The ranch cup was half full, and the garlic-knot tray was empty except for one torn-off end crust dusted with parmesan.

Calories, she thought before she could stop herself. Then she saw the watch on her own wrist and looked away, as if the numbers might have shown up on the face uninvited.

From the kitchen, Zoe's mother called, "If anybody puts soda on the sketchbooks again, I'm turning this into a silent reading party."

Ms. Martinez stepped into the doorway with a tray of paper plates balanced on one forearm and reading glasses pushed up in her hair like a second headband. She always dressed like she was about to leave for an errand and had forgotten to leave: jeans, old green cardigan, one hoop earring missing. Tonight there was a streak of blue frosting on her wrist. She looked at Anika and smiled with the kind of relief adults got when a child had actually come where she said she would.

"Anika, hi, baby. You want lemon-lime or cola?"

"Water is fine."

"This house is not a hospital waiting room. You can have sugar at a birthday party." Ms. Martinez lowered her voice as she set plates down. "But take your time. You look tired, mija."

Anika nodded and did not answer. Her breath came too shallow, the same pre-rally hitch she got before a hard point. The living room noise pressed on her skin from every direction: song changes, controller clicks, somebody yelling that a character was cheating, the cousins shrieking again when one crashed into the couch. It was ordinary kid noise, untidy and loud and pointless.
