The cracked strap pinched her pulse point.

Zoe pulled the gift bag free and peered inside. "No way. The good paper? Dude." She hugged the bag to her chest. Her eyes snapped up, suddenly sharp. "You remembered I hate pages that bleed through."

"Yeah."

"Thanks." Zoe squeezed Anika's shoulder once, quick and awkward, and shouted at the room. "Nobody touch this. If I catch your greasy fingers on this, I'll end you."

Malik did not look away from the game. "You said that at lunch and then traded me your fries."

"Different legal system," Zoe shot back.

Anika smiled without meaning to. It felt strange on her face, like trying on somebody else's glasses.

They ate on paper plates in laps and on the floor. Anika took one plain slice and folded it in half to keep grease from running down her wrist. She chewed each bite until it went almost sweet and counted swallows to keep her hands still.

Her molars ached from the control.

Dad's voice threaded through those numbers anyway: every hour matters, you lose shape quickly, and nobody outworks us.

A few minutes later Zoe dropped onto the rug beside her and held up a second slice. "You're doing the thing again."

"What thing?"

"Spreadsheet face." Zoe angled her own eyebrows down and pinched her mouth into a parody of concentration. "The one where you're calculating whether mozzarella is morally wrong."

"I'm just eating."

"You're auditing your pizza." Zoe bit into her slice and talked around the bite, shameless. "Relax your forehead before it gets stuck like that forever."

Anika's forehead did hurt. She had not noticed until Zoe said it.

She stretched her jaw and tried to look at the TV instead of the ache behind her eyes. On-screen, cartoon racers banged into rainbow-colored walls. Malik muttered predictions before each turn under his breath, too low for anyone except the person next to him: "He's gonna miss the boost. Yep. Banana peel, told you."

When the music playlist shifted to an older song, Zoe's cousin Camila jumped onto the couch and started conducting with a plastic lightsaber as if she were leading an orchestra. Each downbeat nearly took someone's eye out. Kids screamed, ducking and laughing. Ms. Martinez yelled from the kitchen that nobody was allowed to bleed on the rug because she had just paid the carpet cleaner.

The noise should have been unbearable.

Instead it loosened something.

Not all at once. Not enough to make breathing easy. But enough that the numbers in Anika's head stopped lining up in neat columns for almost a minute.

After pizza, Zoe clapped loudly until everyone looked at her.

"Game time. And yes, this one is humiliating." She held up a sleeve of store-brand chocolate sandwich cookies. "Forehead cookie challenge. No hands. Get it from your forehead to your mouth. Winner gets first cake slice and bragging rights forever."

"That's disgusting," Malik said, already standing up.

"Exactly. That's why it's perfect."

Anika started to shake her head, but Zoe saw it and cut her off.

"Nope. You're in. Birthday law."

"I don't want to."

"That's not a legal phrase in this room. Sit down. Tilt your head."

The circle closed around them on the rug. Camila counted down with both hands in the air, bracelets clacking. On three, everyone tipped their heads back and balanced cookies above their eyebrows.

Anika tried to solve it like drill work by using small movements and keeping her center.

The cookie slid down to the bridge of her nose and stuck there. She wrinkled hard, trying to walk it lower. It jumped sideways and lodged against her temple.

"You're making a face like a lizard doing algebra," Zoe wheezed.

Anika snorted. The cookie dropped straight down into the collar of her T-shirt.

For half a second there was stunned silence. Then the room detonated. Camila fell over laughing. Malik slapped the floor so hard one of his bottle caps skittered under the sofa. Even Ms. Martinez leaned on the kitchen doorway, laughing with her hand over her mouth.

Anika sat frozen for a beat and reached into her shirt, pulling out the crumbed, warm cookie in two pieces.

"Disqualified," she said.

"Tragic," Zoe said, wiping tears from her eyes. "Legendary, but tragic."

Anika heard a sound and did not recognize it as her own laugh until it happened again, louder this time, bent in the middle. Her stomach hurt from it, not from planks or hunger but from laughing too hard.

The game ended in chaos and cheating accusations and frosting on the carpet anyway. Zoe got first slice after declaring herself commissioner and judge. Nobody objected because everyone wanted cake before Ms. Martinez changed her mind and made them sing first.

When they did sing, it was off-key and too loud and full of fake dramatic vibrato, and Zoe bowed to an imaginary balcony like a washed-up singer in a sparkly jacket. Anika joined in on the second line.

The cake was chocolate with blue frosting roses. Anika ate the whole piece without doing the math until she was already licking icing from her thumb.

Then guilt came in a hard wave. She pictured heavy legs tomorrow, Dad's legal pad, and the neighbor pounding the wall if she started ball taps too late. Her pulse skipped; her throat went dry.

She set the paper plate down too hard and the plastic fork snapped.

"Hey." Zoe touched her elbow. "Walk with me? Balcony break. My cousins are entering a feral phase."

Anika nodded, grateful for an excuse to leave the room before somebody asked why her eyes had gone flat.

The fire escape outside Zoe's bedroom window was rusted orange and cold through thin leggings. The street below smelled like wet concrete and bus exhaust. Somebody on the corner argued in Spanish, the words quick and bright, while a delivery scooter idled with its hazard lights blinking. Inside, the party noise came through the glass as a muffled pulse.

Zoe sat with one knee tucked up and scraped blue frosting off her thumbnail with the edge of her other nail.

"So," she said. "You gonna pretend that was fun, or should I write down that you suffered bravely through my birthday?"

"It was fun."

"Cool. Because you looked like you were timing your chewing." Zoe waited a second and added, "I know that face too."

Anika stared at alley light striping the wet bricks.

"I've got practice in the morning," she said. "If I mess up, I feel it all week."

"You have practice every morning."

"Exactly."

Zoe exhaled hard through her nose. "See, this is the part where you turn into a robot and I want to throw a shoe at you."

"Don't. Your mom would kill us."

"My mom would kill me for ruining her own shoe. She likes those little death heels." Zoe leaned back against the brick and looked at Anika directly. "I'm not joking now, though. You disappear for weeks. Then you text me like, hey, want to hang out tonight? Like I'm takeout you can order when your schedule opens."

Anika flinched.

"It's not like that."

"It's exactly like that." Zoe's voice stayed low, not cruel, which made it worse. "I miss you, and then when you show up you're halfway gone anyway, counting something in your head. Minutes, bites, whatever."

Anika worried the cracked plastic strap with her thumb until the edge pressed a groove into her skin.

"I'm trying," she said.

"Trying what?"

"To keep everything from falling apart."

"Everything what? School? Squash? Your dad's mood?"

"All of it."

Zoe held her gaze and did not let her look away.

"You never say her name anymore," Zoe said quietly.

The words knocked the air out of her.

Anika's first instinct was to answer the wrong question. She heard herself begin: "My serve percentage dropped three points last week and—"

"Nope." Zoe shook her head once. "Don't stats me right now. I'm right here."

Anika shut her mouth.

Below them, a bus wheezed to a stop and knelt for passengers. The hiss of the doors opening sounded, for one terrible second, like the oxygen line in the hospice room when the nurse turned the valve.

She blinked hard.

"Every night," she said, barely above a whisper. "I still listen for my mom's key in the door."

The sentence came out plain, almost calm. Her body did not match it. Her shoulders had climbed to her ears. Her jaw shook.

"I hear somebody in the hallway and I think maybe she forgot she died." Anika swallowed and wiped her nose with the back of her hand before she could stop herself. "I know how stupid that sounds."

"It doesn't sound stupid." Zoe's voice went softer than Anika had heard it in months. "It sounds awful."

"I keep waiting for the jingle. She had that ugly plastic tag from the hospital, remember? It clicked against the keys. I can hear it even when it isn't there." Anika pressed her palm against her chest as if she could flatten the panic. "Then nobody comes in. Or it's Dad, and I can tell by his step on the third stair because his ankle still catches, and then I feel relieved and mad at the same time, and then I hate myself because relieved at what? That he came home? That she didn't?"

Zoe let the silence sit. She did not fill it with comfort lines from a movie. She just put her shoulder against Anika's shoulder, steady pressure through sweatshirt fabric.

"I wasn't mad because you train," Zoe said after a while. "I was mad because you cut me out and acted like that was normal."

"I didn't mean to cut you out."

"Doesn't matter what you meant. This is what it felt like." Zoe nudged her with her elbow, not gentle this time. "You ghost me for weeks and blow up my phone at two in the morning when you're scared. I'm not doing the reset-button thing anymore."

Anika nodded before she could talk herself out of it.

"Okay."

"Say it like you heard me."

"I heard you. I can't do that anymore."

"Good." Zoe scraped at the rust with the toe of her sneaker. "Also, for the record, if you text me at two in the morning because you're listening for keys again, I'll answer. I'll be pissed, but I'll answer."

A laugh escaped Anika, wet and broken.

"That's not a good rule."

"It's the honest one, that's all." Zoe tilted her head. "Can you text me when you're starting to spiral, not after you've sat there listening for keys for an hour? Maybe once?"

Anika stared at her hands.

"I found paperwork last week that made everything feel like a timer," she said. "Now I think if I train harder I can stop something bad from happening, like if I get good enough fast enough maybe the house stays standing and Dad stops looking like he's about to crack, and maybe—"

She stopped because the words sounded ridiculous as soon as they were outside her mouth.

"You're eleven," Zoe said, sharp with disbelief. "That's not your job."

"I know."

"Do you?"

Anika did not answer.

Inside the apartment, Camila pounded on the window and shouted that they were opening presents. Zoe yelled back that they were coming and flipped her cousin off with theatrical affection. Camila cackled and vanished.

Zoe stood and offered a hand down to Anika.

"Come on. We can be tragic for exactly one more minute. Then you're helping me open twenty-seven slime kits and pretending to love all of them."

Anika took her hand and stood.

"Twenty-seven?"

"People think I like slime because I said that one time in fourth grade. This is my burden." Zoe squeezed Anika's fingers once before letting go. "You staying till the end?"

The watch on Anika's wrist read 8:41.

Her shoulders tightened anyway, already bracing for Ravi's face and the scratch of his pen.

In front of her, Zoe waited for an answer that was not a schedule.

"Yeah," Anika said. "I'm staying."

"Good."

Back in the living room, wrapping paper flew. Ms. Martinez passed around cut fruit because she did not trust any of them to survive on frosting alone. Malik narrated each gift before it was opened and was correct enough times that everyone accused him of cheating. Zoe opened the sketchbook last and held it up like a trophy.

"This one wins," she announced.

"Biased," Malik said.

"Absolutely. It's my party."

When parents started texting that pickup rides were outside, the room thinned quickly. Kids found mismatched shoes and yelled goodbye from the hallway. Camila fell asleep upright against a pillow, blue icing dried on her cheek like war paint.

Anika helped stack paper plates without being asked. Ms. Martinez rinsed bowls at the sink and spoke over the running water.

"Thank you for coming," she said, keeping her eyes on the dishes so it would not feel like a speech. "She missed you."

Anika folded a pizza box flat. "I missed her too."

Ms. Martinez nodded once. "Good. Keep missing each other out loud, not silently. Silent is where people get lost." She bumped the faucet off with her wrist. "You need me to call your dad and say this was my fault for ending late? I can sound very persuasive."

Anika almost said yes. Then she pictured Ravi's face if another adult managed his schedule for him.

"No. I'll handle it."

"All right. Take cake for home."

"He won't eat it."

"I didn't ask about him." Ms. Martinez slid a foil-wrapped wedge into Anika's hand. "For you. Midnight if necessary."

On the walk to the bus stop, the foil packet warmed her palm. The street had gone glossy from a thin rain while they were inside. Storefront gates were half down. A man sprayed bleach water across the sidewalk outside his deli and pushed the gray foam toward the curb with a broom.

Zoe walked her to the corner, arms folded against the wind.

"Text me when you get in," Zoe said.

"I'll."

"Not a thumbs-up. Actual words."

"Bossy."

"Correct."

The bus took twelve minutes to come and another sixteen to crawl through traffic. Anika stood the whole ride because every seat smelled like wet coats and old fries. The foil packet in her pocket knocked against her thigh at each stop.

She watched her reflection in the dark bus window, overlaying the city lights: small face, hair frizzed from steam and rain, frosting still at the cuff of her sleeve. She looked like a kid who had been at a birthday party. She could not remember when she had last looked like that.

At her stop she got off, crossed under the tracks, and climbed the apartment stairs two at a time until the third flight, where her shin pinched and forced her to slow. By the time she unlocked the door, the hallway motion light had already clicked off once.

Ravi sat at the kitchen table under the yellow overhead light, still in his depot polo, collar dark with sweat at the neck. His left ankle was stretched out straight in front of him, shoe off, sock heel rubbed thin. A legal pad lay open beside his mug. Columns of times and checkboxes marched down the page.

He looked from the legal pad to her face, measuring the gap.

"You're late."

"Bus was slow."

"You were supposed to be back by nine-thirty."

"Cake was late." She set her keys down and slipped out of damp sneakers. "I texted at nine-twenty-two."

"I saw it." Ravi tapped the legal pad with his pen. "What did you eat?"

The question came out automatic, same tone as what time is warm-up, but the legal pad made it feel like an audit.

"Pizza and cake."

His mouth tightened. Blue frosting was still under her thumbnail, bright as evidence.

"How much?"

Heat climbed up the back of her neck. His pen scratched across the legal pad before she answered.

"Dad."

"I'm asking because tomorrow starts at five and if your stomach is heavy we adjust intervals."

One ordinary night, she thought, and still the room turned into a test station.

"One slice. One piece of cake. And fruit."

He nodded as if logging data. "Fluids?"

"Water and one soda." She put the foil packet on the table without comment.

Ravi glanced at the foil packet and searched her face.

"Did you do any mobility before coming up?"

Her right shin pulsed once, sharp, like it wanted a vote.

"No."

"Then do ten minutes now. Calves, hip openers, band work if your shin is tight."

The old script rose in her throat, ready-made, and she heard it in full: she was sorry, it was her fault, and she would make it up.

She swallowed it.

"I'm going to shower and sleep," she said. "I'll do warm-up in the morning."

Ravi's chair scraped as he sat forward. "Anika, you can't treat this casually. One loose night turns into a loose week."

"It wasn't loose." Her voice stayed level, surprising her. "I went to my friend's birthday. I stayed for presents. That's all."

"That's not all. It changes recovery, schedule, focus."

"Maybe. But I still went." She met his eyes and held them. "I'm not taking it back."

Ravi reached across the table and held out his hand. "Give me your phone."

Cold slid under her skin faster than the rain on her sleeves.

"What?"

"If you want to improvise your nights, you lose phone privileges. No calls except school logistics. No hanging out after school this week. Home, training, sleep. Hand it over."

"No." The answer came out before fear could sand it down. "I'm not giving you my phone because I ate cake at a birthday, and you're not reading my texts."

His jaw flexed. "This isn't about cake. This is about control. You don't get to drift right before qualifiers."

"I'm not drifting. I'm living in my own life for three hours."

Ravi grabbed the legal pad and wrote in hard block letters, the pen digging grooves through the page.

4:40 WAKE-UP.
PHONE CURFEW 9:00 PM.
NO SOCIAL PLANS UNTIL QUALIFIERS.

He tore the sheet free and slapped it flat beside her keys. "Those are the consequences. Starting tomorrow."

Her chest tightened so fast it hurt. She wanted to sweep the paper onto the wet floor and grind it under her heel until the ink smeared.

She stopped at the hallway mouth and turned back.

"You can ground me if you want," she said. "But when I'm spiraling, I'm texting Zoe before it gets bad. I'm not doing that reset-button thing anymore."

Ravi stared at her, thrown off for half a beat.

"Since when do you set terms in this house?"

"Since tonight."

Instead she picked up the foil cake and turned toward the hall.

"I had fun," she said without looking back.

Behind her, his answer came clipped and tired. "Go sleep. Don't be late for your own penalty."

In her room she peeled off damp clothes, showered quickly, and changed into an old camp T-shirt with cracked letters across the chest. She sat on the edge of her bed with the foil packet and ate half the cake with her fingers in the dark, blue frosting staining the pad of her thumb.

Through the wall she could hear Ravi moving in the kitchen, cabinet doors opening and shutting in measured rhythm, kettle filling, followed by the low creak of his ankle on the third hallway board as he passed her door toward his room.

Anika froze, listening.

It was not his step she waited for. She waited for the other one, the imaginary lighter one with the quick key-jingle and the plastic hospital tag tapping metal. She listened until her ears rang and the building settled and nothing happened.

Can you text me when you're starting to spiral, not after you've sat there listening for keys for an hour?

She pulled her phone from her hoodie pocket and typed before she could backspace herself into silence.

I'm starting to spiral. Are you up?

Three dots appeared almost immediately.

I'm up. Put your feet on the floor. Name five things you can touch.

Anika planted both feet on the rug. She pressed her thumb to the rough mattress seam, then the cold watch face, then the damp foil, then the peeling paint on the windowsill, then the notebook cover.

Rug, mattress seam, watch, foil, windowsill, notebook.

Good, Zoe wrote back. Stay with this. Don't disappear on me.

I won't disappear tonight.

She reached for her notebook on the floor.

She wrote the usual headers: date, time home, food.

Her pen hovered.

Then, underneath the usual columns, she wrote a sentence she had not written in months, the words uneven because her hand shook.

I laughed so hard a cookie fell into my shirt and everyone saw.

She stared at it. She did not cross it out.

The watch on her wrist flashed alarm mode: 4:40. Training would begin. Her shin would complain on the first lunges. Dad would track intervals on the legal pad. By six-thirty they would be under Midtown's lobby lights, signing into qualifiers. The new curfew would lock her phone on the kitchen table tomorrow night whether she wanted it there or not.

All of that was still true.

She lay down anyway, frosting sugar on her tongue, party noise ghosting in her ears, and listened to her own anger breathing beside the old waiting at the door.


# Chapter 8

The Midtown club smelled like eucalyptus cleaner and old money before the sun was fully up. At six-thirty, exactly when she'd known they'd be here, the lobby lights were already bright enough to flatten everyone into the same pale color. She had slept in splinters after Zoe's party, frosting sugar still ghosting her tongue when the 4:40 alarm dragged them out. Jackets dripped by the entrance from a thin spring rain, and the front desk girl kept sliding a hand towel over the marble in small anxious circles, as if the puddles could ruin the place's reputation.

Anika signed in with the pen chained to the clipboard and looked up at the bracket board. Her name sat on line twelve in dry-erase block letters: **ANIKA IYER - QUEENS REC**. Next to most of the other names, somebody had written academy initials in tiny smug pen strokes. Her first-round opponent had a last name Anika could not pronounce fast. Her second-round opponent was still TBD.

Ravi stood so close to her shoulder that she could feel the damp from his jacket through her warm-up hoodie.

"You know the objective," he said.

He always said objective like there was only one noun in the world worth respecting.

"Top eight qualifies for regional pool," she said. "Two wins minimum. Maybe three if seeding goes weird."

"Not maybe. We don't need maybe. Two clean wins and then we evaluate."

Jonah Feld came through the side door from the practice courts, unwinding fresh tape around his right wrist while balancing a paper cup of black coffee on his clipboard. Someone had stuck a glitter unicorn sticker on the back of that clipboard long ago, and he had never peeled it off. He nodded once at Anika, then glanced at Ravi.

"You're early for once," Jonah said. "Good. Courts are fast this morning. Ball's staying low."

Ravi gave him the flat nod he used when he wanted to show gratitude without conceding anything.

"She'll adjust."

Jonah looked at Anika's legs, not her face. Coaches did that when they wanted truth.

"How's the shin?"

The question pressed straight into the sore spot she had been hiding. Anika tightened her jaw.

"Fine," she said. "Warm-up twinge."

Jonah's mouth turned to the side. He had a habit of chewing the inside of his cheek when he did not buy what he heard.

"Twinge means data, not drama," he said. "If it spikes, tell somebody before you do anything heroic."

Ravi cut in before she could answer.

"We'll manage it."

Jonah held Ravi's stare for a second too long, then tipped his coffee cup in a tight, reluctant salute and moved toward another player. Anika watched him go and wished she had said something different, or at least slower.

She changed in the locker room with girls from academies whose duffels had matching logos and separate shoe compartments. Their voices ricocheted off tile and mirrors until her head felt hollow.

She sat on the bench under the hand dryer and laced her shoes. Right loop, left loop, pull, tuck. Maya's old digital watch blinked in thin gray bars. A month ago she had reset it every time it drifted. Now she let it wander. Queens Oncology Billing Services still sat behind everything she looked at, faint gray letters ghosting under homework and cereal bowls.

She bounced on the balls of her feet near Court 5. The first landing pinched. The next sent a sharp nick up the inside of her right shin. Then a brighter line flashed toward her knee and dulled, as if her leg had decided to lie for her.

Ravi leaned in through the warm-up crowd noise.

"What's the number?" he asked.

They had turned pain into numbered increments because numbers felt coachable.

"Three," she lied.

"Good. Keep it there."

By the end of her first match, the pain had climbed from warning to threat.

The opener ran quick on paper, 11-5, 11-7, 11-6, but each lunge to the front right corner sent a bolt through the lower third of her tibia so hot it made her teeth click. Her opponent, a compact girl named Rina, kept apologizing whenever rallies ended on lets.

"Sorry, my bad," Rina said after one collision near the T.

At eleven years old, Anika did not know anybody else who apologized during a sanctioned tournament point. She liked Rina for it and hated her for making liking possible in the middle of panic.

After the handshake, Anika walked toward the chairs in a straight line until she was out of sight of the glass and then bent over with both hands on her knees. Her right shin buzzed with the same metallic rebound she heard in court walls after a hard drive. She pressed her thumb into the spot through the fabric and felt a precise ridge of soreness, not muscle, not skin, something deeper and less negotiable.

Ravi handed her a bottle with electrolyte powder already mixed.

"Good pace," he said. "You gave away too many loose points in the middle. We'll tighten that."

"My right—"

"Hydrate first. Talk second."

She drank because she had learned that arguing took oxygen she needed later.

The second round was against Sloane Becker from Hudson Elite. Between points she tapped her racket frame once against her thigh before stepping into the service box.

Sloane did not hit harder than Anika. She hit smarter. High rails when Anika wanted pace. Slow floaters when Anika wanted structure. Straight drives that kissed the wall and died. By the middle of the second game, Anika could feel Sloane probing for movement, especially to the front right.

Pain surged hot enough to fuzz the edges of her vision.

She did not say it.

At 7-7 in game three, Anika chased a boast that died too close to the tin. Her right foot planted. Something in her shin flared so sharply she saw white spots in front of the ball. She still reached it, flicked the pickup, and watched Sloane bury a winner past her shoulder.

Anika turned away and walked to the back wall, breathing through her mouth. Not because she wanted drama. Because the inside of her leg was pulsing with each heartbeat and she could not swallow around it.

Ravi stood behind the glass with both hands on the rail. He caught her eye and gave the signal he used when she wanted to come off tempo: one fist pumping down, two quick jabs toward the front corner. Push. Shorten. End it now.

She hated him for the signal because it worked.

She went short twice and stole the game 11-9. She took the fourth 11-8 on a rally where she could not feel her toes by the end.

When she came out, Jonah was waiting with a folded towel and that same unreadable coach face.

"You're limping," he said.

"It's just stiffness," Ravi answered for her.

Jonah ignored Ravi.

"Anika. You don't owe anybody hero points. You hear me?"

She looked at the floor instead of either adult. A dark wet footprint from somebody's shoe spread on the tile like an ink blot.

"I hear you," she said.

Ravi checked the bracket app on his phone and swore under his breath.

"Qualifying match got moved up. Court 2 in a few minutes."

No time to let her breath settle, let alone the bone grinding at the inside of her shin.

Her qualifying opponent was Marisol Vega, seeded fourth, from a private Manhattan club where the courts had temperature control and towel service. Marisol had broad shoulders and hummed under her breath during warm-up, not pretty but steady, like a metronome she carried in her throat.

The first game lasted thirteen minutes. Marisol fed height and pace changes until Anika's lungs went ragged. 9-9. 10-10. 11-10 Anika on a backhand nick. 11-all after a stroke call that made half the crowd groan. 12-11 Marisol with a soft drop that died before Anika could get under it. 13-11 Marisol when Anika's drive clipped high on the tin because she was half a step late.

Anika came to the towel with her face blank and both calves trembling.

"You're still in this," Ravi said. "Her backhand is leaking when she's stretched. Keep pressure there and finish in front."

"I can't load my right leg on the lunge," Anika said.

Ravi's nostrils flared. "Then don't lunge stupidly. Early prep. More split-step. We didn't come here to survive one game."

The line between coach voice and father voice had disappeared so long ago that she sometimes forgot it had ever existed.

Game two: Anika 11-6. She found pace. She hid pain. She watched Marisol's shoulders tighten when rallies got physical.

Game three: 11-9 Marisol. Anika had three balls she normally killed, and each time her right shin warned her a fraction early, enough to make her hesitate, enough to turn winners into neutral balls.

Between games, Anika sat on the low bench with the towel over her head and pressed the rough cotton into her eyes until sparks popped behind her lids. She synced her breathing to the squeal of shoes on the next court, in through her teeth, out slow, pretending the rhythm belonged to her.

Ravi crouched in front of her, elbows on knees.

"Look at me."

She looked.

"One game. That's all. One game and we qualify. After that we can ice, image, whatever you want. But one game now. Understand?"

The promise hung between them, formal and brittle, each word waiting for a loophole.

"If I stop, we lose the slot," she said.

"If you stop, somebody else takes it," he said. "You know what the pool means. Coaching support, travel subsidy, visibility. This isn't just a medal."

Thirty-eight thousand four hundred dollars sat behind his words. Anika pinched the towel seam until the rough loops bit her palm.

The words from the hardship office came back with fluorescent hum around them: **We can't process another fee waiver without proof of placement trajectory.**

Anika stood before the ref called two-minute warning.

The referee on Court 2 was a college kid in rectangular neon-orange glasses who called scores in a voice that cracked at the edges.

"Final game," he said. "Love-all. Play."

Marisol took an early 4-1 lead. Anika clawed to 4-4 with two lucky clips and one brutal rally that left her seeing stars at the edges of the court.

At 5-5, Marisol drove tight to the backhand corner. Anika pivoted, sprinted, planted, and pushed off right.

A white-hot stab ripped through her shin, sudden and precise, metal deep in bone.

Her knee buckled. She caught herself with the racket, popped the ball up, recovered by instinct, and somehow won the point when Marisol guessed short and the ball sailed long.

She did not celebrate. She walked to the service box and set her face in place.

From behind the glass Ravi gave the signal again: fist down, fist down, jab forward. Keep going.

The next five points narrowed into a tunnel. Anika took 6-5 with a forehand drop that clipped the nick. Marisol leveled at 6-all when she pinned Anika in the back right and forced a weak boast. Marisol went up 7-6 after a long rally where each breath scraped Anika's throat raw. Anika pulled it back to 7-all on a stroke decision that made Ravi slap the rail once, hard. She edged ahead at 8-7 after a desperate counterdrop and a scrape that left a gray floor streak on her left knee. Marisol took the next point, then the lead at 9-8, and Anika pulled it to 9-all.

At 9-all, Marisol served hard to the body. Anika jammed a backhand return and drifted too far left. Marisol saw the open lane and killed the ball to the front right.

Anika moved before thought. She took a right step, loaded, and fired.

The pain hit so hard her vision narrowed to a ring, black at the edges, court-white in the middle. She got the ball by a fingertip of racket and sent a weak lob high enough to buy one more second. Marisol took it early and drove crosscourt. Anika stumbled, reached, dug, and felt a tearing vibration up the tibia with the impact.

The rally kept stretching, each contact rattling up her arm until her grip felt flayed.

The crowd behind the glass had gone quiet in that mean way crowds do when they smell blood and discipline together.

Marisol finally floated a ball too short in the middle. Anika stepped in and hit a forehand volley drop so soft the ball seemed embarrassed to touch the floor.

She reached 10-9 and earned match ball.

Ravi did not clap. He pressed both palms to the glass and mouthed one word that she could read even through reflections.

**Finish.**

On match ball, Marisol served deep. The rally moved side to side, then front to back, then side again. Anika could not push properly off right anymore, so she started cheating half a step early, gambling on patterns instead of reacting. On the fourteenth shot, Marisol went for a front-left winner. The ball sat up just enough.

Anika lunged with her left leg instead, dragged the right like dead weight, and knifed a backhand counterdrop that died inches above the tin.

Marisol arrived late, scraped, and sent the ball into the metal.

The referee's voice cracked on the final score.

"Eleven-nine. Match to Iyer."

Sound came back all at once: claps, sneaker squeaks, somebody's ringtone, Ravi shouting her name through the glass. Anika turned toward center court for the handshake. Marisol met her with a grim smile and a damp palm.

"Good fight," Marisol said, still breathing hard. "You're scary."

"You too," Anika said.

She meant it. Marisol touched the red charm bracelet once, like checking it was still there, then walked off toward her coach without looking back.

Anika took three steps toward the door.

On the fourth, her right leg gave out completely.

There was no graceful collapse, no dramatic faint. Her shin folded under load and dropped her sideways. Her shoulder hit first, then hip, then the side of her face. The racket clattered and spun away. Pain came not as a stab this time but as a deep grinding shock that made her gag immediately.

She rolled onto her back and made a noise she had never heard herself make, low and animal and humiliated.

People were already inside the court. The referee knelt near her shoes, saying something about space. Marisol hovered by the door with both hands over her mouth. Ravi dropped to his knees by Anika's head and reached for her calf.

"Cramp," he said fast. "It's a cramp. We need electrolyte and stretch."

"Don't touch it," Anika gasped. The words barely came out around the nausea.

A woman in navy tournament medical scrubs slid in between Ravi and Anika with the confidence of somebody who had done this in worse rooms. Her badge said **Denise Delgado, ATC**. A tiny enamel pin of an otter in goggles was clipped above her name, and cinnamon gum made the air sharp when she spoke.

"Mr. Iyer, give me space and keep that leg still," Denise said. "Anika, eyes on me. Wiggle your toes."

Anika did. Her right foot twitched.

"Good. Where's it worst?"

"Inside right shin. Lower. Nine. Maybe ten."

Denise nodded once and palpated gently along the tibia. At one point Anika flinched and swore under her breath.

"Pinpoint tenderness along the distal tibia," Denise said. "Sideline call: match is over and she's non-weight-bearing right now."

"She can stand," Ravi said. "She's been playing through this for weeks. It spikes, then settles."

Denise looked up at him with no drama and no deference.

"That's exactly why she doesn't take another test step."

She signaled to another medic for an air splint and a transport chair. A boy with trembling hands rolled the chair in and bumped into the wall. The crowd near Court 2 had gone from excited to awkward in under a minute, parents staring at phones they were not reading.

Anika tried to sit up. The motion sent a hot wave through her shin and into her stomach. She vomited neon orange electrolyte onto the court floor.

Ravi jerked back. His face changed, not softer, just stripped.

"Anika. Hey. Hey, breathe."

She wanted to answer, but her body had moved past language.

Denise and the second medic splinted the leg, buckled her into the transport chair, and pushed her toward the medical room behind the lobby. Every crack in the hallway tile sent a fresh jolt up bone. She pressed the heel of her hand against her mouth and fixed on the fluorescent buzz and the sway of hallway tiles to keep from floating out of her body.

In the medical room, Denise cut Anika's sock at the seam and checked swelling. The scissors rasped through damp fabric, and every tiny tug felt aimed at the bright center of pain.

"We're calling for urgent imaging," she said. "Could be a stress reaction, could be a fracture line. Either way, she's done competing today."

Urgent imaging meant forms, timestamps, and adults asking when this started. Anika could already hear herself deciding which truth would cost less.

"She qualified," Ravi said, as if the sentence could bargain.

"She qualified and she's done. Both things can be true."

He started pacing the length of the room in short loops, hand over mouth. Each turn made the chair arm creak; each creak made Anika brace for the next pulse up bone.

"We'll take her to urgent care ourselves," he said. "No ambulance."

Anika did not miss the money math under that. Another ambulance bill would land in the same month as everything else.

"Fine," Denise said. "Then she stays in that chair all the way to your car. No test steps."

She handed Ravi a one-page transfer note with tournament logo at the top and checkbox language at the bottom. Ravi scanned it, jaw flexing.

"You wrote suspected fracture."

"Because I suspect a fracture."

"Do you know what this does to seeding?"

Anika stared at the checkbox and felt nausea rise again. Her leg had failed on live court, and seeding was still the first emergency in the room.

Denise held his gaze. "I know what one more push-through can do to a crack like this. Pick your loss."

The subway was impossible with the chair and splint, so Ravi called a rideshare with surge pricing and did not complain about the fare. In the back seat, Anika kept her right leg straight across the seat and watched raindrops race each other down the window. Her hoodie stuck damp to her neck. The city slid past in broken frames: steam from a vent, a man carrying tulips under a trash bag, a deli awning with one letter burned out.

Ravi called somebody from the front seat. Jonah, maybe. Anika could hear only his side.

"She got through. She qualified. Then she went down hard... No, not muscle... I know what you said... Don't start, Jonah... I'm not asking for blame right now."

He hung up and immediately opened the tournament app again, thumb moving too fast over the screen.

"Dad," Anika said.

"Yeah."

"Please stop checking."

He looked at her in the rearview mirror. For a second he seemed surprised she still existed inside this logistics storm.

"Okay," he said, and put the phone face down on his thigh.

The urgent orthopedic clinic sat on the second floor above a pharmacy that sold discount compression sleeves and greeting cards.

At intake, the receptionist asked in a flat sequence without looking up.

"Patient name, date of birth, insurance, and what happened."

"Squash tournament," Ravi said. "She'd been grinding through shin pain, then she folded on court."

He sounded like he had practiced the sentence in the elevator.

They waited forty-two minutes. Pain settled into a mean steady throb that made time stretchy. Anika watched condensation bead and slide down the water cooler and felt phantom starts in her calves, the muscle memory of drills with nowhere to go.

When a medical assistant called her name, she mispronounced Iyer, apologized, and moved them straight to imaging.

In the exam room, an X-ray tech named Hari Patel positioned Anika's leg with quick competent hands.

"Can you point exactly where it hurts most?" he asked.

She pointed.

He took three views and then one more with a marker at the tender spot. Each repositioning dragged a thin, jagged line of pain through her shin.

They were back in the exam room for less than ten minutes before Dr. Sonal Mehta came in carrying a tablet and a paper chart thick with copied forms. She wore bright teal clogs scuffed near the toe, and her stethoscope tubing was patched near the earpiece with a strip of silver duct tape.

She introduced herself directly to Anika first.

"I'm Dr. Mehta. This is a rough way to end a tournament day."

She reviewed the X-rays in silence for a full minute, then turned the tablet so both Anika and Ravi could see.

"See this cortical line along the right distal tibia?" she said, tracing with her finger. "That's a stress fracture, not just shin splints. If impact keeps loading this spot, the crack can propagate into a full break."

Ravi leaned forward.

"Could this still read as a stress reaction? We've got regionals in six weeks. She can bike, keep conditioning, maybe tape and track pain—"

Dr. Mehta held up a hand, calm but hard.

"No impact activity for six weeks minimum. No running, no jumping, no match play, no ghosting, no treadmill incline work. None of it."

"Six weeks is the whole lead-in," Ravi said. "If she shuts down completely, she loses—"

"If you keep loading this bone, she doesn't lose six weeks," Dr. Mehta said. "She loses months, and maybe surgery."

She shifted her attention back to Anika.

"Can you tell me how long you've had focal pain here?"

Anika looked at Ravi by reflex. Dr. Mehta waited.

"About a month," Anika said. "Worse in damp weather. Worse on front-court lunges."

Ravi pressed both palms flat on his knees.

"You said three out of ten this morning."

"I said what worked," Anika said before she could stop herself.

Silence filled the room, buzzing under the fluorescent lights.

Dr. Mehta did not pretend she had not heard.

"Kids under pressure underreport pain all the time," she said. "That's why we use objective restrictions, not vibes or optimism."

She tapped her tablet and pulled up the return-to-play form with bold checkboxes.

"I'm writing the order now. You'll leave with a printed copy and a portal copy: no impact for six weeks, protected weight-bearing with boot and crutches for now, and sports follow-up in ten to fourteen days. If your tournament program asks for documentation, we send it directly."

She turned the tablet toward Ravi again. A signature box glowed at the bottom of the page.

"Sign here so there's no confusion," she said. "Your signature confirms you received the order and that it controls training at home and at the club until sports medicine clears her."

Ravi's face tightened.

"Send it to me. I'll decide what goes to the program."

"You don't get to choose that part," Dr. Mehta said. "In sanctioned junior events, this injury note is mandatory reporting. It goes to guardian and event medical records."

For the first time all day, someone told Ravi no in a voice that did not shake.

He stared at the floor tile pattern, little gray squares repeating into a grid that had no weak point.

"Fine," he said. The word sounded bitten.

Dr. Mehta examined Anika's leg again, this time explaining each step before touching. She checked pulses, sensation, compartment softness, knee range, ankle range. She fitted a black walking boot and then made Anika try crutches in the hallway.

"Left foot steps, crutches together, right leg floats," she said. "No hero hopping."

Anika nodded and moved three careful lengths of the hall. The boot was heavy and made her right leg feel borrowed.

When they returned to the room, Dr. Mehta handed over the paperwork.

At the top, in all caps: **RIGHT TIBIAL STRESS FRACTURE**.

Under restrictions: **NO IMPACT ACTIVITY X 6 WEEKS. NON-NEGOTIABLE.**

At the bottom was her signature, thick and slanted, and a direct clinic line.

Ravi took the pages as if they were a bill collector's final notice.

At discharge, the nurse stapled a packet with pain-management instructions, bone-loading education, and a follow-up appointment slip. Cartoon frogs marched across her scrub cap. As she wrapped the spare ace bandage around the packet, she leaned toward Anika.

"You can be upset and mad at the same time," she said quietly. "You don't have to call pain motivation just so adults back off."

In the pharmacy downstairs, Ravi bought calcium chewables, ibuprofen, and crutch pads, then stood in line for ten minutes staring at the debit terminal while it processed. The total came to $86.14. He flinched harder at the number than he had at the diagnosis.

Outside, rain had turned from mist to cold needles. Ravi folded the clinic packet under his jacket and flagged another car. He helped Anika in, careful now, too careful, his hands hovering before each touch as if permission had become a language he did not know.

Traffic on Queensboro Bridge stalled. Brake lights burned red into the wet darkening afternoon. Anika rested her forehead against the window and watched the river shear into gray panels below.

Ravi spoke without looking at her.

"You should've told me it was worse."

"You would've sat me," she said.

"Maybe."

"Not maybe. You would've sat me and we would've lost the slot."

"You don't know that."

"I know how you talk when there's a choice."

He swallowed and rubbed his left ankle with his right hand, an old habit when his own joint flared in damp weather.

"You think I pushed you because of rankings," he said.

"I think you watched me drag my right leg at nine-all and still gave me the keep-going signal," she said. "You told me to finish when I couldn't plant."

The sentence hung between them, raw and too accurate.

Ravi's hand came off his ankle and hovered toward her boot before curling back into a fist.

"I gave the signal," he said. "Yeah. We were one game from the slot and you said you could still move."

He shook his head once, hard, trying to throw the last rally out the window.

"I should've stopped it when you grabbed the wall at seven-all. I didn't."

His jaw jumped. "But don't talk as if I was the only one chasing that spot."

Anika dragged her nails along the seat seam until a loose thread snapped.

"I was eleven. You were the adult in the glass."

Traffic lurched and threw both of them forward. Ravi caught the door handle with white knuckles and kept his eyes on the wet road.

"We've got Dr. Mehta's order now," he said. "If we break it, they report us and you lose more than six weeks. That's where we're at."

Anika kept her forehead against the window until cold started to burn.

"I heard the order," she said. "I didn't hear you."

She dug her thumbnail into the webbing of her left hand until the skin went hot.

Anika closed her eyes. Behind her eyelids she could still see Court 2, white lines crisscrossed and glaring, a space built for running with prohibition stamped across it.

By the time they reached Jackson Heights, evening had settled fully. The apartment stairwell smelled of frying onions from the downstairs unit and bleach from somebody's half-finished mopping job. Ravi carried the crutches and clinic packet. Anika took the stairs one step at a time with the rail and boot and all the patience she usually spent elsewhere.

Inside, the kitchen was exactly as they had left it that morning: steel tumbler in sink, turmeric stain near the stove, the grocery flyer Priya had mailed them folded under a chili-pepper-shaped magnet.

Ravi set the paperwork on the table and stood there with both hands braced on the chair back.

"Eat first," he said. "Then meds with food."

Anika dropped into the kitchen chair and pulled Maya's notebook from her bag. The cover had gone soft at the corners from months of sweat and bus rides. She turned to a fresh page.

Date. Tournament name. Opponents. Scores.

Then she wrote: **Qualified. Collapsed. Right tibial stress fracture. Six weeks no impact.**

Her handwriting got smaller on the next line.

**Debt does not care if bone heals.**

She scratched it out hard enough to tear the paper.

Ravi moved around the kitchen opening cabinets too loudly, pulling rice from one shelf, lentils from another, then putting both back. He ended up heating leftover lemon rice from last night and overcooking it by five minutes. The mustard seeds popped in the pan and one jumped onto his wrist. He cursed, then laughed once at himself without humor.

When plates were on the table, he slid the clinic packet toward her.

"We need this where we can see it," he said.

He took a magnet from the fridge, then another when the first one slipped under the packet's weight. The order sat center height now, impossible to miss. Bold letters on white paper under fluorescent kitchen light:

**NO IMPACT ACTIVITY X 6 WEEKS**

**RUNNING / JUMPING / SQUASH PROHIBITED**

Near the header, a blue clinic stamp read: **RIGHT TIBIA, STRESS FRACTURE.**

At the bottom, Dr. Mehta's signature cut diagonally across the page, a barrier line.

Ravi pulled the legal-pad interval sheet off the side of the fridge, read the Monday block once, then folded it twice and shoved it into the junk drawer under the takeout menus.

He opened a text thread with Jonah and Priya, took a photo of the order, and typed out loud as his thumbs moved.

"Confirmed right tibial stress fracture. Six weeks no impact. No court work until medical clearance."

He added another line, paused, then backspaced the whole sentence until the cursor blinked on empty space.

"What did you delete?" Anika asked.

He hit send and turned the screen toward Anika for a second before locking it.

"Details for later," he said. "Tonight they get the medical facts."

Anika stared at it until the letters blurred.

Without drills waiting in the morning, there would be hours to fill that had no court lines and no rally counts. Hours where grief could walk in without paying membership dues.

Ravi sat down across from her. He picked at his food, did not eat much, and kept glancing at the paper on the fridge as if it might rewrite itself if he watched long enough.

"I told you to finish when you were already hurt," he said finally. "I made that call."

Anika touched the edge of the notebook page where she had torn it and felt the rough fibers catch her fingertip.

"Then tell Priya that part too."

He rubbed the cracked edge of his phone case with his thumbnail, not meeting her eyes.

"I'm not handing over every mistake by text for people to screenshot," he said. "We'll talk in person."

He stood, reopened the junk drawer, and pulled the folded interval sheet back out.

The watch on her wrist beeped the hour. Before she could pull away, Ravi caught her wrist, thumbed the side buttons, and reset the alarm to 4:40. He held her arm toward the microwave clock until both green displays matched.

The numbers blinked once, then held. She kept her arm where he left it, skin stinging under the strap.


# Chapter 9

At 4:13 a.m., Anika woke before the watch could buzz. Last night Ravi had thumbed the alarm back to 4:40 and matched it to the microwave clock exactly, and now both green digits still read 4:13 while she lay awake waiting for the buzz she no longer needed. The apartment was dark, and she lay still long enough to hear three separate sounds from the building: pipes knocking two floors down, somebody's toilet tank refilling, the elevator cables whining through their own metal throat. Her body had learned this hour the way skin learns a scar. At this hour she used to think in a strict sequence: court time, water bottle, bandage tape, bus.

Then the fact landed again, blunt as a hand on the sternum. There was no court and no impact for six weeks. The restriction sheet on the fridge said `right tibia, stress fracture` in blue stamped letters.

She rolled onto her back and felt for pain before she moved, the way she had been doing since the hospital. The bone did not throb while she was still. It only answered when weight asked it a question. Humid air stuck to her neck, and she could not tell if she was sweating or just already awake enough to notice every part of herself. She flexed her toes under the sheet, testing, counting: one, two, three, four, five. She hated that she was counting before she had even sat up.

The hallway outside her room was seven and a half strides if she took normal steps and six if she made her hips turn fast like ghosting drills. She had measured it three nights ago. Tonight she waited until 4:31, because the rule on the notebook page said she had to try breathing first. In for four counts, hold for four, out for six. A school counselor had taught the class this in sixth grade after a fire alarm drill went wrong and made two kids cry. It had sounded fake then. It sounded fake now.

By 4:38 she was pacing anyway, left foot, right boot, left foot, right boot, one hand on the wall whenever she had to pivot. The fracture boot made a dry plastic click each time it hit tile. She kept the volume low by rolling through the sole, a clumsy motion that worked the outside of her calf and made her ankle ache. She did twelve loops, stopped, wrote 12 in the notebook on the kitchen counter, then did twelve more because twenty-four looked better than twelve and better looked safer.

Stillness had been hard on court too, but there was always the next serve, the next sprint to a corner, the next instruction barked through glass. Here stillness had no whistle and no end. It expanded. It pulled memory toward it.

When she paused at the sink to drink, the metallic taste in the tap water tipped her into a hospital flash she did not ask for. It gave her a bright room, a plastic cup, and her mother saying very gently that the ice chips were too cold for her mouth today. Anika gripped the edge of the sink until her knuckles hurt and forced herself to look at the chipped yellow tile instead of the memory. She could name four cracks that looked like letters. C. I. T. Y. She did that twice and the room came back to Queens, to their narrow kitchen with curry leaves dried to the underside of the spice rack and one dead fruit fly on the windowsill.

She opened the notebook to the first pages where the columns still said `reps`, `load`, `pain / 10`, `food`, `mistakes`. She had started those entries in late winter, the week after they boxed Amma's clothes and before borough ladder weekends swallowed every Saturday. Now new pages carried different headers in her blocky pen pressure: `sleep onset`, `awake minutes`, `night wakings`, `shin ache @ rest`, `panic spike`, `hallway loops`.

She wrote:

Night 5
asleep 01:42
awake 03:56-04:13
panic 6/10
loops 24 (quiet)

Then she added, on a line below she had not planned:

heard Amma cough in memory even though no one coughed.

The sun rose slow and dirty behind the opposite building, light catching on the satellite dish that never pointed right. At 5:47 Ravi came out of his room in his depot polo and navy work pants, lanyard already around his neck, keys clipped to his belt. He had not shaved. Stubble grayed the lower half of his face and made him look older than forty-four, older than her teachers' dads, older than last week.

He glanced at her once and reached for tea leaves like finding her awake at dawn had already become routine.

"You take your tablet?" he asked, opening the cabinet for tea leaves.

"After food." Her voice came out scratchy. "It's better if I eat first. That's what the sheet says."

"Good." He set water to boil and rubbed his left ankle against the back of his right calf, a habit he did when he stood still too long. "You need protein at breakfast. Don't just drink tea."

"I'm not five."

"I didn't say you were."

He kept facing the stove. She watched his reflection in the dark microwave door because he would not turn. Steam fogged the pot lid. He killed the flame before it hissed over.

"What time are you home?" she asked.

"Late."

"Late like eight or late like midnight?"

"Depends on dispatch." He reached for his lunch container, then checked his phone and frowned. "Maybe I'll do app runs after shift."

"Again?"

"Again."

She waited for him to say why, but he only tightened the lid on the steel tiffin and slid it into his worn black backpack. The zipper tab was half broken and he always had to pinch it with two fingers. He had never replaced it.

"You don't have to leave this early," she said. "My checkup isn't until next week."

"I'm not leaving for that." He finally looked at her, only for a second. His eyes moved from her face to the boot and back to the sink. "I'm leaving because I'm on the six-thirty board."

The kettle clicked dry against the burner. He swore, low and tired, and moved it off heat. Then he went to the fridge, stared at the restriction order like he was reading a language he almost remembered, and stuck a utility envelope under the magnet beside it.

Red block letters stamped across the front: FINAL WARNING.

He saw her seeing it and put his palm over the amount line before she could read the number. She had already read it upside down: 1,860.12.

"Eat," he said. "And keep your foot up between classes."

"It's June. We barely have classes."

"Then keep it up at home."

He left without drinking his tea.

Noon heat turned the apartment stale with old warmth and detergent from downstairs. By then Anika had done every seated thing on the discharge sheet twice: ankle alphabet, glute squeeze, calf tension holds without movement. They took thirteen minutes total and left the rest of the day untouched. She stared at the minute hand on the watch she wore, Maya's old digital one with a scratched face and a strap repaired with clear nail polish, and felt anger rise without a target: anger at the bone, anger at Ravi, and anger at kids posting summer-camp photos in bright team shirts like the world was a straight line from effort to reward.

Zoe texted at 1:06 p.m.

`u alive?`

A second text came before Anika answered.

`my brother tried to microwave a spoon. fire. mom screamed his government name.`

Anika smiled despite herself and typed back, `alive. doing ankle alphabet like a prisoner.`

`gross`

`yeah`

`want me to come over w popsicles?`

Anika looked at the dishes in the sink, the folded laundry she had not touched, the notebook open to a page where numbers kept trying to become rules. She wanted Zoe there. She did not want to be watched while she failed at being still.

`can't today. house is weird.`

`k. tell weird house i said fight me.`

She put the phone down and listened for her own laugh. It did not come out.

Couch fabric stuck to the back of her knees by 3:20, her leg propped on two cushions while she tried to read the summer novel packet for school. The sentence on the page stayed readable, then dissolved, then snapped back. After four pages she realized she had not absorbed one thing except that her heartbeat was audible in her right ear. She shut the packet and put her wristwatch speaker against her cheek to feel the faint vibration when the seconds changed.

The apartment was loud with absence. There was no squash ball echo, no timer alarms, and no Ravi calling split times from the doorframe. The silence did not feel quiet. It felt like being trapped under the court glass while everyone else left and the lights switched off one by one.

She got up, forgot the boot by instinct, stepped full weight on the right leg, and saw white. Pain shot up clean and electric from shin to hip. She grabbed the table edge, swore into her shoulder, and hopped backward until she could jam the boot on. The pain settled into a deep hot line along the bone and stayed there like punishment.

She wrote it down because writing it down had become the only proof that she was not making it dramatic. At 15:36 she logged the accidental full load on her right leg, pain spiking to eight for about twenty seconds before settling at four, still pulsing under the boot line.

Underneath, in smaller letters: idiot.

***

Ravi came home after nine with diesel and city heat in his shirt and a fast-food coffee cup gone soft in his hand. He set his bag on the floor, washed his hands, and stood at the sink longer than necessary, palms flat on porcelain while the faucet ran until it coughed air. He still had the lanyard around his neck.

Anika sat at the table with reheated lemon rice and yogurt she had stirred too hard. The spoon clinked against ceramic every time she lifted it. She had timed his absence without meaning to. Fifteen hours and fourteen minutes had passed since he left.

"There's food," she said.

"I ate on break."

"You said you'd come by seven."

"Dispatch got short. Two drivers called out." He dried his hands and checked his phone. "Then there was a collision on Roosevelt. Whole line backed up."

"You could've texted."

"I did text."

He had: `late. eat.` at 7:41. She hated that she had checked the timestamp three times.

He opened the fridge, looked at nothing, then took out the utility envelope she had seen that morning. He tore it with his thumb and scanned the page while standing. His jaw moved once, side to side.

"What did it say?" she asked.

"It says pay by Friday." He folded the letter into a precise rectangle. "It says what these things always say."

"How much?"

"You don't need to track that."

"I already saw it."

"Then why ask?"

The question hit like a slap because it sounded like her own defensive voice, older and meaner. She pushed her bowl away.

"Because no one tells me anything until it's on paper with red ink."

He kept his eyes on the envelope. "You're eleven."

"I was eleven when you told me if I wanted to win I had to know every number."

"That's not the same and you know it."

"It's exactly the same."

"No." He looked up then, finally, and she saw how bloodshot his eyes were. "One set of numbers helps you move better on court. This set tells me whether we have lights next month."

The kitchen went still. Even the fridge compressor seemed to wait.

She heard herself ask, too quickly, "Is it because of me?"

"It's because your mother got sick in America," he said, voice suddenly flat with exhaustion. "It's because hospitals bill like casinos and grief doesn't come with a payment plan and I'm trying to keep the utilities on. It's because life is expensive. It's not because you got hurt."

The word `casinos` sat between them like something sharp.

"Don't put Amma in the same sentence as a slot machine," she said. "Don't make her sound like a charge code."

His thumb bent the envelope edge until the paper creased. "They billed her in fifteen-minute lines while she was still alive, then in bigger lines after. I'm not trying to insult her. I'm telling you what showed up in the mailbox."

"Then look at me when you say it."

He did. It lasted maybe two seconds and cost him. "I'm looking," he said. "I still don't know how to carry this without dropping it on you."

He exhaled hard through his nose and rubbed the bridge of it with two fingers, hard enough to blanch the skin, trying to stop the headache before it spread. When he spoke again, his tone started to drop back to procedural, and she cut in before the checklist could take over.

"Don't do the yes-no thing and call it talking," she said. "Either ask me like my father, or run the whole rehab check with the sheet open."

He stared at her for a beat, then pulled Dr. Mehta's order from under the fridge magnet and sat down again. "Fine. Full check. Exercises?"

"Yes."

"Ice?"

"Twice."

"Any sharp pain at rest?"

"No." She lied without blinking. The ache was still there from the forgotten step.

He held her gaze a second longer. "Tomorrow before I leave, we set the med alarms together. If this slips, it slips on both of us."

He took his plate from the rack and ate standing at the counter, eyes fixed on laminate and nowhere else. He chewed fast, food as fuel and never comfort. Halfway through he hissed and shifted weight, rolling his left ankle once before setting his heel down again.

"Your ankle's bad," Anika said.

"It's old."

"You should get it checked."

"I should get twelve things checked."

His phone buzzed with the depot ringtone, a harsh two-note chirp she hated. He answered before the second ring.

"Yeah, Sandeep." Pause. "I can take it." Pause. "No, I'll keep the app on after. Send me the reroute map." Longer pause, his face turned toward the window where the fire escape cast ladder shadows across the wall. "Yeah. I said I've got it."

He hung up and finished the last bite of rice.

"You're going back out?" she said.

"Half shift." He rinsed his plate and set it in the rack with exact care. "Maybe four hours."

"You just got home."

"I know."

"Then stay."

His mouth tightened. "Don't start this tonight."

"I'm not starting anything. I'm asking you to sit down and look at me for five minutes."

"Anika."

"No, seriously. Five." She held up her hand, all five fingers out, childish and furious at the same time. "You don't talk to me unless it's pills or icing or forms. You're here and you're not here."

He turned off the kitchen light even though she was still at the table; the overhead went dark, leaving only the stove hood bulb and the streetlight through blinds. "I'm trying to keep us afloat," he said. "If you want me to be home more, tell ConEd to wait. Tell oncology billing to forget our file."

"Stop using bills like a shield."

"Stop making everything about my eye contact." The words came out sharper than he intended. He looked away immediately, ashamed and angry at once. "I'm tired."

She stood too fast, chair scraping. "I'm tired too and I'm not even allowed to run around one stupid court and hit one stupid ball. I don't have anywhere to put it."

"Put what?"

"Any of it."

That landed. For one second she thought he might sit down.

Instead he picked up his keys.

"We'll talk Sunday," he said. "I've got day shift only then."

"You said that last Sunday."

He flinched, tiny but visible. "I know. Noon, kitchen table, phones down. If I miss it, you call Priya before I walk back in."

He left with the apartment key held between his first two fingers, an old habit from late shifts. The lock clicked twice behind him. She stood in the dark kitchen listening to his steps go down the stairwell, counting each landing until the outer door shut and the building swallowed him.

She wrote in the notebook without sitting: 21:48 attempt conversation w Dad, result failure, voice escalation both sides, control partial.

The pen tore the page on `failure`. She pressed harder and wrote through the rip.

Then she crossed out `partial` and replaced it with `poor`.

His 11:17 text came from somewhere on Queens Boulevard.

`You asleep?`

She looked at the message bubble until the screen dimmed. Then she typed, `No.`

Three dots appeared, disappeared, appeared again.

`Take med before bed.`

There was no apology and no question. She locked the phone.

***

On the sixth night the apartment had taught her a new map. She knew where the floorboard outside Ravi's room complained, which window needed a folded receipt under the frame so the rattle stopped, how long the upstairs couple fought before one of them cried and the other ran the shower to hide it, and how many seconds passed between subway rumble and glass tremor in the kitchen cabinet.

None of that helped when lights-out came.

The worst time was the seven minutes after she switched off her lamp and before her eyes adjusted. In that strip of dark her body waited for instruction: split step, lunge, recover. No instruction arrived, and grief did.

It came specific and physical, never abstract. She remembered her mother's thumbnail flattened from years opening lab vials, the exact medicinal-sweet smell of chlorhexidine wipes, a strand of hair caught in the seam of the hospital pillowcase that Anika had pretended not to see, and the way Maya had once asked for mango and then could not swallow more than one cube.

Anika sat up, reached under the bed, and pulled out the old silver phone she had charged in secret. It had a crack across the upper corner and a sticker of a cartoon squash racket Zoe had stuck there in fourth grade. The screen took a full five seconds to wake. She opened `Saved Voice` and pressed play on the file she now knew by timestamp: 00:43, February 18.

Maya's voice filled the tiny speaker, a little compressed, background noise from a TV somewhere far off.

"Hey, Niks. It's Amma. I put lemon rice on the middle shelf, with extra peanuts because you always steal those first. Heat it with a spoon of water so it doesn't dry out. Tell your dad to wait for the mustard seeds to pop before he throws in the curry leaves, or he'll smoke out the whole kitchen again."

There was a soft laugh, then a breath that hitched once.

"I know you're pretending you're not tired. Eat first. Homework after. You can be stubborn tomorrow. I love you."

The message ended with the old iOS click.

Anika replayed it.

Before the first sentence finished, shame slid in: she was using her mother's voice the way she used drills, as anesthesia with a timer.

On the third loop she mouthed along with `middle shelf` and `spoon of water`. On the fifth she noticed a sound she had missed before, the scrape of a chair leg in the background. On the seventh she anticipated the hitch in Maya's breath and felt panic jab her ribs anyway. She pressed repeat harder than necessary, thumb white against glass.

She treated forty-three seconds as one rep, because reps were the only unit her body trusted. She made rules because rules were what she had and wrote them in block letters, pressing hard enough to dent the page beneath.

No replay before 10:30 p.m.
No replay in daylight.
No replay if Ravi was home in the room.
Maximum ten loops.

She was on loop sixteen by 12:52 a.m. when Ravi knocked once on her partly open door.

"You're awake," he said.

She yanked one earbud free though she had only been wearing one. "Yeah."

He stayed in the doorway, one hand on the frame, shoes still on. "Screen time this late isn't helping."

"It's not random videos."

"Then what?"

She hesitated. The phone was warm in her palm. "Mom."

His face changed, not soft exactly, but unguarded for half a beat. "Voice note?"

She nodded.

"How many times?"

"A few."

"A few is not a number."

"Why do you need a number for this?"

"Because numbers tell me when something is becoming a problem."

"Everything is a problem for you unless it has a chart."

"That's not fair."

"It's true."

He looked past her at the notebook on the nightstand, open to columns she had not meant him to see. `awake 5x`. `loops 18`. `replay count 11`. His mouth tightened.

"You should tell Tess this when you start PT," he said.

"We haven't started." Her throat felt scraped from disuse and swallowed words. "And she's not a sleep wizard."

"She's part of recovery."

"Recovery from what? The fracture or being a person you can manage?"

He inhaled slowly and leaned his shoulder against the frame as if he needed its support.

"You think I want this," he said. "You think I wanted you in that x-ray room?"

"I think you wanted me to finish no matter what. You said it with your hand through the glass." Her own voice rose; she forced it down before it cracked. "I did exactly what you wanted and now I can't shut my head off for one hour."

"I know."

"Do you?"

"Yes." He rubbed his chest once, quick, like he could smooth the ache there with his palm. "I hear your room light at two in the morning. I hear the boot in the hallway. I'm not blind, Anika."

She almost corrected him to `you're not blind`, then didn't. He sounded older in that moment than she had ever heard him.

"Then come in," she said quietly. "Don't stand there like a supervisor doing rounds."

He gave one humorless huff of breath that might have been a laugh in another life. He stepped in, sat on the edge of her desk chair, and kept both hands clasped between his knees. His gaze kept skidding to the door after each second it touched her face.

"Play it," he said.

She pressed the screen. Maya's voice rose again in the dark room, practical and teasing and alive in all the wrong tense. Ravi closed his eyes at `tell your dad to wait for the mustard seeds` and pinched the bridge of his nose until the message ended.

"Again?" Anika asked.

He stood up immediately. "No." His voice was rough now. "If I listen twice, I won't sleep at all."

He moved to the door, stopped, and spoke without turning.

"Set a limit. Please. For both of us."

Then he left.

She stared at the dark doorway until the hallway floorboard settled. The apartment gave her its usual inventory: radiator tick, upstairs faucet, a far subway groan that made the cabinet glass tremble once. She put the phone face down and counted to sixty with both palms flat on her thighs.

She made it to 1:07 before she failed and pressed play again.

The first syllable hit like swallowing a coin; relief and self-disgust arrived together.

Loop eleven came with her jaw locked so hard her molars hurt. Loop thirteen made the rubbed skin above her ankle burn where the boot had chafed it raw. On loop fifteen she stopped hearing words and started timing breath: laugh, hitch, click, restart. At 1:41 she told herself `last one`. At 1:42 she hit repeat before the click had even finished.

When she finally dropped the phone, a pale strip of dawn had appeared at the top edge of the curtain and her right hand was cramped into a claw around the case.

The 6:00 alarm on Maya's watch went off against her wrist and she slapped it silent without opening her eyes. Next wake was 7:23, bright room, dry mouth, anti-inflammatory still sealed in foil on the nightstand.

She swallowed the pill on an empty stomach, tasted chalk and bile, and sat on the bathroom floor with her forehead against the tub until the nausea eased. At 7:46 she started the seated drill set late. Ankle alphabet smeared into sloppy half-letters by D. Calf tension holds were supposed to be three rounds of thirty seconds; she quit halfway through round two when a hot line lit up the fracture site.

She logged the failure because she no longer trusted memory: night 6 / morning, replay count 18, meds missed window by 68 min, seated drills incomplete (2/3 rounds), shin ache during holds 5/10.

The ritual worked, which was the danger. Her pulse dropped by the third replay. Her hands stopped shaking by the fifth. The relief always arrived and always sent a bill later.

Sunday afternoon, while Ravi was at an extra depot shift that was supposed to be his only shift, she sat at the kitchen table with ice melting down her shin and rewrote her recovery schedule into something she could survive.

Doctor sheet said: rest, elevate, anti-inflammatory, no impact, begin PT intake next week.

Anika version said:

06:00 wake (even if slept late)
06:15 meds + breakfast
06:30 seated drills
07:00 shower
...
22:30 voicemail loops (cap 10)
23:30 lights out

It looked like discipline. It looked like control. It looked almost exactly like the training plans Ravi used to tape on the fridge, except this one had no court time and a new column labeled `night waking reason`.

When Ravi came home after dark, she showed him the page before she could lose nerve.

"I made a schedule," she said.

He scanned it fast, lips moving slightly at each time stamp. "Clock's fine. Penalties are not. Injury week is meds, food, sleep, PT intake. Strip it to that."

"When lights go out my hands buzz and I taste metal," she said. "Numbers are the only thing that turns the volume down."

He set the notebook down, slower this time. "Then we use numbers for reporting, not punishment. Timestamp meds. Log pain. Cap replays. No self-fines, no extra drills."

"Then say that. Don't say `intervene` like I'm a case file."

"I'm saying it." He tapped the table twice, once for each phrase. "You're chasing a forty-three-second file until two in the morning. You're calling that recovery."

"Yesterday was six-fifteen target, seven-twenty-three actual. Cap was ten, I hit eighteen. Shin lit up at five by round two. Blank space goes feral on me, so I'm keeping structure through intake week."

"One missed block isn't failure," he said. He tapped `voicemail loops` with one finger. "This line is a hazard. We flag it first with Tess."

"Then your overtime is a hazard too." She turned the notebook and showed him the same morning entry: replay count 18, meds missed by sixty-eight minutes, drills cut at two rounds, shin ache five during holds. "Depot plus app leaves me in this spiral."

"At intake, I'm showing Tess this page," Anika said. "The real one. No cleanup, no pretending."

He held her gaze a second longer than usual. "Good. She needs raw numbers, not a cleaned-up version."

"And I'm not deleting Amma's voicemail."

"I'm not asking you to delete it." He took out his phone, opened the app-driver screen, and toggled himself offline. "I'm saying we stop pretending it is neutral."

"Then stop stacking jobs every night."

"I can't drop depot overtime." He rubbed his thumb over the cracked edge of his phone case. "I can drop app runs through intake week. After shift, I come home and stay home."

"Say it like a rule."

"No app runs this week. Depot only." His voice stayed low but did not waver. "I clock out, I come straight back, and you tell Tess the actual replay count, even if it makes me look like I missed everything."

"I will," she said. "If I freeze, you say the number in the room."

"Deal."

That night, Anika sat cross-legged on top of her blankets with the old phone in her lap and the notebook open beside her. In the kitchen Ravi's chair scraped once, then stayed put; no keys, no stairwell, no second shift.

She pressed play.

Maya said: middle shelf, spoon of water, mustard seeds, stubborn tomorrow, I love you.

Anika whispered each line in perfect time, her mouth shaping the words before the speaker did. By loop nine she could feel her pulse settle. By loop ten she had sweat cooling along her spine even with the fan on high.

She stopped at ten, waited thirty seconds, and restarted anyway.

At 12:34 a.m., she wrote:

Night 7
sleep: not yet
replay count: 11
panic peak: 7/10 then 4/10
shin ache at rest: 3/10
PT disclosure: yes

She tore the page out, folded it once, and slid it into the gray clinic folder by the door. Across the top she wrote: DO NOT ROUND DOWN.

Then, on a fresh page, on a line by itself:

I can say her exactly, and it still isn't enough.

She closed the notebook, set the phone face down, and lay back without turning the lamp off. Light pooled on the ceiling. In the next room Ravi coughed once, rolled in bed, went quiet.

Anika listened for sleep and found only the familiar minute-click of Maya's watch against her wrist, counting forward whether she wanted it to or not.

Before dawn she opened Maya's half-used notebook again, ruled a clean page into boxes for pain, sleep, swelling, minutes standing, and minutes pretending not to limp, then tucked it into her backpack beside the gray folder Ravi had left ready by the door for intake.


# Chapter 10

By morning, the clinic was three subway stops away and half a block up from Roosevelt Avenue, over a laundromat that vented hot detergent breath onto the sidewalk. The morning was wet and warm, the kind of early-summer air that stuck to Anika's right shin before she even got off the train. Every step up the stairs made the bone throb in a thin bright line, like somebody had slipped a heated wire under her skin and pulled it tight. Ravi walked ahead with a gray folder tucked under his arm, hospital imaging reports in plastic sleeves, insurance letters folded and refolded until the creases looked polished. He kept glancing at his watch and then at her feet, counting how evenly she landed. He stayed on logistics. "You ate?" When she nodded, he followed with, "Protein too?" He treated meals as one more strap on a brace.
