The door said KEATING SPORTS REHAB in white vinyl. Under it, a second sign said RETURN-TO-PLAY COMPLIANCE IS A SAFETY REQUIREMENT, NOT A SUGGESTION. The waiting room had six molded chairs and a water cooler that coughed every thirty seconds. A college sprinter in a knee brace scrolled through highlight clips with the sound on, then turned it off when a front-desk woman raised one eyebrow at him. Anika sat with her backpack between her shoes. She had Maya's digital watch on her wrist and Maya's half-used notebook in the bag, the page for today already ruled into boxes: pain, sleep, swelling, minutes standing, minutes pretending not to limp.

When Tess Keating came out, she carried a chipped steel mug that said DON'T SKIP THE BORING STUFF. She was broader through the shoulders than Anika expected, and a pale old scar curved from the edge of her left tank strap toward her collarbone. Her shoelaces were bright orange, double-knotted, mismatched by one eyelet, and she fixed the mismatch mid-step without looking down. "Anika Iyer?" she said. Her voice was low and steady, not soft. "Ravi, good to meet you in person. Come on back." She tapped her clipboard twice with a mechanical pencil before leading them through the gym.

The rehab room smelled of disinfectant, rubber mats, and the citrus cleaner they used on treadmills. Bands hung by color on a pegboard. A camera sat on a tripod facing a strip of blue tape on the floor for gait footage. Somewhere behind the treatment curtain a printer kept spitting single pages, each one followed by a frustrated little curse from somebody Anika could not see. She liked that sound more than she should have. Adults usually hid panic. Here someone let it out in plain English and then kept working.

Tess set the folder on a table. "Before we test anything, I need three numbers from you," she said to Anika. "Pain right now, worst pain in the last twenty-four hours, and how much you're scared of making it worse if you move. Zero to ten for all three." Anika said, "Two, four, one." She gave the numbers quickly so they would not be argued with. Tess looked at her for a beat that was not accusatory, just patient in a way that felt harder to dodge. "Try again," she said. "Use the number your body would give me, not the number you think keeps everyone calm." Ravi shifted his weight. "She just told you." Tess nodded at him but kept her eyes on Anika. "I heard her. I'm asking her to answer."

Anika stared at the seam where two mats met, a dark line full of chalk dust. "Three, six, seven," she said. The seven came out like a theft. Tess wrote it down without reaction. "Good. Shoes off. We'll start simple." She filmed Anika walking, first normal pace, then heel-to-toe, then single-leg balance. On the right side Anika's toes clawed at the mat and her shoulder tipped forward. Tess crouched by her ankle, fingers warm and dry against swollen tissue. "You're guarding before load, not just during load," she said. "That means your brain's already bracing for impact." Ravi leaned in. "She's careful. That's good." Tess stood. "Protective movement is useful short term. Long term it shifts force into places that are still healthy and turns one injury into two."

The first real test was a step-down from an eight-inch box. Left leg planted, right heel tapping the floor, ten reps each side. Anika flew through the left side. On the right she made it to rep five before her knee wobbled inward. By rep seven her jaw clenched so hard her molars squeaked. By rep eight a hot pulse climbed from shin to hip, and her right foot landed flat with a slap loud enough to turn the sprinter in the brace. Tess held up a hand. "Stop." Anika did one more rep anyway because stopping in the middle of a set made her throat close up. Tess stepped in and physically guided her down from the box. "That right there," she said, still calm, "is exactly what I mean."

"We're doing calf raises next. Fifteen on each side, ninety seconds rest between sets." Tess handed her the rail. Anika finished the first set and started again at twenty-three seconds without looking at the timer. Tess put a flat palm on the rail in front of her. "Rest means rest." Anika bounced on her toes. "I'm fine." Tess shook her head once. "No. You're scared of stillness. That isn't fine and it isn't your fault, but it is part of the rehab." Ravi exhaled through his nose. "Can we stay on the injury and not do pop psychology?" Tess took the pencil from behind her ear, clicked it once, and said, "This is the injury. If she can't tolerate thirty quiet seconds, she can't tolerate the load decisions we need her to make on court."

Ravi crossed his arms. "She's had six weeks down already. She's got regionals in July. We need a return date, not a lecture." Tess pulled up the gait video on a tablet and replayed it at half speed. Anika watched her own body flinch before her foot hit the ground. On screen her shoulders pitched forward and her right hip dropped a beat early, her whole frame braced for impact before impact arrived. Tess tapped the frame where her hip dropped. "Here's your return date answer," she said. "Phase one is no-impact conditioning and movement correction until this pattern changes. Phase two is controlled court footwork at capped volume. Phase three is monitored competitive load. You skip phase one and she lands back in imaging, maybe with both legs compromised." Ravi's voice stayed low enough that it sounded polite to anyone not standing three feet away. "You're being conservative because you've got legal exposure. We're the ones paying when she misses her window."

The word pay sat in the room heavier than the medicine balls by the wall. Anika could hear the collections voicemail from last week in her head, the same woman each month reading digits in a flat commuter cadence: thirty-eight thousand four hundred due, minimum payment due fifteenth, account status at risk. Tess did not pretend not to understand. "I know rankings are time-sensitive," she said. "I also know a repeat fracture can shut down an entire season, not a month. My job is to keep her playing longer than one desperate summer." Ravi gave a short laugh with no humor in it. "Your job ends at this room. Our life doesn't."

Tess set the tablet facedown. "I need five minutes with Anika alone. Standard for pediatric overuse cases." Ravi's head came up fast. "No." Tess held his stare. "It's standard. You can stand at the glass if that helps." He looked at Anika for a second, not asking but expecting. She kept her face still. After a long beat he stepped back, moved to the hallway, and stopped exactly where he could see through the half-open door.

Tess sat on a low bench so her eyes were level with Anika's. "I'm going to ask direct questions," she said. "You can say pass, but if you pass on everything I still have to document that." Anika nodded. The room sounded louder without Ravi breathing in it. "How often do you hide pain from your dad?" Tess asked. Anika picked at a frayed seam on her shorts. "Not hide. Just... round down." Tess waited. "How often?" "Most days before the fracture. Sometimes now." "Why?" Anika shrugged. "If I say five, everything stops. If everything stops, I can't catch up." Tess's expression did not soften, but it changed shape. "Catch up to what?" Anika looked at Maya's watch instead of Tess's face. The seconds flashed and reset and flashed. "To debt. To everyone else. To the part where people stop looking at me like broken glass."

Tess wrote a note. "Thank you for being honest. Next question. Are you sleeping?" "Enough." Tess lifted one eyebrow. "That sounded like another rounded number." Anika pressed her tongue against a sore spot in her cheek. "I sleep with my headphones in. I play my mom's voicemail until I pass out. If I wake up, I play it again." She waited for pity, then for a speech. Tess gave neither. "That's grief management, not sleep," she said. "It makes sense and it isn't sustainable. The program protocol includes counseling check-ins for exactly this pattern. It isn't punishment. It's injury prevention for the part of you we can't tape." Anika stared at the resistance bands, all those clean colors waiting to be pulled thin. "Do I have to talk a lot?" she asked. "You have to show up and answer truthfully enough that the counselor can do their job."

When Ravi came back in, Tess switched to public voice, precise and unadorned. "Here's the plan, in order. Pain log in the compliance app by nine p.m. every night." Ravi stayed standing, one hand on the back of the chair he refused to use. "If pain goes above four during work, we stop immediately and hold load for forty-eight hours." Anika felt her throat tighten at the word stop. "Two PT sessions a week with me, one counseling check-in with program behavioral health, no unsupervised impact work until phase two clearance." Ravi did not sit. "Counseling isn't in her orthopedic order." Tess pointed at the top page in the folder, where Return-to-Play Restriction Order was stamped above the imaging summary. "The order gives the medical team discretion on safety conditions. Sleep disruption and fear spikes are safety conditions. This is one." "This is mission creep." "This is what keeps her eligible."

Ravi stepped closer until his knuckles pressed the folder flat. "Strike counseling from her file and we'll finish your sessions. Leave it in, we walk tonight and I file a parent complaint before breakfast." Tess did not raise her voice. She pulled a one-page form from the clipboard, slid it across the table, and tapped the header: WITHDRAWAL AGAINST MEDICAL ADVICE. "If you walk, this goes in her case by 8 a.m.," she said. "Tournament medical sees it, marks noncompliance, and freezes her slot pending review. You're her parent. You can choose conflict. You can't choose immunity from policy." The air felt packed tight enough to bite. Anika looked from one face to the other and understood something she had not seen before: adults did not agree in private and then perform unity for children. Sometimes they fought in front of you because the rule lived somewhere bigger than the kitchen table.

The session ended with clamshells, glute bridges, and ten minutes on an assault bike with the seat set too low for comfort. Anika's shin did not spike above four, but fear did. Every rest interval felt like swallowing a stone. Tess wrote exercises on a printout and drew a column labeled "What I felt when I stopped." At the bottom she added her direct line number and then, in smaller handwriting, "If Ravi asks for extra, tell him I said no." She handed the page to Anika, not Ravi. "Bring this back with real numbers, not pretty ones."

On the subway home, Ravi kept scrolling through the protocol packet with his heel hammering a hard metronomic beat into the train floor. At Jackson Heights-Roosevelt he almost missed the stop because he was drafting an email with his thumbs moving so hard the phone clicked against its case. He did not show her the message, but she saw the subject line reflected in the train window when the lights hit it: REQUEST FOR ACCELERATED RETURN EXCEPTION - ANIYA IYER. He always misspelled her name when he was angry and typing fast. She wanted to correct him and did not.

The apartment was hot and smelled of curry leaves toasted too long in oil. Ravi turned on the box fan in the kitchen and called program operations before he even took off his shoes. Anika stood in the hall and listened to his half of the conversation while she unlaced her sneakers slowly, one loop at a time. "No, I understand policy," he said. "I'm asking for individualized judgment." Pause. "Because she's not average. Because we can't lose another month." He listened, then rubbed his eyes hard enough to leave red crescents under them. The voice on speaker was a woman named Nadia from compliance; she sounded young and exhausted and kept pausing to sip water before each sentence, her throat clearing between lines. "Mr. Iyer, I'm not dodging you," she said. "It's just... this case is in the auto queue now. If your daughter misses a report or breaks plan, the system slaps a lock on the slot and kicks it to medical review." Ravi said, "Then unlock it." Nadia gave a tired little laugh that did not mock him, just admitted the machine. "I wish I could. I can't touch that switch from ops, and my lead can't either." Anika stepped into the kitchen before she could talk herself out of it. "If I do every part exactly, does my slot stay live even if he files complaints?" she asked toward the speaker. Ravi snapped, "Anika, not now." Nadia was quiet for a beat, then answered her anyway. "If you comply, your slot stays provisional-active. Complaints don't erase medical terms." Ravi ended the call without saying goodbye.

At 6:42 p.m., the formal warning arrived. Ravi's laptop chimed once. He opened the message, read the first paragraph, and went very still. The subject line was in all caps: COMPLIANCE NOTICE - CHAPTER 8 FRACTURE CASE - ELIGIBILITY CONDITIONS. Anika watched over his shoulder from the doorway while the fan chopped the room into warm gusts.

> Any breach of prescribed rehabilitation protocol, including unsanctioned impact activity, missed daily pain reports, or refusal of scheduled behavioral-health check-ins, will trigger immediate provisional suspension of Junior Performance Program eligibility pending medical review.

Ravi read the line twice, fingers dug so hard into the trackpad the cursor jittered.

The next section opened in bullet points, each line appearing with a soft blue check as Ravi scrolled: daily pain report due by 9:00 p.m.; pain above four triggers a forty-eight-hour load hold; appeals had to be filed within one business day of a suspension flag. He read each line under his breath like he could wear the ink down. Anika tracked the deadlines against the red circle around the fifteenth on the wall calendar.

It copied Tess, program medical staff, and tournament administration. It ended with a plain signature block: *Nadia Serrano, Compliance Coordinator*. Under her name, in smaller text, was a line that made Anika blink: *Please allow up to one business day for response; I coach my son's Little League team from 5-7 p.m.*

Ravi shut the laptop halfway and opened it again. "This is intimidation," he said. "They're putting language in writing so they have cover later." Anika stepped into the kitchen. Her shin ached from standing, but she planted both feet and stayed there. "If we follow it, my slot stays live, right?" Ravi snapped, "It's not that simple." Then, softer, "These people only see a checklist." She looked at the open email and the boxes Tess had drawn on the exercise sheet and the red circle around the fifteenth on the wall calendar where the oncology payment was due. "Then the checklist still runs the slot," she said. "For now, that's enough."

He pulled up the compliance app and turned the screen toward her. "Enter pain as two tonight. You handled the session." Anika took the phone and typed four for peak pain, three for fear, and yes for sleep disruption. Her fingers trembled, but she left each number where it landed. Ravi saw the numbers and reached for the phone. "Keep it at two." The words came out sharp, then frightened. She held the phone against her chest. "This is what happened." He drew breath to argue. She beat him to it, voice smaller than she wanted but steady. "No. I'm not typing fake numbers again. It hit four at clinic, and my shin shot fire up to my hip on rep eight. If I shave it to two, the crack comes back and we're back in imaging. I'm done calling that bad luck."

For a long second he stared at her as if she had switched languages. The fan rattled. Somebody upstairs dragged furniture across the floor. "Send it," he said finally. Then he pointed at the screen. "From tomorrow, every entry happens with me in the room. You don't talk to Keating alone again. You don't send numbers behind my back." He sat down hard, opened his laptop, and started a new email to program medical with the subject line REQUEST FOR CASE REVIEW.

Heat rushed into Anika's face so fast her ears rang. The watchband bit into the damp skin of her wrist, and for a second she heard Maya's voicemail voice in her head: write it down before anybody renames it. Because Ravi had just claimed the room, she opened the notebook on the counter, drew a hard line under the app entry, and printed in block letters: 6:49 P.M. - DAD WATCHING REQUIRED FOR ALL REPORTS.

Later, after dinner, Anika filled in Maya's notebook with the app numbers and the exercises Tess had given her while Ravi typed in the kitchen and did not turn on the TV. In the margin she added a new column: "Who said no." For years that column would have had one name in it, always his. Tonight it had two: Tess and Anika. She sat on the edge of her bed, put in one earbud, and did not play the voicemail. The apartment pipes knocked and hissed; a siren climbed and fell on Northern Boulevard; the fan in the kitchen clicked every ninth turn. Stillness was not quiet. It was a waiting room.

At 8:58 she submitted the second pain check Tess had asked for after evening exercises while Ravi stood in the doorway and watched her thumb hit send. The app flashed green: COMPLIANT. In the kitchen his laptop pinged as the draft went out to medical and compliance, contesting the counseling requirement and requesting provider reassignment. On the wall calendar, noon at hospital records was underlined twice; storage billing posted at midnight whether anyone slept or not.


# Chapter 11

The hospital looked smaller from the sidewalk than it had in every memory Anika carried. In memory it was only doors and machines and shoes squeaking at three in the morning. In daylight it was brick, a cracked ADA ramp, and a florist bucket of tired carnations by the pharmacy entrance. A banner near the revolving door said SUPPORT OUR CANCER WALK in cheerful orange letters that made her jaw lock.

Noon at hospital records had been underlined twice on the wall calendar. Last night the pain app had flashed COMPLIANT green at 8:58. By noon they were here anyway, because storage billing did not care who had slept.

Ravi checked the time before he checked her face. "Storage closes at five. Billing posts at midnight. We clear it now, we stop another month."

He always spoke in clocks when something scared him. Times, shifts, last trains, payment windows. Anika nodded and kept her right foot careful on the curb. Damp weather made her shin sting in a thin line from ankle to knee, a hot wire under skin. She had learned to walk without a limp on the good days and with a smaller limp on the bad ones.

Inside, the air smelled like sanitizer and steamed rice from somebody's lunch box. They took elevator C to basement records, where a woman behind thick plexiglass looked up from a half-finished Sudoku. Her badge said LIDIA CRUZ. She had silver nail polish chipped down to metal moons and a cartoon turtle clipped to her lanyard. A paper cup of fennel seeds sat by her keyboard; she popped one into her mouth between stamps.

"Locker two-fourteen?" Lidia asked, already reaching for the form. "Renewal date is today. If it's not empty by four-thirty, finance posts next month at midnight and won't reverse it. They never do."

Ravi slid over his ID, Maya's death certificate copy, and the letter they'd gotten three weeks ago and ignored until the red FINAL NOTICE text showed up yesterday. "We'll be out in an hour."

"Good," Lidia said. "I leave at four-thirty and I don't trust the night desk with paperwork. He loses everything." She stamped three copies and passed them through the slot. "You bring every item back through here for sign-out. No loose biohazard materials, no sharps, no meds without pharmacy review. I'm not babysitting a needle incident today."

Anika watched the stamp hit paper with the same rhythm as Ravi tapping his knee. Lidia caught her looking and softened by two degrees.

"You okay, kid?"

Anika made the small-voice answer she gave adults who might ask follow-up questions if she used more words. "Yeah."

Lidia did not push. She turned to Ravi. "And you, sir, don't rush her through grief because billing is ugly. I've got a son taking his road test today and I still know the world won't end if we breathe for five extra seconds."

Ravi gave a tight smile that showed no teeth. "Understood."

Lidia buzzed the side door open and pointed down a cinderblock corridor that smelled faintly of bleach and old rainwater. "Second left, cage lockers. Two-fourteen is top row. Cart's around the corner." She lowered her voice toward Anika again. "If you need a minute, take a minute. Paper can wait sixty seconds."

The cart had one wobbly wheel and a strip of blue tape around the handle. Ravi pushed it fast anyway, metal rattling against tile seams. They passed laundry bins, oxygen canisters chained to a wall, and a corkboard full of flyers: free blood-pressure screening, grief support Tuesdays, union meeting postponed. Anika kept reading words she did not need to read, because reading gave her eyes somewhere to go besides the end of the hall.

Locker 214 sat inside a chain-link cage with a padlock that looked newer than the rest of it. Ravi checked the number twice, then used the key from Lidia's envelope. The lock opened with a sound like someone clearing a throat.

He lifted the rolling door halfway, then all the way, and stopped.

The locker was not full the way closets at home had been full. It was arranged. Three banker boxes with Maya's precise block letters on tape: WORK, HOUSE, ANIKA. A black duffel. A folded hospital lab coat in clear plastic. Two reusable grocery bags. A yellow envelope thick as a paperback, tied shut with one of Maya's old black hair elastics.

Anika felt the back of her tongue go dry.

Ravi recovered first. "Okay. We sort by category. We keep legal documents and identity stuff. We discard expired supplies. We take anything personal. We don't open every piece of paper in this hallway."

He said we, but he moved like a foreman assigning labor to one employee. Anika pulled the box marked ANIKA toward her knees. Dust smeared onto her palms in soft gray arcs. Inside were practical things first, the kind adults called important and children learned to fear: copies of vaccination records; a folder with school login passwords in Maya's handwriting; old MetroCards rubber-banded together; a pediatrician printout about stress fractures with one paragraph highlighted; a zip pouch with insurance cards and an emergency contact sheet; a college savings statement that said account temporarily suspended for nonpayment.

Beneath that sat a lined notebook page folded into quarters: Grocery if chemo day goes wrong. It listed plain yogurt, ginger tea, salt crackers, detergent, black garbage bags, and frozen peas. At the bottom Maya had written, If no appetite, still make rice. Ravi will forget.

Anika pressed the page flat on her thigh until the fold lines whitened.

Ravi opened WORK and pulled out Maya's old ID badge, lab schedules, and a stack of oncology billing letters in window envelopes. He flipped each one by date, mouth moving as he did math. "$38,400 total. $6,400 past due. Current cycle is $640 due July 15, 11:59 p.m." He muttered "Fuck" under his breath, dug out his phone, and accepted a 10:30 p.m. airport run before the screen could dim. "At least these are copies."

"At least," Anika echoed without meaning it.

She lifted the yellow envelope last. Her own name was written across the front in Maya's handwriting, the way it looked on lunch notes and permission slips and one Post-it on the fridge that had survived six months because nobody wanted to touch it. Under her name were smaller labels in blue ink: 12. 13. 16. If needed sooner, open anyway.

Her chest gave one hard thud and then forgot rhythm.

"Dad." Her voice came out thinner than she wanted. "There are letters."

Ravi turned, saw the labels, and crossed the two steps between them so fast the cart wheel chirped. "Give those here."

"Why?"

"Because we don't know what's in them and we're standing in a hospital basement." He held out his hand. "I said give them to me."

The old command voice. Not loud, not dramatic. Just the assumption that the next movement in the room belonged to him.

Anika pulled the envelope to her chest. "They're for me."

"They're from your mother to this family." His hand stayed out, palm up, fingers trembling once before he curled them straight again. "I'll read first so we can handle this right."

She almost laughed, and the laugh scared her because it sounded brittle enough to cut. Handle this right had been the phrase for everything since Maya died. Handle this right meant no crying at school. Handle this right meant keep training. Handle this right meant do not add problems.

Ravi reached anyway. His thumb hooked under the elastic. Anika caught his wrist with both hands and held on.

"Don't," she said.

He froze. For half a second his eyes went blank with surprise, as if she had spoken in a language he had never heard. Then his jaw set.

"Anika. Let go."

"No."

A cart squeaked somewhere behind them. Footsteps approached, slowed, kept moving. No one stopped. Hospitals trained people to hear a lot and look away.

Ravi lowered his voice. "Do not do this here."

She hated that the phrase worked on her body even when she disagreed. Her grip loosened by instinct. He felt it and tried to take the envelope in one quick pull.

The paper tore at one corner.

Anika jerked it back, stumbled against the shelf, and the black duffel tumbled open at her feet. Out slid a cheap silver digital recorder, the kind with a tiny speaker and rubber buttons. A label on masking tape read VOICE NOTES - MAYA.

Neither of them moved for two breaths.

Then Anika crouched and picked it up. Her hands shook enough that she hit PLAY twice before it clicked.

Static first. Then Maya's voice, thinner than memory but unmistakable, threaded through the metal speaker.

"Testing. If this is too soft, tell me and I'll yell at this stupid machine. Ravi, don't roll your eyes, I can hear paper rustling."

Ravi's face changed at the sound of his own name. The fight drained out of his shoulders and left something smaller and meaner behind: fear with nowhere to go.

The recording continued. "If you two are hearing this, then either you found the locker or Lidia threatened to mail it to you with glitter. She said she'd do that. I believed her." A soft cough. "Okay. Practical notes first because that's who I am. Work folder has my lab pension forms and the account number. House folder has copies of utility autopay and the landlord contact. Check the red notebook for passwords because I never trusted cloud storage."

Anika swallowed. Maya always did logistics before feelings, like setting a table before asking hard questions.

"Now the part you'll both try to skip," Maya said. "Ravi, if you're listening alone, don't. Put this on speaker."

Ravi took one step toward Anika. "Turn it off. Not in here."

She backed up until the shelf pressed into her spine and turned the volume up one notch.

"I can hear that basement in my head," Maya's voice said, roughened by another cough. "The bleach smell that sticks in your throat. The cart wheel that screams on tile. You're both going to want rules because rules feel cleaner than fear. Don't make her body pay for that. If her shin hurts, sit down and ask twice. If she wants squash, back her. If she needs a month where she hates the racket, let that be true too. I didn't fight this hard so she could live one narrow life."

Ravi's hand shot out. This time he did not ask. He grabbed the recorder and hit stop so hard the button squealed.

Silence slammed down.

"That was enough," he said.

"It wasn't finished."

"It was enough for a hallway with strangers." He shoved the recorder into the duffel and zipped it closed. "We're taking everything home. We'll sort there."

"You're taking everything." Her throat burned. "You're deciding again."

"I'm trying to keep this from becoming a scene."

"It already is one."

His eyes flicked to the corridor, to the clock above the exit sign, back to her. He grabbed for the yellow envelope again. This time she had expected it. She slid one letter out in a single practiced motion she did not know she possessed, tucked it beneath the elastic strap of her PT binder, and shoved the rest of the bundle toward him before he could see what she had done.

"Fine," she said. "Take them."

Ravi stared at her, suspicious, breathing through his mouth. Then he snatched the envelope, checked the torn corner, and held it against his chest as if paper could bolt.

"Cart," he said.

The pronoun had collapsed. They had separate jobs now.

They loaded the boxes in stiff quiet. Practical documents, intimate scraps, grief sorted by cardboard and tape. Ravi moved like he was late for a shift. Anika moved like each object might detonate if she set it down wrong. The lab coat smelled faintly of detergent and iodine. She folded it once more, because Maya would have.

When they rolled back to the desk, Lidia glanced up, took one look at both of their faces, and pushed the sign-out sheet forward without commentary.

"All items removed?" she asked.

"Yes," Ravi said.

Lidia checked the form. "Locker closed effective today. No July charge." She reached under the desk and handed Anika a wrapped peppermint. "For the elevator. Tastes less like panic than basement air."

Anika nodded thanks and put the candy in her pocket unopened.

Outside, humidity pressed against her face, wet and heavy. Ravi hailed a rideshare instead of taking the bus because he had already traded the saved transfer time for the 10:30 airport run. In the back seat he kept the yellow envelope under his forearm and stared out the window, tracking traffic lights and never looking at her.

Anika kept her PT binder on her lap. The hidden letter sat against her thigh, edges sharp through thin cardstock. Every time the car braked she felt it shift and had to resist checking that it was still there.

Queens moved by in familiar fragments: halal cart smoke, fruit stands misting oranges, a little girl in a sparkly soccer jersey dragging her shin guards on concrete. At Northern Boulevard the driver swore at traffic in a cheerful rhythm. Ravi apologized once for no reason and then stopped speaking entirely.

At a red light, his phone buzzed. Dispatch app. He looked, dismissed, looked again. Work messages stacked faster than he could ignore them. His left ankle bounced in short violent taps. Anika knew that rhythm too. It was his panic metronome.

When they reached home, he carried the boxes straight to the kitchen table and arranged them by size before taking off his shoes. Procedure first, always. The apartment smelled of old tea and the curry leaves he'd fried in oil that morning before they left. The overhead fan ticked every six seconds.

"If I don't put this in order, I'm gonna lose it," he said. "Documents first."

Anika set her binder on the counter and unzipped it slowly, making sure his back was turned. She slid the single stolen envelope inside Maya's old notebook, between two pages where she'd logged pain scores and hidden extra drills. The paper disappeared under her own handwriting. Numbers covering a different kind of evidence.

Ravi spread bills into piles: oncology statements stamped BALANCE $38,400.00, a red slip marked PAST DUE $6,400.00, and a one-page notice that read $640.00 DUE JULY 15 - 11:59 P.M. He kept restacking the same three envelopes until the corners curled under his thumb. "Six-forty by the fifteenth or it rolls straight to collections. I keep thinking if I stack this right, nothing else falls apart."

That was what grief sounded like in his mouth: triage.

"Dad," Anika said.

"One second."

"I want my letter."

He did not look up. "You'll get all of them after I've reviewed."

"Why'd you get to read my letter first?"

That made him stop. He flattened one bill with his palm until it crinkled. "Because I'm your parent. Because your mother's voice is going to hit you like a truck and then I'm the one who has to catch whatever happens after."

"You didn't catch me when I broke my leg." The words came out before she chose them. "You told me to finish the match."

His expression emptied out; a pulse jumped at his jaw hinge and kept jumping. "Stress fracture, not break."

"That isn't better."

"It's different."

"That form didn't make it hurt less."

He shut his eyes briefly and pressed thumb and forefinger to the bridge of his nose. "Anika, please. I can't do this fight and this paperwork at the same time."

The contraction came out ragged anyway. She could feel him trying not to split.

"Then stop doing paperwork for one minute," she said.

He opened the yellow envelope with the same care he'd use on a court schedule from a national coach. He read the first line of one letter, then the second, and something in his throat jerked. He set the page down as if it had heat.

"What did she say?" Anika asked.

"Not now."

"You don't get to decide now for everything."

"I said not now."

His voice rose half a notch. The fan ticked. A siren passed three blocks away. Anika felt the old reflex to shrink into compliance climb her spine, and she hated that it still knew the route.

She went to the bathroom, locked the door, sat on the closed toilet lid, and took out the envelope she had hidden.

The front said, in Maya's handwriting: For Anika - open when winning starts to feel like breathing and everything else feels optional.

Her fingers went cold.

Inside was three pages, blue ink fading where the pen had dragged. The first line made her laugh once, sharp and painful.

Kanna, if you've found this on schedule, I'm impressed and mildly afraid.

Anika snorted, then clamped a hand over her mouth when a pan clinked in the kitchen. She slid off the toilet lid onto the bathmat, knees to chest, and kept reading.

You might still leave wet towels on your bed. You might be taller than me, which feels rude. I can't predict your height, your haircut, or whether you finally admitted that mango pickle is better than ketchup.

After a death, adults get weird about control. We label leftovers, stack bills by due date, and act like a clean checklist can keep the next disaster outside the door.

Your father does this when he's scared. I do it too, with spreadsheets and labeled containers and three reminders to buy detergent. It's fear in good handwriting, not holiness.

The line about labeled containers pulled a memory of Maya lining up pill bottles by refill date on the kitchen counter, cursing at her own handwriting. Anika wiped her eyes with the heel of her hand, flattened page two against her thigh, and kept going.

If squash still gives you joy, I hope you play with your whole body and learn the corners, patience, and how to lose without making it your identity. If squash turns into the only room where you're allowed to breathe, that's information, not failure, and being eleven somewhere else is not betrayal.

Anika checked the bathroom lock again. She had planned to skim and hide the letter before Ravi knocked. Instead she turned the page with both thumbs and read faster.

You're not my memorial project. You're not the oncology payment plan with a racket in your hand. You don't owe me success because I died.

When I was twenty-three, I skipped your aunt's college dance show to finish charting and called that discipline for years. What I remember now is the empty folding chair with my name taped on it and Priya saving me a samosa that went cold.

Her chest tightened hard enough that she had to breathe through her nose in counts of four, the way Tess taught for flare pain. When the dizziness passed, she braced the page on the sink and read the part she had been avoiding.

Choose a life that's wider than winning. Wider means messy, ordinary, embarrassing, funny, late, inconsistent, alive.

Your father may hear accusation in this. He may hear relief. He may hear a bill he can't pay. That's his hearing, shaped by fear and invoices, and yours does not have to match it.

I'm not writing a verdict from the grave. I'm writing this because both of us loved you while scared, and scared people grab too hard.

Anika pressed her forehead to the door and listened to Ravi open the silverware drawer and shut it too hard. She wanted to stop there. Instead she turned to the last paragraph.

I love the way you count footsteps in strange buildings so you can find your way back. I count this too: one body, one childhood, one life; none of them are meant to be spent proving you can endure more than everyone else.

I'm still your mother even in paper.

Love,
Amma

By the time she finished page three, Anika's legs had gone numb from how tightly she held herself on the bathmat. She unfolded slowly, forehead against the cool bathroom door, and listened to kitchen sounds through wood: paper shuffling, drawer opening, kettle filled and not turned on.

Her throat hurt in a place that had nothing to do with crying.

She pictured Maya in the apartment kitchen, hair tied up with the same black elastic that had bound the envelope, arguing about detergent brands and bus schedules and whether eleven was too young for coffee sips. Not sainted. Not soft focus. Real Maya, who once swore at an insurance chatbot for thirty straight seconds and then laughed so hard she had to sit on the floor.

More than scoreboards and brackets.

The phrase lodged under her ribs, splinter-deep.

When she came out, Ravi was standing at the sink with both hands on the counter, shoulders lifted toward his ears. He had not changed out of his hospital shoes. Three opened letters lay beside the stove, face down.

"I'm making tea," he said without turning.

"Okay."

He finally looked at her. His eyes were red but dry. "Give me the one you took."

So he had seen after all.

Anika leaned against the hallway wall and crossed her arms over the binder she still held. "No."

"Anika."

"No."

"I'm not trying to punish you."

"Then don't check me like a backpack at school."

He gave a short, disbelieving laugh. "A backpack?"

"'Cause you unzip everything first. Lunch money, school forms, PT sheets, even her letters."

His jaw worked once. "You think I want this job?"

"You keep taking it."

The kettle clicked as metal settled. Nobody moved to turn on the burner.

Ravi pulled out a chair and sat down hard. For a second age hit him all at once: older than forty-four, older than widower, older than father. He sat with his shoulders pitched forward, breath high in his chest, too proud to check whether he'd split himself open.

"You know what that recorder said before you hit play?" he asked.

She shook her head.

"I listened once in the parking lot, the day she made it. I sat there with the engine on because I couldn't go up yet." He rubbed his left ankle, thumb grinding the old stiff spot. "She told me, 'Don't confuse urgency with love.' I wanted to throw the recorder into a storm drain."

He tapped one of the face-down letters. "And this line in the one I opened, about choosing a life bigger than trophies."

Anika's grip tightened on the binder. "I read that line."

"You hear freedom," he said. "I hear accusation. I hear that I narrowed your life till all you could do was perform."

"I hear breathing room," she said. "I hear Amma saying I'm not a debt payment with a racket."

Anika waited.

"Do you know what urgency is to me?" he said. "It's my chest locking every time an unknown number lights up. It's collections calling while I'm on shift. It's that $640 notice due July fifteenth sitting on the table while dispatch blows up my phone because somebody no-showed and overtime is grocery money. It's you on one leg saying you're fine when you're not fine."

He inhaled, then lost the next sentence halfway through. "It's this apartment after the funeral. One toothbrush gone, one plate still in the rack, and me doing math at midnight so the lights stay on."

His voice cracked hard. He pressed his knuckles to his mouth, then forced the words out anyway. "So yes, I grab the gate. I grab all of it. Because if I miss one thing, if I let one thing through, I don't know how to not lose you too."

The sentence sat between them, raw and unswept, an exposed wire humming in kitchen light.

Anika felt the old pity reflex rise with the old fear reflex, both trying to make her surrender the letter and call it peace. She did not surrender.

"I still get to keep one," she said.

Ravi looked at the binder, then at her face. His jaw ticked; his fingers flexed once and stopped. To Anika it read as a fast cost check now that rules outside the apartment existed: PT protocols, eligibility warnings, adults who had started saying no to him in rooms where she could hear.

He nodded once. The nod looked like swallowing glass.

"One," he said.

"One."

He stood, turned on the kettle, and measured tea leaves with meticulous care. Anika watched his hands because watching hands was easier than watching eyes. They did not shake while they worked.

Dinner was leftover lemon rice reheated in a skillet and eggs scrambled too dry because both of them forgot the stove for ten seconds too long. They sat at opposite ends of the table with letters stacked between them like a third person nobody trusted.

"How's the leg after PT?" Ravi asked, already reaching for the top envelope with oily fingers.

Anika caught his wrist before he touched it. "Not with food on your hand."

His eyes flashed. "It's paper, not scripture."

"It's Amma's paper."

He pulled his arm free and started, "If we don't finish these tonight-"

"Then we don't," she said. She stood, took the stack, and set it on the counter under an upside-down colander like she was trapping a rat. Metal rang against laminate.

Ravi half-rose from his chair. The chair legs screeched. He looked at the covered letters, at her, then sat back down so hard his water glass jumped and spilled across the table. "Shit." He wiped the water in short angry strokes with the edge of his sleeve.

"No touching the letters while we eat," Anika said.

Ravi pressed a paper towel over the spill and wrung water from his sleeve. "Fine. Pain number."

"Three after PT. Two if I ice."

"Three means no stairs tonight and no extra drills." He shoved his plate away, then pulled it back. "I'm not negotiating that."

"I'm not negotiating my letter either."

He scrubbed a hand over his face and tried a softer voice. "I can drop the 10:30 airport run. We can read one together and be done fighting for today."

She shook her head. "Don't buy peace with a shift. I want the rule, not a favor."

His mouth twisted. "If I drop that run, we miss the $640 due on July fifteenth and eat another fee."

"Then drive," she said. "But we still stop at one letter tonight."

He carried the soaked towels to the sink, washed his hands this time, and came back without reaching for the colander-covered stack.

After dishes, he pulled a legal pad from the WORK box and wrote tomorrow's schedule in block capitals.

6:00 wake
6:30 mobility
8:15 bus
4:00 PT exercises
6:00 dinner

He slid the pad toward her with two fingers. "We'll keep structure."

Anika read it, then drew one line beneath his list and added her own in smaller print.

4:30 call Zoe
7:30 no training talk

Ravi's mouth tightened. "No training talk isn't practical."

"It's thirty minutes."

"We need to review compliance before Thursday."

"Then do it at eight."

He looked as if he wanted to argue to exhaustion, to outlast her on principle, to turn the night into a lecture where he could win by staying upright longer than an eleven-year-old. Instead he set the pen down.

"Fine."

The word landed cold, more forecast than promise.

She took the pad, tore off the page, and taped it to the fridge under chapter schedules and utility reminders and a magnet shaped like a mango that Maya had stolen from a community health fair years ago. A shared document with contested handwriting.

In her room, Anika tucked Maya's letter into the notebook behind a page of pain scores and rep counts. She added a new line under today's date.

Locker 214 cleared before fee renewal.
One letter kept.
Instruction: wider.

She stared at the word until the letters stopped looking familiar.

From the kitchen came the sounds of Ravi cleaning an already clean counter, cabinet doors opening and closing, the kettle refilled for morning. Every noise said movement without resolution.

At ten, he knocked once on her doorframe.

"Lights," he said.

"In a minute."

He stood there long enough for her to feel his presence at her back, then walked away without repeating the order.

Anika turned off the lamp and lay in the dark with her shin propped on a rolled towel the way Tess had shown her. The apartment settled around her: fridge hum, neighbor's television, one laugh in the hallway, Ravi's footsteps crossing the kitchen and stopping by the sink where Maya used to stand.

She mouthed, "Choose a life that's wider than winning," then set Tess's clinic reminder before she could bargain the line into something safer.

In the next room Ravi moved a chair across tile and then did nothing for a long time. No lecture came through the wall. No revised schedule slid under her door. Just the thick, unresolved silence of two people still in the same home and no longer pretending obedience was the same as peace.

Before sleep took her, she slid Maya's notebook into her backpack, tucked Maya's watch into the front pocket, and draped the ice sleeve over the zipper so she wouldn't skip Tess in the morning.


# Chapter 12

By noon, the reminder she'd set before sleep had done its job and carried her to Tess Keating's clinic, where July air sat on the sidewalk like wet cloth. A produce truck idled at the curb and made the bus-stop bench vibrate under Anika's knees. She had an ice sleeve Velcroed around her right shin and Maya's watch damp against her wrist. Tess stood in the doorway with a clipboard tucked to her ribs while Ravi's voice came through Anika's phone on speaker from a depot break room that sounded like metal doors and vending machines.

"I'm saying the same thing I wrote," Tess said. "No impact outside the posted plan. If either of you freelances, she loses eligibility."

Ravi's voice crackled. "I'm not freelancing. I'm trying to keep her from falling behind."

"Then stop bargaining with anatomy," Tess said. "Go home tonight, set terms in writing, and put one weekly rest day on the fridge where both of you can see it."

Anika tightened the bottle cap in her hand until the ridges bit her skin. "We'll do it tonight."

"You'll do more than do it." Tess pointed her pen at the phone screen and then at Anika. "You'll follow it when it costs you something."

By the time Ravi came in from the depot that evening, the kitchen table looked like school instead of dinner. Anika had pushed the dented steel plates to one side and laid out three things in a straight line: Tess's return-to-play printout with the clinic logo, a blank July calendar she had copied by hand because the printer had died in May, and Maya's half-used notebook opened to a clean page. The fan in the window dragged humid air through the room and spread the smell of bus diesel off Ravi's shirt across mustard seed and old tea.

He dropped his keys in the blue bowl and studied the table like it had accused him of something.

"You didn't eat?" he asked.

"I ate," she said. "Can we do this first?"

He kept standing. "Do what first?"

"Terms." She pointed at the chair across from her. "Not hallway yelling. Actual terms. Twenty minutes each side. Then we write what we agreed."

Ravi gave a short laugh that had no amusement in it. "Each side? You sound like a court hearing."

"Maybe that's better than what we do now. Sit down."

He took the chair with his work bag still strapped across his chest, as if he planned to bolt. The zipper had split near the handle weeks ago. She watched his thumb worry that torn seam while he read Tess's protocol line by line, lips moving on certain words: no-impact progression, load cap, immediate symptom disclosure.

"You copied this wrong," he said. "It says gradual return. It doesn't say tiny return."

"It says no jumping volume."

"Volume is how you get fit enough to keep your place."

Anika flipped to the notebook page she had ruled with a geometry ruler. She had titled two columns in block letters: `Non-negotiable` and `What happens if broken`.

Ravi looked at the heading and his shoulders went up. "What happens if broken? Are you giving me punishments now?"

"I'm writing consequences so we don't pretend we forgot." She checked the watch on her wrist, Maya's old digital rectangle with a scratched face. "I start. You don't interrupt. Then you go."

"Anika."

"You said in the clinic we'd talk when we got home. We're home."

He shut his mouth. The kitchen clock clicked loud enough to count with.

She read from the page because her throat went tight if she looked at him. "First: one full rest day every week. Full means no court, no running stairs, no ghosting in the hallway, no 'light touch' disguised as drills. Second: school time is school time. No leaving before last period unless it's an official tournament day with paperwork. No texting me six times during math about split-step videos. Third: pain has to be said immediately. Not after session, not at night. If my shin spikes, I say it right there and we stop arguing about whether it counts."

Ravi leaned back and rubbed his left ankle, that old habit when he was cornered. "Who said anything about arguing?"

She looked at him then. "You did. Every time."

He inhaled through his nose and held it. "You have this tone from Priya now."

"This tone is from me."

The fan coughed and slowed. Somewhere above them, the upstairs neighbor dragged furniture across the floor, heavy legs thudding in uneven bursts.

Ravi tapped the protocol sheet with one finger. "You want me to talk?"

She nodded.

"Fine. Here's reality. You're already behind after six weeks. Cora isn't doing yoga and journaling while you heal. She's training. Every kid in that pool is training. You take one full day off, plus school restrictions, plus early stop for every ache, and you get buried. Rankings don't wait. Brackets don't care that we had a bad spring." He pointed toward the pile of envelopes by the microwave, the ones with red windows and final notice bands. "Bills definitely don't care."

The word bills was never just one word in this apartment. It was the oncology number she had seen in itemized rows on the hardship forms. The total was thirty-eight thousand four hundred. It was the utility warning they had folded three times and tucked under a coupon circular like paper could be shamed into silence. Anika felt the old arithmetic start in the back of her head, the part that translated every hour into possible rescue.

Ravi kept going. "You think I like pushing? You think I enjoy being the bad guy? We don't have private-club money. We don't have backups. We have your game and my shifts. That's it."

She let him finish. She did not let the numbers finish her.

"Are you done?" she asked.

"No, I'm not done."

"Then do your twenty minutes. But answer this first." She slid the notebook toward him and tapped the blank line under `What happens if broken`. "If we break protocol and I get suspended, what do we have?"

A cheek muscle twitched. "We don't break protocol."

"We almost did last week in the clinic parking lot."

"I was talking."

"You were negotiating my bone." Her voice shook, and she hated that he would hear it, but she did not stop. "My right shin is still hot after stairs. Tess wrote it in front of you. You heard her say noncompliance means suspension. You heard it." She put her palm flat over the notebook page to hide the tremor. "I'm not doing secret extras. I'm not lying again."

Ravi's eyes cut to the watch on her wrist. "You're talking like you're alone in this."

"I'm talking like I'm inside this."

Silence sat down between them. It was not the old silence that followed shouting and slammed doors. This one had shape. It had both of them in it.

Ravi unstrapped his bag and set it on the floor. He folded the protocol sheet once, then unfolded it, aligning corners too carefully.

"One rest day," he said finally. "Saturday."

Anika wrote it down.

The pen scratched louder than it should have. Sweat collected under Maya's watchband, and a thin pulse kept tapping along the sore ridge of her right shin.

"School," he said. "You attend full day unless tournament or medical appointment. If a coach asks, we say no."

She wrote that too, then looked up. "If you text during class, I won't answer."

"Fine."

"Not fine. Say it clearly."

His mouth tightened. "I won't text you during class unless it's urgent."

She wrote: `No training texts during class.`

Tiny was one of his favorite escape words, loose enough to stretch around anything. If she let it sit undefined, he'd pry every locked rule back open by Thursday.

He rubbed his forehead with two fingers, smearing depot grime across his skin. "Pain disclosure... immediate. But you don't weaponize tiny sensations to skip hard work."

"Define tiny."

"You know what I mean."

"No. Define it."

He gave her a look that used to end discussions. It did not end this one. He looked away first.

"If pain changes your movement," he said. "If you start compensating, limping, changing lunge depth. You report. We stop and assess."

"And if you disagree with my pain report?"

He swallowed. "Then we stop anyway and call Tess."

She wrote that exactly.

Ravi reached for the pen. "Add this. We keep weekly volume targets within her limits. I need something measurable."

"Within whose limits?"

"Within protocol limits," he snapped. "Don't do that cross-exam thing with me."

"Then say protocol limits." She passed him the pen.

He printed in careful block letters, each stroke pressed hard enough to emboss the paper underneath: `All volume targets must remain within PT protocol caps.`

He drew a line under it before handing the pen back.

Anika read the list aloud from top to bottom, as if she was reading directions in science lab and any skipped step could blow glass apart.

"One full rest day every Saturday. School stays whole-day, no early pull-outs. I report pain right away; if we argue, we stop and call Tess. Volume stays under PT cap. No training texts during class." She took a breath. "If either of us breaks it, court dies that day and tomorrow's canceled."

Ravi's head came up. "Canceled by who?"

"By both of us."

"That's not enforceable."

"It's enforceable in this kitchen."

He stared at her long enough that she could hear water begin to hiss in the pipes, someone's shower kicking on. Then he gave one short nod.

"Fine," he said.

She hated that this small word could feel like winning and grief at the same time.

"Calendar," she said.

She slid the hand-drawn month grid to the center. Ravi leaned in despite himself. Planning language was his native language. She watched him choose a blue pen instead of black, a detail he would insist meant nothing.

Together they blocked the week. Monday after school: PT clinic, three subway stops, no court. Tuesday and Thursday: forty-five minute controlled court sessions at Queens rec, monitored movement only. Wednesday: homework first, mobility work at home, no impact. Friday: school plus short technical feed if pain-free. Saturday in thick letters: `REST - NO EXCEPTIONS`. Sunday: capped practice and video review.

Ravi added transit times in tiny print between blocks. `Q32 bus 3:20.` `F train backup.` `Bring tape.` He started to write `stairs` under Tuesday commute and stopped when Anika's pen touched the paper beside his.

"No hidden conditioning," she said.

"I know what no means."

"You know how to rename it."

His nostrils flared. For a moment she thought the room would flip back to the old pattern. Then he capped the pen and pushed it away from himself.

"Put it up," he said.

Anika stood on the chair and taped the calendar to the fridge with two magnets: the cracked Ganesh magnet Maya had bought from a temple fundraiser, and a Midtown club magnet with gold lettering that looked expensive even when it held coupons and overdue notices. The paper sat above eye level for Anika and exactly eye level for Ravi. Every morning and every night, they would both have to look at it.

She climbed down. Ravi kept his eyes on Saturday's `REST - NO EXCEPTIONS` block long enough to make his silence obvious, then reached for the sink and shook the colander once, rice slapping wet against steel.

"Eat," he said quietly. "Then ice ten minutes."

"It's not hot now."

"Still. We treat the warning, not just the heat."

"Okay. Timer on the counter so it doesn't turn into twenty."

The word okay felt less like surrender than it had two months ago.

When she went to brush her teeth, she passed the living room and saw him still in front of the fridge, arms folded, reading Tuesday as if he could force a second line to appear beneath it.

---

By Thursday the heat had thickened enough to stick her shirt to her back before warm-up. Queens rec was louder in summer. The HVAC unit above Court Two rattled like a coin in a tin can, and every hard drive hit the side wall came back with a doubled echo because the door seals had started peeling.

Anika sat on the wooden bench and retied her right shoe twice. The knot kept landing wrong against the tender ridge on her shin. She could feel Ravi watching from the hallway glass where parents gathered with tote bags and coffee cups and opinions.

Jonah was crouched by his ball bucket, wrapping fresh tape around his wrist with the concentration of someone threading a needle on a moving bus. He had a dented lunch tin next to him, decorated with faded cartoon dolphins. Inside were mints, spare tape, and a nicotine patch in a foil sleeve he kept forgetting to throw away.

"You gonna play today or marry that shoelace?" he asked without looking up.

"Playing," Anika said.

"Good. I don't officiate weddings." He snapped the tape with his teeth, stood, and squinted at her legs. "How's the right side?"

"Baseline two."

"Speak human for me."

"Mild ache. No gait change."

He nodded once. "That's human enough."

Ravi stepped in from the hallway, already holding his notebook. "Cap's forty-five, hard stop," he said to Jonah, loud enough for the two younger kids on the next court and their mothers by the door. "Clock hits zero, session's done. If her movement changes, we shut it down and call Tess."

Jonah raised both eyebrows. "Historic day. Somebody log it."

"Log whatever you want," Ravi said. "Run the posted block."

Anika caught the brief glance between the men, years of quiet argument in half a second. Jonah clicked his timer and tossed her a warm-up ball.

"We start with controlled drives," he said. "Seventy percent pace. Hold your lines and skip hero shots."

The first five minutes felt wrong in the way everything healed felt wrong. Her body remembered panic speed and had to be pulled back by force. She hit too hard, ball jumping long off the back wall. Jonah did not yell. He just held up two fingers and said, "Softer hands," until she heard him.

Sweat crawled down her spine. On her watch, seconds stacked with cruel neatness. Every drill had an old ghost of itself beneath it, the version where Ravi would have added two more sets, then three, then one for character. She kept waiting for that familiar command, for volume to return like weather.

At minute nineteen, lunging to her forehand, a needle of pain flashed along the inside of her right shin. Not sharp enough to buckle, sharp enough to warn.

She straightened.

Jonah saw it first. "What changed?"

Her tongue stuck for half a beat. The old reflex arrived on schedule: hide, finish, confess later. She could almost feel the lie forming, thin and easy.

Ravi's voice came from behind the glass. "Anika?"

She wiped her face with the back of her wrist. "It's up a click," she said. "Right shin, inside line. I was at two, I'm at three."

The words tasted metallic, like biting foil.

Jonah stepped closer but kept his voice even. "Movement change?"

"I'm not limping. Push-off's sharper."

Ravi was through the door in two strides. "Heat shift?"

"Same as warm-up."

Ravi planted himself at the edge of the service box, notebook still open in his fist. Jonah didn't yield an inch. "We cut lunge depth and keep eyes on the right side," Jonah said. "If it jumps again or she compensates, we kill court. She calls every shift."

Ravi's jaw worked once. "Run it."

Jonah handed Anika a towel. "Good report. That's how athletes keep seasons." Then, almost as an afterthought, he dug a mint out of the dolphin tin and popped it into his mouth. "And maybe lungs."

They resumed with shorter ghosting patterns and controlled straight drives. They took no dives and made no desperate reaches. At minute thirty-four she wanted to speed up; at minute thirty-eight she almost did. Ravi rapped once on the glass and held up an open palm: steady.

By minute forty-five she was in the middle of a serve-return sequence, only four of ten cycles complete, when Jonah's timer screamed.

"Time," Jonah said.

"I'm in the set," Anika said. "Let me finish this one."

Ravi stepped through the door with her water bottle and towel. He did not hand either over yet. "Clock says stop. No extensions."

"Two minutes isn't an extension."

"Two minutes is still overtime."

Her grip tightened on the racquet handle until the tacky wrap bit into her palm. "I've finally got timing on the back wall."

"Good," Ravi said. "Bank it for Friday."

"If Friday gets canceled, I lose it."

He held her gaze, and she could see the old urge in him, the one that loved one more set more than sleep or sense. It flickered and settled.

"We're done," he said. "Pack."

She hated him for saying it. She loved him for saying it. Both felt dangerous.

They packed in silence. Jonah signed her practice card with a thick black marker and added `45:00 cap honored - drill incomplete` in neat all-caps. Then he slipped the marker back into the dolphin tin and muttered, mostly to himself, "Quitting cigarettes should've been easier than quitting bad coaching habits. Turns out nothing is easy." He nodded at Anika. "See you Friday if Tess clears it."

Outside, heat slammed into them from the sidewalk. Ravi unlocked his phone and started tapping before they reached the bus stop. Anika watched his thumb move fast, already drafting updates before the bus even turned the corner.

"Who are you messaging?" she asked.

"Schedule update," he said.

"To who?"

"Jonah. Tess. And me."

He held the screen out. New calendar entry: `Friday court tentative - pending AM pain report.` Beneath it, in smaller text he had started and not sent yet: `Optional mobility stairs if pain-free.`

Anika pointed at the second line.

"Delete that."

"It's mobility, not impact."

"It's hidden conditioning with nicer words. Delete it."

He bit the inside of his cheek. A bus wheezed up to the curb and exhaled hot brake air around their ankles.

"You're relentless," he muttered.

"You taught me that."

For one second she thought he would refuse, right there with strangers boarding around them and coins clinking in the fare box. Then he hit backspace, once, twice, erased the line, and showed her the blank space where it had been.

"Happy?" he asked.

"No," she said. "Clear."

The word scraped her throat on the way out. Clear meant she had to hold the line when it cost them money, court time, and whatever fragile peace they had bought on Tuesday.

"Good," she said. "Then Friday's canceled."

His head jerked toward her. "What?"

"You tried to sneak conditioning right after we posted terms. We wrote the consequence in your own handwriting. Session ends. Next day's court canceled."

"That line never got sent."

"You still tried it."

Ravi stepped into her lane and braced his forearm on the bus-door rail, not touching her but turning the curb into a narrow chute. "Don't do this on a sidewalk like I'm some stranger trying to grab you."

Her pulse punched up into her throat. She shifted back, felt the curb edge under her heel, and pain zipped hot up the inside of her right shin hard enough to blur the next breath. The bench rattled when her hand hit it. People were squeezing past them with backpacks and grocery bags, pretending not to look.

For a second her body tried to run the old script: back off, de-escalate, save the session. She hated how quickly that obedience came.

"Then move," she said, voice cracking anyway. "You're blocking me and arguing semantics. Say it plain. You tried to put stairs back in."

The bus driver leaned on the horn and shouted, "Board or clear the doorway." Ravi dropped his arm but did not step away. His jaw worked. "That court's prepaid. No refund after six."

"Then that's what this costs."

When he dropped his arm she didn't feel safe, only steadier. Relief and anger tangled tight in her chest, impossible to separate.

For a beat he looked like he might call her bluff. Instead he opened the rec booking app, canceled Court Two, and held up the red banner when it flashed: `Late cancellation confirmed. Credit not issued.`

Only then did he reopen the message draft and type with both thumbs: `Friday court canceled. I tried to add stairs after agreeing not to. No workaround session. Review with Tess before next impact.` He stopped on `I tried` as if the phrase had teeth.

Watching him type that sentence felt like watching him sign a debt in public. It didn't fix anything, but it made the cost impossible to pretend away.

"Read it," he said.

She read every word, then pointed to the address line. "Add me."

"You were standing here."

"Add me."

He added her contact, hit send, and shoved the phone into his pocket so hard the case smacked his hip.

On the ride home, he stood so she could sit by the window and keep her leg straight. At Roosevelt Avenue a man with grocery bags argued with the driver about a transfer slip. At 82nd Street two women got on carrying salon aprons that smelled like acetone and coconut oil. It was normal weekday noise. No one in that bus cared about pain scales or junior rankings. Anika found that comforting and insulting at the same time.

At home she iced her shin while Ravi made tea too strong and forgot sugar in both cups. He asked if she had finished her science worksheet in the same voice he used for weather reports. She said yes without looking up, and the question died there. They ate reheated lemon rice with pickle from the jar while the fan wobbled and his phone buzzed once with the no-credit receipt from the rec app.

After dishes, Anika opened the fridge for water and saw the calendar. Saturday still shouted `REST - NO EXCEPTIONS.` Tuesday and Thursday still had hard borders. Friday now had Ravi's updated handwriting in blue: `COURT CANCELED - REVIEW W/ TESS.`

She touched the paper edge. It had gone soft where steam from the rice pot hit it.

Ravi came up behind her with a roll of clear tape. "Corners are curling," he said. "We'll need a new sheet before August."

"It's still July," she said. "We're not even through this week."

His mouth twitched like he wanted to answer with a joke and could not find one.

He flattened each corner like he was sealing a wound: top left, top right, bottom left, bottom right. His fingers were rough and careful in the same motion.

"It'll stay," he said.

"Until when?"

"Until we replace it next month." He met her eyes in the reflection of the microwave door. "Not before."

Later, in bed, Anika listened to the apartment settle. Pipes knocked. A distant siren rose and fell. Someone laughed on the stoop below. Her shin throbbed dull and regular, less pain than message.

She heard Ravi in the kitchen after midnight, the fridge opening, paper rustling, magnet tapping once against metal. She got up and stood in the hall without turning on lights.

He was at the calendar with a pencil behind his ear, not writing, just holding it. The clock over the sink turned 12:07. The rec-app receipt sat faceup by the fruit bowl, `Credit not issued` glaring in thin red letters. He stared at Saturday's `REST - NO EXCEPTIONS` block like he could thread extra hours through it without anybody noticing.

He did not move until he felt her there.

"Couldn't sleep?" he asked.

"No. I heard the fridge and thought you were rewriting Saturday."

He looked down at the pencil, then set it on the counter. "I was looking for a loophole. I didn't find one."

They stood shoulder to shoulder in the weak refrigerator light, both looking at the same taped page, both pretending the paper was stronger than it was and that morning would leave it untouched.
When she finally slept again, Saturday had already started.


# Chapter 13

The first rest day began with mustard seeds popping in hot oil and the tiny metallic rattle of the kitchen window in its loose frame. Anika woke before the alarm anyway, because her body had learned to wake at five thirty and stay alert for commands, but the clock on Maya's old watch read 6:42 and no one had banged on her door. Humid light pushed through the curtain in bands. Somewhere in the building somebody was blasting old Hindi songs through a phone speaker, and somebody else was cursing at a jammed laundry cart in the hall.

She lay still and counted backward from sixty the way Tess had taught her when her shin flared at night. The right leg carried a deep bruise ache along the inside of the bone, swollen by the damp. It was honest pain instead of panic. On the fridge, the calendar corners still shone where Ravi had taped them down last night, and in blue marker the Saturday square said REST in his printed block letters. She had touched the word with the pad of her thumb before bed as if it could smear off.

In the kitchen Ravi was standing in his depot T-shirt and old track pants, stirring onions with his left hand and rolling his left ankle in tiny circles against the tile. The ankle circles were his morning tell. He only did them when sleep had gone bad. A stack of folded grocery bags sat by his elbow in perfect white triangles, ready for the drawer.

"You're up late," he said without looking at her. His voice was neutral, not clipped. "Sit. I'll plate." 

Anika sat. The wooden chair rocked once on the uneven leg, then settled. Curry leaves crackled in oil, tea had boiled past bitter, and Friday bleach still clung to the sink ring that never came all the way out.

"Did you message Zoe back?" Ravi asked. "You said she wanted library today."

That made Anika look up. "Yeah. Noon." 

"Good." He slid a plate toward her, two thin dosas and potato masala with too much turmeric. "Eat before it gets rubbery."

He poured chai into her steel cup, then his. No lectures about protein timing. No reminder to ice the shin. He asked if her math packet was finished. He asked if she needed money for glue sticks. He told her a bus had broken down near Woodside and he would have to cover an extra shift Monday unless Malik picked it up.

Ordinary, she told herself. This is ordinary.

She chewed slowly and let the grease coat her tongue. Last month she would have been calculating the calories while she ate, running the numbers beside rep counts in the margin of her notebook. Today the notebook stayed on her shelf. She had promised herself that. Rest day meant no court, no secret ghosting in her room, no compensating run on the stairs. She would go to the library with Zoe, return her borrowed graphic novel, maybe walk to the corner store and buy the tamarind candy Maya used to hide in the freezer behind peas.

Ravi drank half his tea in one pull, set the cup down, and wiped the rim with his thumb. His phone buzzed face-down near the salt tin. He did not reach for it.

"You sleep okay?" he asked.

Anika nodded. "Mostly."

"Nightmares?"

"No."

He nodded back, as if he had checked a box.

The phone buzzed again, then once more, a short-long-short pattern from a thread he had forgotten to mute. Anika watched his jaw tighten. He flipped the phone over in one movement, scanned the screen, and turned it face-down again.

She saw only three words before the glass went dark.

`Outside by 8:30?`

He stood. "Finish fast. You need to change." 

The words dropped into the kitchen like a glass hitting tile.

Anika put her fork down. "Change for what?"

Ravi opened the overhead cabinet and took down the small canister of new balls he saved for match prep. "Sparring. One hour. Maybe seventy-five minutes if pace is good."

She did not move. "Today is rest day." 

"This is not training volume. This is a live read. You need left-wall pressure reps and we got a slot."

He said we got, not I booked. The back of her neck went hot.

"You said Saturday is off," she said. "You wrote it."

"I wrote it before this chance opened. Listen to me. Nia from Staten Island is in Queens for cousins. Thirteen, top bracket, lefty, ugly tempo. You need this look."

He was already at the sink rinsing her water bottle, his hands moving fast, procedural. He set two electrolyte packets on the counter as if they had always belonged there.

Anika stared at them. She knew that silver brand. He only bought it for tournament days because each packet cost almost four dollars.

"You scheduled someone," she said.

"I confirmed someone."

"Without asking me."

"It's one hour."

"It's my rest day."

Ravi exhaled hard through his nose. "Don't start legal language at breakfast."

She stood so quickly the chair leg scraped and jumped. "It's not legal language. It's what we agreed." 

He pointed at her shin with the spoon handle. "And your leg looks good this week. Damp day, yes, but gait is clean. If we wait, the next lefty exposure is regionals. You want to walk in blind?"

Ravi's words clicked into their usual order - slot, ranking, debt - while the spoon ticked against the counter between phrases. The sound hit her ribs before the meaning did. A hum started low in her chest.

"Not today," she said. "No sparring."

His head snapped up. "No what?"

"No sparring on a rest day. We agreed." 

"Anika."

"Don't do command voice. It's still no."

She heard the thinness in her own voice and hated it. She swallowed and felt the tea sit heavy in her throat.

Ravi set the spoon down with deliberate care. "Go put on your shoes. We're leaving in twelve minutes."

"I'm not going." 

He looked at her a long second, then smiled with only one side of his mouth, the smile he used with dispatch drivers when they asked for impossible coverage. "You're tired. I get it. This is exactly why we maintain routine. You'll feel better once we start moving."

"I'm not tired. I said no."

"Don't confuse discomfort with injury." 

"I didn't say injury. I said no. I said I need the day we agreed on."

He was still calm, but the calm had edges. He reached for the calendar on the fridge, the one with color blocks for PT, school, and training windows, and tapped the Saturday square.

"Rest means no impact load," he said. "It doesn't mean lying in bed all day pretending life paused for us."

"You know that's not what I meant." 

"Then stop acting like I ambushed you. I'm adjusting to real conditions. That's coaching and that's parenting."

The hum in her ribs became a throb in her jaw. She could hear the downstairs neighbor's pressure cooker whistle through the floor, three sharp bursts, and then the kitchen was all heart noise and refrigerator motor.

"You hid it," she said. "You waited until I finished eating and then told me to change." 

"Because if I told you last night you would have spun yourself sick about it."

"You don't get to decide that for me."

"I've always had to decide that for you." He spread both hands, palms up, a gesture like offering evidence to a jury that did not care. "Since your mother got sick. Since before that, honestly. You were eight years old and forgetting homework in the freezer. You need structure or everything falls apart."

Anika felt the words hit one by one. Eight. Homework. Freezer. Structure. Everything falls apart. She had a clear memory of the purple folder with fractions tucked behind frozen okra, and Maya laughing so hard she had to sit down on the step stool. Ravi had laughed too, once. Back then he could still laugh and not treat it like leakage.

"I'm not eight," she said.

"Then prove it. Get your bag."

She stared at him. He stared back. Neither moved.

When Ravi spoke again his voice dropped, quieter and rougher. "We don't have the luxury of throwing away opportunities, Niks. We're already late on oncology payment this month. If you keep slipping ranking windows, this whole pathway closes and then what? You think the debt gets kinder because you needed a day off?"

He was not shouting. The softness made it worse.

"Don't do that," she said.

"Do what? Tell the truth?"

"Don't put the debt on me."

"It's on all of us." He took one step toward her. "You think I like driving strangers at one in the morning so ConEd doesn't shut us off? You think I like choosing between your tournament fee and groceries?"

Shame rose like bile and burned the back of her tongue. The number from the paperwork flashed in her mind in clean black print: 38,400. She could see Maya's thumbnail leaving a crescent in the bill while she nodded through the clerk's script.

Ravi saw the flinch and pressed.

"Your mother knew exactly what things cost," he said. "She sat in billing with me when her hair was coming out in clumps and still—still asked about your court time first. She did that because this mattered. Because you matter in it. You're built for more than... for more than this scared version of you."

The kitchen narrowed. The window fan clicked on and off as the extension cord failed to sit right in the socket. Anika's right shin pulsed with her heartbeat.

"Don't use her," she said.

He kept going, eyes wet now, voice turning jagged. "I held her when she couldn't breathe, and she said, 'Keep her moving.' Maybe I heard only what I wanted. No, I did hear what I wanted. But she didn't mean you quit the second this gets uncomfortable."

The sentence landed hard enough that Anika had to grip the chair back.

Maya's letter from the locker lived in the top drawer of Anika's desk, folded twice, paper going soft at the creases. In it Maya had written about ordinary life in a voice so plain it hurt: school projects, stupid jokes, sleep, friends, the right to be eleven when possible. She had underlined `sleep` twice, hard enough to score the paper. Anika had traced that groove with her thumbnail. Winning had to fit around those small things or it pressed down like weight on her sternum.

Ravi did not know that wording. He knew enough to bend the rest.

"Not after that," Anika said.

He blinked. "What?"

"Not after you used her like a lever." 

"Anika, I—" He dragged both hands down his face. "I shouldn't have used her like that. I'm sorry. I am. But we can't burn a booked slot."

"And my leg is already lit up from the stairs. I'm not burning impact because you panicked about rankings."

He slapped his palm on the counter, not full force, but hard enough to rattle the spoons in their steel cup. "No. We're done arguing. Get your shoes." 

She flinched, then straightened. "I'm not performing."

"You're turning one adjustment into a crisis because Priya filled your head with negotiation bullshit."

"Don't talk about her like that."

"Then stop acting like a tenant with terms and conditions. You're my daughter." 

"Then be my dad," she said, and her voice came out steadier than she felt. "Not my scheduler."
