That made him go still. For two beats she thought he might apologize. His face softened at the mouth and hardened again.

"Get your shoes," he said.

Anika looked at him, at the damp hair at his temples, at the old ankle rolling unconsciously against tile, at the dish towel over his shoulder with bleach stains from years ago. She thought, If I keep explaining, I will end up on court. If I bargain, I lose.

"I'm leaving before this turns into another court day," she said.

Then she turned and walked to her room.

Ravi called after her. "You walk away now, and you're proving every concern I had was right."

She did not answer.

In her room the air was hotter and still. The fan squeaked on every third turn. She pulled the small black duffel from under the bed and unzipped it all the way so the sound of the zipper teeth was one clean rip, no hesitation.

What counts as overnight when you are eleven and angry enough to shake?

She threw in clothes without folding, then froze with the duffel open while the fan squeak counted off seconds. She found the toothbrush pouch, deodorant, and the charger with frayed tape by touch instead of sight. Her hand hit the algebra packet; she shoved it back, yanked it out again, then jammed it in sideways. She added the shin sleeve and PT band, then packed Maya's watch charger even though the battery lasted four days. Last came the notebook and the letter tucked flat inside its cover so the old creases would not split.

Ravi appeared in the doorway with her racquet in his hand, as if he had already won.

"Put that down," she said.

"Take your court shoes and we can still make the slot." 

"I'm leaving." 

"For where?"

"Priya's."

He laughed once, short and bitter. "You called her already?"

Anika held up her phone. On screen Priya's name showed 8:11 a.m., call duration 00:43.

Ravi's eyes flicked to it and away. "You're escalating this on purpose."

"You did that at breakfast." 

"I'm your parent. I made a decision."

"You made a secret." 

His grip tightened on the racquet handle until his knuckles paled. "Don't do this to me."

The plea cracked on the last word. Ravi's grip loosened on the racquet for one beat, and her feet stalled on the carpet. Then the duffel strap bit deeper into her shoulder and she kept moving.

She zipped the duffel and slung it cross-body. The strap dug into the side of her neck.

"Move," she said.

"If you leave this apartment over one session, don't expect this to disappear by dinner."

"I know." 

"You think Priya can house you long term? In that one-bedroom with her files and no space?"

"I said tonight." 

"And tomorrow?"

"We'll talk tomorrow. Not now." 

He leaned on the doorframe, ankle stiff now, no circles. "You're making a child decision."

"Maybe," she said. "I'm still not going."

He did not move. She stepped toward him until she could smell soap and sweat and yesterday's bus diesel still clinging to his shirt. His jaw jumped once.

He stepped aside.

She walked past.

In the hallway she heard him say her name in a hoarse half-whisper, the old stoop-call voice that used to mean dinner. She kept walking.

Fried onions from the second-floor family rolled through the stairwell's damp concrete stink. The elevator had been broken for three weeks, a handwritten sign taped over the button with a cartoon frown face somebody had drawn in blue pen. By the second landing her shin throbbed and she hated that her body noticed. She tightened her grip on the rail and kept descending.

On the street the August heat wrapped around her in one wet slap. She stood under the awning and opened the rideshare app. Priya had already sent her card and a note: `Grey Camry. Driver name: Salma. Stay by the deli door.`

Three missed calls from Ravi blinked at the top of the screen.

She typed one line and hit send before she could edit it into something softer.

`I am safe. I am going to Priya's.`

She stared at the uncontracted words and did not fix them. She wanted them hard.

The Camry pulled up in seven minutes, hazard lights blinking like a nervous heartbeat. The driver was a woman in her fifties with mint-green nails and a Knicks visor clipped to the dashboard, and she had a tiny plastic Ganesha glued beside the meter with one missing arm.

"Anika?" she asked.

"Yeah." 

"Seat belt, please." She glanced in the mirror, took in the duffel and the face and whatever else was obvious, then added, "Traffic on Hoyt is garbage. We go 31st Avenue, okay?"

"Okay."

Salma turned the radio down from a call-in show about rent hikes and muttered, not to Anika exactly, "Every landlord in this city thinks we print money in the trunk."

The complaint sounded like something Maya used to say on bill days, and the memory made Anika's stomach clench.

They moved through Jackson Heights toward Astoria in short bursts, red lights every block. Men were unloading crates of mangoes outside Patel Brothers. A kid in a soccer jersey chased a pigeon across the crosswalk and got yanked back by his sister by the hood. At the underpass a bus hissed to a stop and the side panel ad for a private academy flashed white shoes, white courts, white teeth.

Ravi called again. Then again.

On the fourth call she answered and held the phone near her chest.

"What," she said.

"Where are you?"

"In a car." 

"Tell the driver to turn around."

"No."

"Anika, listen to me. We said things hot. Fine. Come back and we'll reset." 

"Reset to what?"

He inhaled, audible, trying to find stable ground. "To us. To family. You don't leave home over one argument."

"It wasn't one argument." 

"Then what was it?"

"You broke the deal and used Mom like a weapon." 

Silence, then, "I didn't weaponize your mother." 

"You did, and you know it." 

"I was reminding you what matters." 

She looked out the window at a pawn shop sign blinking CASH TODAY over a rack of cracked guitars. "I've got to go," she said.

"Don't hang up on me."

She hung up.

Her fingers trembled in a fine, constant vibration, like after too many ghosting sets. She pressed her palms against her thighs until heat replaced the buzz, but her jaw still clicked every time her molars met.

Priya's building in Astoria had narrow brick steps and a broken intercom where the 4 button stuck if you pressed too hard. Priya opened the door before Anika could ring. She was in black joggers and a faded legal-aid clinic T-shirt, one earring in and one still in her hand, hair clipped back with a binder clip because she had lost the real claw clip somewhere between work and home. She had two phones as always, one wedged between ear and shoulder with a client on hold, one face-down on the console table buzzing every thirty seconds.

She looked at Anika's bag, then at Anika's face. "Come in. Shoes off if they're wet." 

Anika stepped inside. Detergent hit first, then cumin and the sharp lemon cleaner Priya used when she was anxious. A ceiling fan turned above a couch that converted to a bed, and file boxes labeled `Housing`, `Family`, and `Immigration` were stacked along the galley kitchen wall in fat black marker. Maya's photo was on top of the bookshelf, a cheap printout from a picnic with her mouth open mid-laugh and sunglasses crooked.
The door latch snapped shut behind her. The metallic click hit the same nerve as Ravi's palm on the counter, and she bit the inside of her cheek hard enough to taste blood.

Priya lifted a finger to Anika and spoke into the phone on her shoulder. "Ms. Barros, I'm here. I have your papers. Do not sign anything tonight. I'll call you at nine in the morning and we do this together. Yes, I mean it. Drink water. Bye." She ended the call and finally put both phones on the table.

"Okay," she said. "You safe right now?"

"Yeah." 

"Did he touch you?"

"No."

"Did he block the door?"

"He stood there. Then he moved."

Priya nodded once. "Good. Stay specific with me. You're here tonight, and if we need tomorrow, we use tomorrow. Calling me before you hit the street was smart."

She held Anika's eyes. "This still has a price tag. If this spills past tomorrow, he'll start yelling interference and try to make school pickup ugly. Monday from here is two trains and an early transfer. None of that means you should've said yes. It means we plan the fallout instead of pretending this is free."

Anika dropped the duffel by the couch and sank onto the cushion. The springs complained.
The shaking got worse now that the door was closed. Her hands fluttered against her knees on their own; when she tried to slow her breath, her chest snagged halfway.

"I don't want to blow everything up," she said.

Priya crouched in front of her, voice level. "That part is already moving. First we lock boundaries."

Priya picked up the family phone, the one with the cracked blue case, and typed without hesitation. She read aloud as she wrote, calm and plain.

"Anika is with me at my apartment tonight. She is safe. Do not come over unannounced. We will speak tomorrow at 4:30 p.m. with ground rules."

She hit send.

It landed as a public fact, clean enough to leave no room for bargaining.

Anika's watch vibrated with an incoming message before Priya had set her phone down.

Dad: `Bring her home by nine. If not, I file custodial interference and pick her up at school myself Monday morning.`

Priya read over Anika's shoulder. "Screenshot that. He's moved from guilt to threat."

"Do I answer?" Anika asked.

"One message. Boundaries only. No argument."

Anika typed: `I'm safe at Priya's. My shin is already throbbing, and I'm not doing another court trip tonight. I need school things for Monday. We'll talk at 4:30 with Priya.`

She sent it. No bubbles came back.

Priya stood and walked to the fridge. "Now food. Then shower. Then we sort school and transit."

"I already ate." 

"You argued for an hour after that. Eat anyway."

Anika watched her scoop rice into a bowl. Priya always scraped the sides clean with the spoon before microwaving, as if leaving food stuck to plastic was a moral failure.

"He'll say I overreacted," Anika said.

"He can say what he wants. You said no. He didn't accept no. That's the center."

"He booked a spar with someone I don't even know." 

"I know." Priya handed her the bowl. "He already texted me about opportunity windows and the sixty dollars he burned on that slot."

Anika stared at the steam rising from the rice. Sixty dollars was ConEd plus half a week of MetroCards. She felt guilt climb her throat, fast and familiar.

Priya saw it and clicked her tongue. "Don't swallow all of it for him. He spent money on a session after agreeing to a rest day."

"And yes, sixty dollars is real money in your house. We can hold that truth without handing him your body to fix it."

Anika dug the spoon in and burned her tongue because she did not wait. Egg curry, too much chili, Priya's style. The pain was bright and useful.

Priya leaned against the counter and folded her arms. "Shower now or after you finish that?"

"After."

Priya set water to boil, then remembered she already had the kettle full from this morning and turned the flame down. "Shower first," she told Anika. "Then we sort practical things. School clothes. Toothbrush. Any meds left at home."

"I packed most of it."

"Good. That's your brain working under stress."

Anika took the shower and let the water run hot until the tiny bathroom mirror fogged. The argument replayed anyway. `She told me, "Keep her moving."` Did Maya say that? Maybe she did, once, during chemo when stairs were hard and everyone spoke in instructions because fear was easier in logistics. Maya could have said it and still meant something wider. Ravi had taken one line and pulled on it like a tow strap, dragging her toward Court 3 while her shin knocked pain up her leg on every stair.

When she came out in clean shorts, Priya had set up the couch bed with an old blue sheet printed with faded planets. She had also pulled a small toiletry bag from a drawer and lined up travel shampoo, a sealed toothbrush, and a tiny tube of toothpaste in military order.

"Emergency stash," Priya said, seeing Anika look. "Use whatever you need."

Anika sat on the edge of the couch and toed off her socks. The right shin ached deep, weather ache plus stair ache plus adrenaline dump. She pressed two fingers along the bone until she found the tender strip and stopped there.

Priya noticed. "Pain scale?"

"Three." 

"Real three or athlete three?"

Anika thought. "Five." 

"Thank you. Ice pack in freezer door." 

Priya's work phone buzzed. She glanced at the screen and swore once, softly. "Client emergency. I need fifteen minutes on the balcony. You okay alone?"

"Yeah."

Priya paused in the doorway to the balcony, then turned back. "One more thing. Leaving was the right call when your no wasn't being heard."

"Tomorrow is still going to be ugly: custody talk, school logistics, money talk, all of it. Keep your phone charged and don't meet him alone."

Then she stepped outside and closed the door most of the way, voice low as she took the call.

Anika held the ice pack against her shin and listened to the fan, the balcony traffic, the murmur of Priya saying words like notice period and landlord harassment and emergency filing. Grown-up war language, different battlefield.
Her phone stayed dark. That should have felt like relief. Instead it made the skin between her shoulder blades tighten. Ravi went quiet only when he was building the next move.

Anika pressed the melting ice pack harder against her shin until cold water ran down her wrist.

She opened her notebook instead. The pages from chapter six onward were dense with small writing: times, reps, pain numbers, debt math, reminders to ask Tess about shoe inserts, reminders to smile at teachers so they would stop asking if she was okay. Today she wrote only one line.

`Rest day held.`

She stared at the words. They looked thin for what they cost.

Priya came back in, rubbing her forehead, and pulled a yellow legal pad from the file stack. "Client's covered for tonight," she said. "Now us."

She sat across from Anika at the fold-out card table, one leg shimmed by a paperback Tamil dictionary, and drew three boxes down the page: `Tonight`, `Tomorrow 4:30`, `Monday School`. "We keep this boring and documented. That's how we keep you safe."

The apartment settled toward evening. They ate leftover rice and pickle from the jar and built Monday on Priya's pad: subway options, a spare MetroCard from Priya's desk, which teacher Anika could ask for hallway access if Ravi appeared at dismissal. Ravi did not call again. The silence felt less like peace than a fuse burning in another room.

Night came with thunder that never turned into rain, only pressure. Anika lay on the couch bed in Priya's too-small living room and listened to footsteps from upstairs, pipes knocking, a motorcycle revving and revving three blocks away. Her own room lived sixteen blocks west with its fan squeak and glow-sticker planets she had been embarrassed to remove. The court was somewhere else too, along with drills where every second had a purpose assigned by somebody else.

The emptiness should have felt like relief. It felt like standing at the top of a stairwell in the dark with one hand not quite finding the rail.

She took Maya's letter out from inside the notebook cover and unfolded it with careful fingers. She did not read the whole thing. She touched the part where the pen pressure had cut deeper into the paper, the places where Maya had paused and started again.

From the bedroom Priya called softly, "You need anything?"

"No," Anika said.

A beat, then Priya again: "I put your school clothes on the chair. Alarm is set for six thirty."

"Okay."

"Goodnight, kid." 

"Night."

Anika put the letter back, then reached for her watch and set tomorrow's alarm herself. The digital buttons were worn smooth from Maya's thumb and now hers. 6:30. School from Astoria. Bus or train depending on how bad the transfer was.

She unzipped the side pocket of her duffel for her student MetroCard and found only her library card, two quarters, and a hair tie. The MetroCard was still clipped to the ceramic bowl by their apartment door.

Round trip without student fare was $5.80. She had $2.50 and no way to get the card tonight without walking back into the same kitchen.

Her phone buzzed one last time. No text this round, just a calendar notification Ravi had not deleted.

`8:30 AM - Sparring (Nia, Court 3)`

Anika opened the event, pressed delete, and confirmed.

She turned the phone face-down on Priya's coffee table. Outside, thunder moved farther east without breaking. She listened until the building quieted and her own breathing found a steady tempo.

Tomorrow meant 4:30 at Priya's card table, yellow legal pad still open, three boxes down the page and a pen leaking a blue dot into `Monday School`. Monday still meant a school commute she could not pay for yet.

Tonight meant a thin mattress, a packed duffel by the couch, and her hand checking the zipper again to make sure it was really closed.

She let herself sleep with the bag where she could touch it.


# Chapter 14

By four-thirty the next day, Priya's apartment had trapped the whole day and refused to let it go. The one window above the sink was open three inches because the frame stuck in summer, and the air coming through it smelled like bus brakes, frying onions, and rain that had not arrived yet. The table was a fold-out card table with one leg shimmed by a paperback Tamil dictionary. Every time someone shifted weight, the table gave a small wooden complaint.

Anika sat with her overnight bag at her feet and her notebook closed under both hands. Maya's old watch pressed warm plastic against the inside of her wrist. The yellow legal pad Priya had left open last night still sat three boxes down the page, a blue dot bleeding through `Monday School`. Across from her, Priya lined up legal pads by the edge of the table and clicked one phone to silent, then the other. The work phone had a cracked corner case and five missed calls from clients stacked on the lock screen. She swiped them away with her thumb and did not apologize for the delay she was causing anyone. She tucked her short hair behind one ear, missed a piece, and left it there.

"I'm not doing this in chaos," she said. "We speak one at a time. We stay specific. We don't use your sister's name as a weapon. If either of you interrupts twice, I stop the meeting for ten minutes and we start again."

She slid a glass of water toward Anika and one toward the empty chair.

"Do you understand me?"

Anika nodded.

"Say it."

"Yes," Anika said. "I understand."

Priya tapped her pen once against her teeth. It was a habit from years of deposition prep, and Anika had watched entire family arguments wait for the pen-tap like traffic at a light.

The hallway floor groaned. Ravi knocked once and opened the door before anyone answered, like he still lived inside every room connected to Anika.

He looked smaller than he had two days ago. Same body, same narrow shoulders, but his dispatch shirt hung loose and his left ankle made him take stairs sideways. His ID badge was still clipped to his belt loop. He had forgotten to remove it after shift, and it swung when he moved, hitting his thigh with a plastic tick.

"Hi," he said.

Anika did not answer. She stared at the badge until the logo blurred.

Priya pointed to the chair. "Sit."

Ravi sat and put a folder on the table. He kept his hand on top of it, fingers spread, like someone might take it.

"Before we begin," Priya said, "status is unchanged from last night. Anika is staying here. No one goes home today because of pressure. We only move after terms are clear and written."

Ravi inhaled through his nose. "I came to make terms clear."

"Good," Priya said. "Round one. Each person gets two minutes. No interruption. You answer one question: what are we deciding."

She looked at Anika first.

Anika swallowed and felt her throat click. Her right shin was quiet while she sat, but the ache waited like a person in a different room. "We're deciding if I go back to the apartment," she said, "and if training is a thing that happens with rules, or a thing that changes whenever Dad is scared."

Ravi's jaw moved.

Priya held up a palm without looking at him. "Your turn." 

Ravi leaned forward, then leaned back. "We're deciding how to get her home and stable for regionals without blowing the work we put in for a year."

"That's half an answer," Priya said. "Try again without coach language."

He looked at her like she had asked him to do math in another alphabet. "We're deciding if my daughter comes home," he said. "And if she can trust me."

The table went still enough for Anika to hear the refrigerator motor kick on.

Priya wrote three lines on the top page. Her handwriting was square and fast.

"Round two," she said. "Rest day. What happened, no cleanup version. Anika first."

Anika fixed her eyes on a grease stain in the table laminate shaped like Sri Lanka. "I woke at five-thirty to two alarms fighting each other. The phone alarm had that cheap marimba loop. Mom's watch was beeping under it, high and sharp, because he let one run. I was still in socks when somebody knocked at six-oh-seven. Three hard hits. Dad opened before I got to the door. Nia was there in court shoes, racket bag wet against her hip. The hallway smelled like rain and wet rubber grips. Dad said there had been a schedule change."

Ravi's eyes shut for a second. "I wasn't trying to ambush you. I was trying to-"

"Ravi," Priya said, palm up. "Hold it."

Anika kept going. "I said no. He stepped between me and the shoe rack and left the door open so she could hear. He said Mom wouldn't let me waste a day this close to regionals. He said grief wasn't an excuse. He said if I wanted a life bigger than winning, I had to prove I could handle winning first."

Priya's pen stopped. "Did he touch you?"

"No."

"Did he block the door?"

"With his body, yes. Not with his hands."

Priya watched her for a beat. "What happened in your body when he said all that?"

"My hands went cold and my heart started slamming," Anika said. "I couldn't hear the second alarm. I could only hear him and that knock still banging in my head."

"Did it feel like you had a real choice?"

Anika watched the watch numbers blink to :42. "No. Not until I grabbed my bag and walked out."

Priya turned to Ravi. "Your version. Same rule: no sales pitch."

Ravi dragged the folder closer until it nudged his water glass and left a wet ring on the table. "I booked a hitter Thursday night. I didn't tell her in advance. I told myself it was footwork and short rallies, not full load. I told myself rest could move to Monday because school was lighter. I told myself a lot of things."

"Did you invoke Maya?"

He looked straight at Anika then away. "Yeah."

"Why?"

"Because I knew she'd refuse." His voice was flat now, technical. "And because when she refuses I hear this clock start, like everything's slipping and I can't catch it."

"Slipping where?" Priya asked.

"Regionals first. Then sponsor odds. Then money. I kept telling myself one session wouldn't matter. I kept telling myself missing regionals would matter more."

She let silence sit long enough to be annoying, then said, "Round three. Debt and fear. No euphemisms. Ravi first."

He flipped the folder open to the billing pages and flattened them with his palm. "You want numbers? Fine. Queens Oncology, thirty-eight thousand four hundred on payment plan, due the fifteenth every month. Utility arrears, one thousand eight sixty, still hanging over us because I paid medicine before ConEd in April and June. Rent is current because if rent slips once we never recover. My base pay is thirty-nine hundred net and overtime covers whatever crisis is loudest. Regionals is not a hobby line item. It is the only place where a sponsor might show up before the debt eats us."

Anika looked at the folder under his hand. She knew those numbers. Knowing did not make them less sharp.

"And fear," Priya said.

Ravi kept his eyes on the table. "The night Maya died, monitors kept alarming because her oxygen kept dropping and they kept resetting because nobody wanted to call time before family arrived. The sound was high and stupid and constant. I still hear that sound in the shower sometimes."

His left hand pinched the skin between thumb and finger until it went white.

"After that night, every quiet hour in our house felt dangerous. Training was loud. Training had a timer and a count and a result. If she was moving, I could tell myself she was alive and moving toward something."

He finally looked at Anika. His eyes were red, not wet. "I am scared of losing you," he said. "Not in a metaphor way. In a body-bag way. I lost your mother in one long hospital room. I cannot do that again."

Anika flinched hard enough that the watch face hit bone.

Priya leaned forward. "Say it with contractions," she said quietly. "You're not testifying."

Ravi swallowed. "I'm scared of losing you like I lost Maya," he said. "Every time your pain spikes, I feel like the floor drops. Then I grab control because grabbing feels better than waiting."

The refrigerator clicked off. Somewhere outside, a bike bell rang twice and faded.

Priya turned to Anika. "Your turn. Cost."

Anika had planned sentences in her head all morning: concise, adult, bullet-point clean. None of those sentences arrived. Her mouth moved before structure did.

"The cost is I don't know if home is home or a training site," she said. "I don't know if breakfast is breakfast or carb timing. I don't know if you ask how I slept because you care or because you want shin pain data before warm-up."

Ravi's shoulders folded in.

"The cost is I started lying in spring," she said. "I lied about pain because pain was a problem to solve, not something I could have. The cost is I hear Mom's name and wait for what command comes after it."

Priya did not interrupt. She only nudged the water glass closer when Anika stopped to breathe.

"You treat me like a project," Anika said. "Like if you adjust enough variables I become the outcome that pays bills and proves you were right. But I'm not a project. I'm your kid. Projects can be overworked and rewritten. Kids remember."

She felt heat climb her neck. "When I came here Friday night, I slept six hours straight for the first time since qualifier season. No alarms. No surprise sessions. I woke up and my first thought was cereal, not whether I had disappointed you before sunrise. That is what it cost me to stay."

Ravi stared at the folder. "I never wanted you afraid of me."

"That's not the same as not making me afraid of you," Anika said.

Priya wrote, tore the page out, and placed it between them. On it she had printed two columns in block letters.

HOME = CARE + CONSENT + RULES.

TRAINING = LIMITED + MONITORED + REVERSIBLE.

"We move to terms," she said. "If we can't get terms you both can live with, status stays what it is. Anika remains here. Ravi gets visitation with schedule. That's legal reality while she's a minor and I'm emergency guardian."

The word guardian sat heavy in Anika's gut. It was a legal word, but it still felt like somebody had to guard her from her own father.

Ravi looked up sharply. "You're threatening me."

"No," Priya said. "I'm describing options without fog."

She stood and went to the tiny counter. Her movements were quick and practiced, but she paused to shake cumin into the pan where leftover rice was reheating for later. She always cooked when she had to think, and the room filled with hot spice and old grief in equal measure.

When she came back, she set a fresh legal pad in front of herself and uncapped a blue pen.

The pen click hit Anika's teeth like a start-pistol.

Priya wrote 1 in the margin and boxed it. "First clause: residence and exit. Anika goes home only after this agreement is signed. If terms are breached, she can come back here for seventy-two hours while we reset."

Ravi's head jerked up. "So one bad day and she leaves?"

"One breach and she gets safety space," Priya said. "No chase, no argument, no relitigation at the doorway."

Anika found the rough strap of her overnight bag under the table and wrapped two fingers around it until her pulse slowed.

"And no blocking the door," Anika said. "Write that too."

Priya nodded and added a line. "No physical obstruction at exits."

"You can't run a house with an escape hatch," Ravi said.

"You can't run one with a trap door either," Priya said.

Anika met his eyes. "If I can't step out, it's not trust. It's compliance."

His jaw flexed twice. "Fine. Seventy-two."

Priya tapped the next line with her pen. "Second clause is workload and visibility. Eight hours max on court each week, including matches. Three hours max strength and mobility. One full rest day. Shared log to Tess Keating, Jonah Feld, and me: start time, duration, pain before and after, sleep, and any change to plan."

Ravi shook his head before she finished. "No. Eight is maintenance, not build. Regionals is four weeks out."

At the word build, Anika's right shin lit with a ghost ache, the same hot wire she had felt inside the MRI tube when she still thought she could bargain with bone.

"The cap is the point," Priya said. "Not a suggestion."

"Then write this honestly," Ravi said. "Write that we're choosing to lose and maybe closing the last sponsor window while debt keeps compounding."

"Write it," Anika said. "I'm still not giving my leg to a spreadsheet."

Ravi opened the folder, pushed one highlighted statement across the table, then pulled it back when Priya did not look down. "Collection calls are hitting me at seven a.m. in front of dispatch staff. That's the timeline I'm carrying."

The numbers thudded in Anika's chest with old familiarity: thirty-eight thousand four hundred, one thousand eight sixty, rent always one late check from collapse.

"I hear you," Priya said. "What's your counter."

"Ten on court, two strength, internal logs only," Ravi said. He dragged a hand over his face. "No, listen, nine maybe. But not eight with nightly copy chains."

"No," Anika said.

"You don't get to erase math because it scares you," Ravi snapped.

"You don't get to call panic math," Anika shot back. "You already did that Friday."

Ravi shoved his chair back hard enough that the shimmed table leg slipped and thunked. Water jumped in both glasses. He grabbed his folder. "Then we're done. She stays here, I pay for two homes, and regionals dies."

"Stop," Priya said, cutting across him. "Break. Ten minutes. Nobody leaves the apartment."

Ravi stood at the sink with both hands on the counter, breathing through his nose. Anika stayed in her chair and counted the rain strikes on the fire escape, groups of five, then five again. Priya did not speak until Ravi sat back down.

"Reset," Priya said. "Eight on-court hours stays. Ravi gets one forty-five-minute film block each week for opponent prep. Film only. Zero impact. Logged and reviewable."

Ravi stared at the pad. "Film is not court."

"No," Priya said. "It's what you get instead of a ninth hour and another fight about her shin."

Anika nodded once. "I can do film. I can't do another surprise block."

Ravi dragged a thumb across his eyebrow. "If I'm giving up two court blocks, I need predictable scheduling. No last-minute surprises from either side."

"You'll get school deadlines by eight p.m. in the shared log," Anika said. "If I miss an update, next morning session is canceled. That's on me."

Priya wrote that down, then looked at Ravi. "Your cost: no unscheduled hitter bookings. If you book impact work with less than forty-eight-hour notice or without Anika's written yes, seventy-two-hour relocation triggers and the next two on-court sessions are forfeited."

Ravi's mouth tightened. "Two sessions is steep."

"So was Friday," Priya said.

He held Anika's gaze, then nodded once. "Fine. Put both penalties in."

"Done," Priya said, boxing the clause.

Priya boxed the penalties and moved down the page. "Third clause: automatic pause trigger. Pain at four out of ten during a session, or night pain that wakes Anika, equals a forty-eight-hour stop. Tess decides return progression."

"Four is tight," Ravi said, quieter now. "Some mornings she's already at three."

Anika pressed her thumb to the shin ridge through her sock. "At four, the ball sound goes blurry and I chase bad shots because panic feels like effort. We stop at four. No bargaining mid-drill."

Ravi rubbed his jaw. "Then add mandatory PT reevaluation inside that forty-eight-hour pause so stop does not become drift."

"Added," Priya said.

Without lifting her eyes, Priya started the next heading. "Fourth clause: language and repair. No invoking Maya to force compliance. No framing refusal as betrayal. Weekly grief counseling for Anika for twelve weeks. Parent counseling for Ravi every two weeks minimum."

Ravi flinched, then rubbed the center of his chest. "Put the Maya line in bold."

"Underline it too," Anika said.

Priya underlined the line twice.

"Every two weeks is rough with depot shifts," Ravi said. "But monthly is fake and we all know it."

Priya slid her work phone onto the table, screen already on appointment notes. "Queens Family Health has Saturday slots. Sliding scale with income verification. Separate providers. Start next week."

He stared at her. "You lined this up before we sat down."

"Yes," Priya said. "Hope doesn't make appointments."

He let out a tired breath that almost turned into a laugh. "You always built exits before anyone admitted there was a fire."

"That's how I keep people from getting cornered," she said.

She hooked the page corner and kept reading. "Fifth clause: school is protected. No dawn court on exam or presentation days."

Ravi rubbed his badge with his thumb. "I can do that if I get notice by eight the night before. Depot swaps don't happen at sunrise."

"You'll get screenshots in the log," Anika said. "No five-thirty ambush alarms."

Priya clicked her pen once. "Sixth clause: Sunday check-in, thirty minutes, agenda in advance, witness rotates."

Ravi frowned. "Rotates how?"

"Me first month," Priya said. "Then Tess if she can. Jonah by phone if she can't."

Anika hooked a finger under the notebook elastic and held it taut. "If voices spike again?"

She wrote the last number hard enough to dent the legal pad. "Seventh clause: twenty-four-hour cool-down. Logistics texts only. No post-fight essays."

No one argued with that.

Priya tore off two full pages and slid them side by side, then rewrote the terms in clean numbered language. The apartment sounded suddenly huge around the scratch of her pen. Neighbor kids ran in the hallway, feet slapping the wood. A pressure cooker hissed in another unit. Rain finally started and hit the fire escape hard enough to sound like handfuls of nails.

While Priya wrote, Ravi stood and went to the sink. He braced both palms on the counter and looked out the stuck-open window.

Without turning, he said, "When you were in that MRI tube, I sat outside pretending to read the contrast form. I kept staring at your sneakers on the table. I kept thinking, if this scan comes back bad again, I'm done." He wiped his mouth with the back of his hand. "Not dead. Just the worst version of me. Useless. Mean."

Anika stared at the blue veins under her own wrist skin.

"After your mother, everybody kept saying be strong, be practical, keep routines," Ravi said. "Nobody said how to stop when routines become punishment. I didn't know where that line was. Maybe I still don't."

Priya did not look up from the page. "Then this is the line," she said.

He turned back to the table. "I'm sorry," he said to Anika. "Not performance sorry. Not meeting sorry. I scared you on purpose because I was scared."

Anika felt the apology land and skid. Part of her wanted to throw it back because it had arrived late and clean and after witnesses. Another part wanted to fold it into her pocket anyway because she had been waiting months to hear anything like it.

"I believe you're scared," she said. "I still need the paper."

"You should," he said.

Priya finished writing and read the document out loud. Her cadence was steady, almost boring, which made every line feel heavier.

When she reached the signature block, she paused.

"Legal note," she said. "Anika is eleven. She cannot execute a contract independently. Guardian signature controls household obligations. But we include her signature as participant acknowledgment, and we distribute copies to enforceable third parties. That creates practical consequences if terms are breached."

She slid the pages to Ravi first.

He read twice, lips moving. At clause two he circled the names and wrote "context note re debt + tournament schedule" in the margin. At clause three he added "PT reevaluation within 48h" and looked at Anika for permission. She nodded.

He signed in blue ink: RAVI IYER, guardian.

The pen left a slight tear in the paper where he pressed too hard.

Priya signed beneath as mediator and temporary guardian contact.

Then she turned the pages to Anika and anchored them with one finger at the signature line, where Ravi's ink had not fully dried.

PARTICIPANT ACKNOWLEDGMENT: ANIKA IYER.

Anika held the pen above the page. For one bad second she heard Friday's double alarm again, the marimba loop and the high watch beep stacked crooked on each other. Her pulse jumped against the watch strap. She pressed the pen down anyway and wrote her name in careful print, not cursive. The letters leaned right, like they were trying to move.

When she finished, the rain had intensified. The old window rattled in its frame. Priya blew on the ink, then photographed each page with her work phone, then her personal phone, then fed copies through the small printer on the shelf by the microwave. The machine whined and spat warm paper that smelled faintly of toner and cumin.

Priya handed one copy to Ravi in a manila envelope and one to Anika, folded small and tucked into the side pocket of her bag.

"Tonight," Priya said, "Anika decides where she sleeps. No persuasion speech. Just answer if she asks a question."

Ravi looked at his daughter and waited.

Anika listened to rain on metal and the printer fan winding down. She imagined the apartment kitchen in Jackson Heights: narrow tile, mustard seeds snapping in oil, the fridge calendar where rules could be posted or ignored. She imagined this table in Astoria, cramped and safer, with Priya's two phones lighting up every ten minutes and no one pretending life could be solved by another drill set.

"I'll go back tomorrow morning," she said. "Not tonight. I want one more night here, then I walk in with the paper already active."

Ravi nodded immediately, too quickly, then forced himself to slow down. "Okay. Tomorrow morning. I'll come at nine unless you text different."

"Nine-thirty," Anika said. "I've got a counseling intake call at nine."

Priya looked up. "Good. Put that in the shared log as first entry."

Anika opened her notebook to a blank page. Her private columns had started as reps and pain and calories and hours slept. She drew new headers with deliberate pressure.

DATE.

SLEEP.

PAIN BEFORE.

PAIN AFTER.

WHO CHANGED THE PLAN.

At the last header she paused, then wrote one more line under it.

WAS THERE CONSENT.

She closed the notebook and slid the copy of the agreement inside.

Ravi stood, picked up his envelope, and clipped his badge loose from his belt. He folded the lanyard and put it in his pocket like a small surrender.

At the door he turned back.

"I can't promise I'll never panic," he said. "I can promise panic doesn't get to write schedules anymore."

Priya leaned against the table edge. "That line is yours," she said. "Keep it."

He left.

The hallway swallowed his footsteps one stair at a time.

Priya let out a breath that sounded like she'd been holding it since lunch. She reached for the pen, realized the cap was missing, found it under the legal pad, and clicked it shut with unnecessary force.

"I'm reheating rice," she said. "You need to eat before you crash." She hesitated. "And if you decide tomorrow that you're not ready to return, we revisit. Paper is a floor, not a cage."

Anika nodded. Then Priya's stove timer chirped from the counter, and Anika jerked hard enough to tap her water glass with her knuckles.

"Shit," she muttered, grabbed her duffel, and checked the side pocket where the agreement sat. She moved her library card and two quarters into the front zip, where her hand could find them in the dark. She set her shoes by the door before she could talk herself out of it.

Outside, thunder rolled over Queens in a long low line. On the table, the signatures dried. By the couch, her bag stayed packed.


# Chapter 15

The Midtown club smelled like lemon cleaner and old money, two sharp notes under the colder air that blasted through the vents above Court Three. Anika stood at the back wall in her warm-up jacket and watched condensation crawl down the outside of her water bottle. Outside, the city had been wet heat and bus exhaust and people swearing at stalled trains. In here, everybody's hair looked dry and expensive.

Her name sat on the white bracket board in black marker: IYER, A.
Under it, the board listed BELL, C.
It was a quarterfinal match, best of five.
The signed agreement from Priya's table rode in the side pocket of Anika's bag, folded small and loud.
Three hours earlier thunder had rolled over Queens while those signatures dried; now the only weather in her lungs was refrigerated and chemical.

Ravi had arrived early enough to claim two seats up in the second row, off to the right of the glass. He had not taken the coaching box, and he was not pressed against the door the way he used to stand with his hand flat on the frame, breathing like he could push her into each point by force. He sat with his knees spread and his elbows on his thighs, ticket stub folded into a hard little rectangle between his fingers. Every few seconds he unfolded it and folded it again.

Anika bounced once on her toes and felt her right shin answer with a quiet, dull warning. It wasn't pain exactly, just a knuckle tapping from inside the bone. Damp weather always did this. She pressed two fingertips against the ridge through her sock, waited, then let go.

The sensation stayed blunt, a muted knock instead of the knife-edge she feared.

She kept her face blank and walked to the service box for ghost swings.

Cora Bell came in from the far hallway with a navy academy duffel and a kit that looked pressed. Even her compression sleeve, black and pulled over her left knee, was clean enough to reflect the fluorescent strip above the bench. She set her bag down, lined up three practice balls by the red dot like she was arranging medication, then crouched to retie the same lace she had already tied. On the second knot she paused, looked up at the scoreboard monitor, and rolled her shoulders back until her posture clicked into place.

When she saw Anika watching, she gave one short smile.

"Hey," Cora said. "You made it back quick. Thought they'd keep you on a leash longer."

Her tone stayed moderate, not loud, and it still scraped.

Anika bounced the ball against the sidewall and caught it on the third rebound. "I'm cleared."

"Yeah." Cora's mouth tilted half an inch. "I read. Compliance superstar."

There was no cartoon sneer in it, no hiss. Cora sounded like she was discussing weather, which made it worse.

Anika nodded once. "Good luck."

"You too." Cora picked up her racquet and took two fluid shadow swings. "Seriously. I don't want an asterisk if I beat you."

She said it with the same polished tone she'd use for an interviewer. Anika could not decide whether that made it honest or mean.

The referee for their court, a woman with a silver braid and a pink insulin pump clipped at her waistband, called them in for the pre-match check. Her name tag read M. GARCIA in block letters. She looked at Anika's shoes, then at Cora's, then at both racquet strings with the fast competence of somebody who had repeated these gestures for years.

"Any equipment issues, say it now," Garcia said.

Neither spoke.

"Good. Warm-up starts when I call it. Keep your traffic clear and your appeals clean. I'm not interested in drama."

Cora's expression did not move, but she pressed both thumbs under the edge of her knee sleeve and tugged it up a centimeter. Anika noticed a faint line of old tape residue on Cora's calf where the skin was lighter. Private evidence under public polish.

Ravi stood as they walked toward the door, then remembered himself and sat back down before she reached the service box. His mouth opened, closed, then he raised his hand as if to straighten imaginary hair behind his own ear. A gesture with no message in it.

Anika stepped inside the glass.

The door seal thudded shut behind her. Sound changed immediately: HVAC hum, soles squeaking, ball on wall like a hard rubber heartbeat. Spatial pressure slid into place around her ribs. Four white walls, red lines, tinny crowd noise leaking through the door seams.

She had to compete under the new limits.
She could not try to dominate or prove she was indestructible; she had to compete.

She said the word to herself once, lips barely moving.

Warm-up was ninety seconds each. Cora hit hard drives early, then softened into high lobs that died deep with no warning. It was the first hint that pace change was habit for her, not trick.

Anika answered with straight length, right side then left, feet set each time before contact. She used discipline as architecture. If she rushed, she sprayed. If she sprayed, Cora would own the T in under a game.

"Time," Garcia said.

Cora won the spin and chose serve. She rolled the ball in her palm, bounced it twice, and waited until the gallery quieted before starting her motion. Not long enough for a violation. Long enough to make everybody look at her.

First rally: hard serve nicked the left wall, fast enough that Anika had to lunge and lift. Cora pounced and drove low to the back right. Three shots later Anika guessed wrong and moved up for a drop that never came.

Garcia called the point for Cora.

Second rally: Cora opened with a high defensive arc that bought herself five seconds and reset both lungs. Next shot, she cut the pace in half again with a float to the back. Then she suddenly ripped a straight drive like a slap.

Anika got there late and framed it. Two-love to Cora before Anika's lungs had settled.

By 4-1, Anika's calves were already hot. Not burning, just full. Cora was changing the speed of each exchange the way some people changed tone mid-sentence: quick-quick-slow, hold, burst, feather, smash. The rhythm would not settle.

Anika went to process. She wiped her palm, reset her feet, and chose one target per rally. Deep backhand. Deep backhand again. Make Cora hit from behind her hip. On the next point, she got that exact ball, stepped in, and sent a hard straight length tight enough to die before the nick.

Cora could only scrape a boast short, and Anika punished it crosscourt for 4-2.

Ravi made a tiny fist in the stands, then smoothed his hand flat against his knee like he had touched something hot.

With Cora up 6-3, she took a full extra beat before serve, looking down at her strings as if checking a fray that was not there. Garcia said, "Play." Cora nodded, served, and then attacked with immediate pace, catching Anika still adjusting to the pause.

The monitor flipped to 7-3 while Anika was still unclenching her grip.

Anika swallowed hard. The cool air tasted chemical. She could hear two kids in the row behind Ravi whispering odds and names of academies and who had gotten private-sponsor shoes this summer. Somebody said, "Bell's got this in four." Somebody else said, "Iyer used to outlast everybody."

The phrase used to stung worse than the score. If she answered that with speed just to prove she still could, she would hand Cora exactly what she wanted and burn through the load limits before game two.

Down 8-4, Anika changed her own tempo for the first time. She slowed on purpose before returning serve, bounced once on her heels, then sent a high cautious lob that forced Cora to take it late. Next shot, Anika drove low and hard. Then she held, showed crosscourt, and straightened at the last instant.

Cora guessed cross and ended up wrong-footed. 8-5, and the court felt breathable again.

Anika took two more points with disciplined width, but Cora closed the game 11-7 with a backhand drop that died as if the floor had swallowed it.

She refused panic because it was only one game.

Anika walked to the towel hook and did not look at the stands. She knew Ravi would be there with his jaw locked and numbers running behind his eyes. She could not carry his math and her own.

Game two opened cleaner. Anika found a serve variation to Cora's body that jammed her left arm and drew short returns. She converted twice. 3-1.

Cora answered with a grin that showed one chipped canine on the left side. New detail Anika had never noticed from across a court. Human, not poster.

"Nice," Cora said, almost approving. "Do it again."

Anika did. This time Cora expected it and volleyed early to the front right. Anika scraped it up. Cora lifted. Long rally. Twenty-two shots, maybe more. Anika stopped counting after twelve because counting made her breathe wrong.

She felt her shin on shot nineteen, a low pulse under the bone.
She stayed on it anyway and won the point with a high dying length that clung to the sidewall. The scoreboard clicked to 4-2.

Ravi shifted in his seat, leaned forward, then leaned back. His right hand rose two inches from his thigh, fingers splayed in the old signal for widen angle. He froze, looked at his own hand, and closed it slowly. Then he tucked both hands under his legs.

Anika saw it in the mirror reflection of the front glass, blurred but unmistakable.

No code was coming from the seats. She had to pick the next pattern herself.

The thought came unwelcome and stayed.

Cora changed tactic at 5-4. She stopped going for outright winners and started feeding Anika medium-paced balls to the same back corner, over and over, forcing her to generate her own pace from tired legs. Mean in a smart way. No hero shot, just attrition arranged like homework.

Anika recognized the trap and stepped half a sneaker onto the T. She took the ball before it kissed the back glass, shortened her swing, and kept the racket face quiet through contact instead of forcing a hero cut. Two clean rallies later, she was up 7-5.

When Cora finally forced a loose ball with a disguised hold, Anika chased, got a racket face under it, and somehow found a high defensive lift that bought breath. Crowd noise swelled and collapsed around her like surf.

She took game point at 10-8 and converted on the next rally with a straight kill that kissed the nick and barely bounced for 11-8.
The match stood at one game all.

She did not celebrate. She only flexed her fingers and felt the pulse at her throat. Her chest worked hard but even.

Her knees held steady.

Between games, Garcia drank from a stainless bottle covered in stickers from half the borough leagues and made a note on her clipboard. "Let's keep the clear requests early," she said to both girls. "I'm not reading minds."

"Yes, ma'am," Cora said.

"Got it," Anika said.

Game three became a knife fight in small spaces. Cora sped up the start of rallies with quick serves to the backhand, slowed the middle with hanging length, then spiked the finish with sudden low drives. Anika answered with a body checklist: breathe in on bounce, breathe out on contact, plant left heel before the swing, recover to the T with the racket up.

They collided lightly in the middle at 3-3 as both moved for a tight drop. Garcia called a let. Cora lifted a hand in apology. Anika nodded without words.

Cora tried a boast at 5-4 and left it sitting up; Anika put it away. The next rally burned Anika when she guessed cross and Cora went straight to level at 5-5. They fought through a forty-shot exchange to 6-6, ending with Cora clipping the tin and saying a clipped, private "damn" that did not match her polished face.
Anika edged ahead at 7-6.

Ravi was no longer trying to signal. He sat with his shoulders pulled high, ticket stub now shredded into paper curls in his fist.

By 8-7, the first real fatigue wave rolled through Anika's hips, thick and sudden. Reduced volume had made her cleaner in short bursts and made her calves lock sooner in these long grinding exchanges. That gap was why Priya had forced signatures onto paper and why Tess would bench her if she lied. She could chase one flashy winner for the row-three voices, or she could pin Cora behind her hip with another hard length. She chose the back wall.

Cora saw it too. She lengthened the next three rallies, forcing corner to corner, and took two points to lead 8-9.

Anika served wide. Cora returned short. Anika drove deep. Cora counter-dropped. Anika got there with her last clean stride and flicked it up. Cora volleyed aggressively and clipped the top of the tin, bringing it to 9-9.

The gallery had gone still enough that Anika could hear the squeak of Cora's knee sleeve fabric when she bent before receive.

Anika moved to 10-9, game ball, after a stroke call.
She lost it on a rushed drop. 10-10 again.

Deuce turned into another tempo trap. Cora took extra seconds before one serve, wiped her brow with the back of her wrist, bounced three times, and sent a high conservative ball that forced Anika to generate. Next rally she skipped the pause and fired low with immediate volley pressure. By the time Anika mapped the shift, Cora had edged ahead.

Cora moved ahead 11-10.

Anika held with a tight drive that died deep. 11-11.

Next point lasted forever. Anika's lungs scraped. Sweat stung her eyes. Her right shin buzzed with warning but stayed blunt, never sharp. Cora tried to pin her in the back left. Anika lifted. Cora cut it off early. Anika scrambled, retrieved, recovered, hit width, hit width again.

On the eighteenth shot Cora guessed drop and moved in. Anika saw the movement and punched length instead for a winner that made it 12-11.
She had game ball.

She finished it with patience, not violence: deep straight, deep straight, then a measured drop when Cora's feet stayed back for 13-11.
She now led two games to one.

When she came out for the ninety-second break, Ravi was standing now, then forcing himself down into the seat as she looked up. He pressed both palms to his thighs like he was pinning himself there.

He did not mouth instructions.

He only said, quiet but audible through the crack at the door when Garcia opened it for ventilation, "Breathe, Niks. Just breathe."
No command pattern followed, no verbs about winning, no shape of punishment behind it; only breath.

Game four punished her anyway.

Cora started taking the ball absurdly early, volleying from midcourt before rallies could settle. When Anika tried to counter with height, Cora waited and hammered. When Anika went hard and low, Cora feathered and made her sprint. Pace whip, pause, whip again.

Two fast exchanges later it was 2-5, then 3-7, numbers climbing against her before each breath recovered.

Down 4-8, Anika hit a perfect length and still lost the rally when Cora countered with a drop so soft it looked accidental. Anika reached it half a beat late and shoved the ball into tin with a sound like a small gunshot.

The crowd inhaled together, and the scoreboard read 5-9.

Her face burned. Her shoulders felt poured full of wet sand. She heard, from somewhere in row three, a man laughing and saying, "She's cooked."

Shame came hot and immediate, old as chapter five, as the day she had limped alone running suicides because pain had felt like proof. The old script rose in her body and told her that after this she should punish herself with extra ghosts and stair repeats until nausea so she could earn the right to stop hurting by hurting more.

She nearly said yes to it on instinct. On the next rally she could have gone for a reckless front-court nick and maybe bought one roar from the crowd. Instead she sent three high lengths to reset the exchange and made Cora earn the finish.

Then she saw her own reflection in the side glass, small and flushed and still upright, and the script hesitated.

She had to compete under the new limits.

That was the contract she had signed with Priya's black pen while Ravi stared at the table and then signed too.

Cora closed game four 11-6 with ruthless efficiency and no showboating. She took what was there and moved on.
The match was tied at two games all, and the fifth was the decider.

Garcia called for a brief wipe because sweat had pooled near the front left. A volunteer ran in with a towel and did the quick circular mop. He was a teen boy with acne along his jawline and an ankle bracelet peeking above his sock. On his way out he whispered to nobody, maybe to himself, "Don't slip, please don't slip," with the prayerful panic of somebody who had already watched one bad fall this season.

Anika drank. Her hand shook enough to tap bottle against teeth.

Ravi stayed seated. He lifted his hand once, index finger half-raised in a tiny old signal for hit deep middle. He looked at it, exhaled, and lowered it to his lap. He rubbed his left ankle through his slacks, a private habit when pain or panic climbed, then folded both arms.

He let her go back in without another word.

Game five started with a rally that announced the terms: thirty-one shots, no clean winner, both girls refusing short vanity. Anika took it with a backhand drive tight enough to shave paint. 1-0 Anika.

Cora answered with two fast points and a delayed serve that made Anika start then stop, burning a little extra oxygen before contact. Cora up 2-1.

At 2-2, Anika hit a good width and recovered late. Cora punished the open court to make it 2-3.

Anika forced Cora deep at 3-3 and earned a loose reply she could kill. She did, then caught herself wanting to glance at the stands for approval. She kept her eyes on the back wall instead. 4-3.

Cora nodded once, as if checking a box.

"There you go," she said between points, no smile now.

Anika did not answer.

They traded to 6-6, then 7-7. Each point pulled a little more from her legs. Her breathing stayed loud but controlled. Her shin still buzzed but never spiked. She filed that fact in a hidden ledger even while she fought.

Facing 7-8, she retrieved a dying drop, then a second, then a boast, and finally forced a weak ball she could have slapped crosscourt for style. She chose straight and deep instead, tying it at 8-8.

The rally that broke it came at 8-9. Cora served quick. Anika dug it out. Cora slowed with two high lifts, then accelerated with a hard body line that jammed Anika's swing. The return floated short, and Cora killed for 8-10.
Cora had match ball.

Anika took one deep breath and stood still until the tremor in her forearm settled.

Cora bounced the ball once, twice, with no extra theatrics now.
She served tight to the backhand.

Anika got it up, deep enough to keep rally alive. Cora drove hard to the opposite corner. Anika reached, set, sent length. Cora held, showed cross, went straight. Anika slipped half a step on the still-damp patch near midcourt and recovered late.

Her final shot sat up in the middle.

Cora stepped in and cracked a forehand winner that hit three inches above the tin and died.
Garcia called the score 11-8 and the match to Cora Bell, three games to two.

For half a second the world went silent in Anika's head. Then sound flooded back: claps, one sharp whistle, somebody saying "Hell of a quarter," Garcia announcing official score, sneakers squeaking as people stood.

Anika stood upright.

Her lungs hurt. Her shoulders trembled. Her right shin throbbed like a warning light that had not yet turned red.

She kept standing under her own weight, breathing through the shake instead of folding into the old theater of worth.

She walked to the center and held out her hand.

"Good match," she said.

Cora's palm was dry and warm. "Good match," she replied, then, lower so only Anika heard: "You made me work for every point. That's not fake."

Up close, Cora's mascara had smudged at the outer corners and she had a crescent moon of ball fuzz stuck to sweat on her neck. She looked thirteen then, not polished property of an academy.

"Thanks," Anika said.

She meant it and hated that she meant it.

Cora glanced toward her own side of the gallery where two adults in club blazers were already motioning for her. "I've got to go do the polite thing," she muttered. "See you next draw."

She jogged off with her posture back in place before she hit the door.

Garcia signed the score sheet and held it out for both girls. "Strong choices," she said to Anika, tapping the page once with her pen. "Late in game five you kept your shoulders down and got back to the T after contact."

The referee's compliment was clinical and brief, but it pressed under her ribs where shame had been blooming.

Outside the court, phones were already up. A boy from another bracket had posted a clip before she had even zipped her bag. She saw her own face frozen mid-lunge on his screen with the caption CLOSE ONE.

A man in an academy quarter-zip stepped into the aisle as she passed and said, "Tough one, kid. Fifth game, your legs were gone."

Heat shot from Anika's collarbones to her ears. Her stomach cramped so hard she had to swallow twice before breath moved cleanly again.

Another parent clapped Ravi on the shoulder. "Next cycle you push volume, she'll bury Bell."

Ravi turned fast, peeled the hand off with two fingers, and set it back at the man's side.

"Don't coach my kid," he said.

His phone lit with rec-center messages in a bright stack across the lock screen. He flipped it face-down hard enough to rattle the bench, then nodded for Anika to move.

Ravi waited until they were clear of the bench area before he spoke.

"Water first," he said.
He left the nine-all rush alone and kept tonight pointed at recovery.

Anika uncapped the bottle and drank until her stomach sloshed.

They walked down the corridor toward the locker rooms, past framed black-and-white photos of champions with perfect teeth and surnames carved into silver plaques. The hallway smelled like detergent and hot electrical wiring.

At the end, near the vending machine that never took crumpled bills, she stopped.

Her hands had started shaking harder now that motion had ended. Shame moved through her in pulses: you lost, you lost, everyone saw, everyone saw. Right behind it, quieter and therefore harder to trust, another feeling: relief with teeth still in it.

Her shin had held.
Her body had held.

She hated how good that felt.

Ravi set both bags down and leaned against the cinderblock wall. For a long moment he said nothing. He watched her the way he watched weather radar at home before deciding whether to leave early for a shift: not passive, not in control, just paying attention.

"I'm sorry," she said before he could start. The words came out rough. "I should've taken that nine-eight rally earlier. I went safe and then I got jammed and then at match ball I slipped and--"

"Hey." He lifted one hand. "Stop. You don't owe me an apology for losing a quarterfinal in five."

The sentence hit her at a strange angle and nearly made her laugh, except her throat was too tight.

"I let her pick every rally's speed," Anika said. "She kept making me stop-start, and I kept arriving half a step late. If I had more fuel in my legs I could've held those long corners."

"That's true," Ravi said, then rushed the next part. "So we fix it with load. Pre-school ghosting Monday through Thursday, extra conditioning after dinner, and we keep it off the shared sheet until we get you sharper."

Anika's head snapped up. "No."

"Anika--"

"No. I'm not doing hidden sessions. That's exactly how I broke my leg." She tapped her shin with two fingers. "Clause three caps weekly load. Clause four says Tess and Jonah see the schedule before we run it."

"I'm talking thirty minutes," he said. "Targeted work."

"You always start with thirty." Her hands shook harder. "Then it's stairs. Then it's me lying about pain because I can't hit your numbers. I'm not doing that again."

Ravi opened his mouth and then shut it. He looked at the floor tile, rubbed his left ankle through his slacks, and breathed out through his nose.

"You're right," he said. "That was old control voice. It came out before I caught it."

He met her eyes again. "Let's do this clean. You adapted three different times when she shifted. You won game two by taking the ball early. You stole game three by refusing your ego. And at eight-all in the fifth you still had options. That means you're in the right match."

Anika stared at the vending machine spiral where a peanut-butter cracker pack hung crooked.

"I wanted to beat her so bad," she said. "Not just beat. I wanted to make everybody shut up about the fracture and the limits and how I used to be this and now I'm that."

Ravi nodded. "I know."

"And also"--she swallowed, angry at herself already--"also when it ended and nothing tore and I could still stand, I felt..."

She could not find a word that did not sound cowardly.

Ravi supplied the word first.

"Relieved."

She looked at him, startled.

"Yeah," she said. "Relieved. Which is disgusting because I just lost."

"No." He shook his head once. "Not disgusting. It's a data point. No--it's the data point tonight: five games, and your shin never crossed four."

He crouched to retie his shoe, then winced and rolled his left ankle before standing again. "For months we logged pain like dues: shin barks, add ghosts, call it earning it. Real training is the sheet: court minutes, recovery, next-day pain score. We skipped the sheet when I panicked." His mouth tightened. "I taught you that, and I still slip into it when I'm scared."

Her eyes burned. She turned toward the cinderblock wall so the corridor cameras and passing players would get her profile, not the full face.

"Everyone saw," she said. "The whole draw watched game five."

"Yeah," Ravi said. "They did."

No platitude about not caring what people think.

"What'd they see?" he continued. "They saw you lose in five and still keep your feet under you late. They saw you drive that eight-all ball tight to the back wall instead of spraying for highlight shots. That's film we tag rally by rally before the next court block."

"You sound like Tess."

"I can live with that."

Somewhere down the hall, a girl screamed in celebration from another court and the sound bounced hard off tile. Anika flinched, then laughed once at herself.

"I hate this feeling," she said.

"Which one?"

"All of it. The shame. The relief. The part that already wants tomorrow's session like I need it in my blood." She pressed her thumb into her palm until it hurt. "I didn't lose and think maybe this isn't for me. I lost and thought I need to get better at this exact thing that hurt me today."

Ravi's expression shifted in a way she had seen only since the mediation table: grief and pride arriving together and not canceling each other out.

"That's ambition," he said quietly. "Not costume ambition. Work-order ambition. Show up for film, do the boring reps, clock out before damage."

He picked up her bag and handed it over, but did not sling it onto her shoulder for her the way he used to when he wanted to steer where she walked.

"We stick to the agreement tonight," he said. "Food. Stretch. Ice if you need it. No secret extras."

"And no off-sheet sessions this week," Anika said. "Even if I ask for them."

"Done." He nodded once. "Tomorrow we review film for twenty minutes, pick one drill for Cora's pace shifts, and send it to Tess and Jonah before court time."

Anika exhaled. "One drill. If my shin hits four, we stop. No bargaining."

"No bargaining," Ravi said.

"Okay."

"One thing," he said.

"One thing," Anika repeated.

He nodded toward the lobby. "Before we go, there's a board by check-in with tournament stipend notes. Top four gets travel reimbursement. It's not huge, but it's something."

Money entered the air and changed its weight. Nothing abstract about it: subway fare, utility arrears, the fifteenth of the month, and Queens Oncology Billing Services printed in red on envelopes they stacked under the fruit bowl.

"How much?" Anika asked.

"Probably one-fifty, maybe two," Ravi said. "Depends what they classify."

It would not save anything, but it would matter.

Anika exhaled slowly. "Let's get it before someone says they lost the form."

That made him snort a tired laugh.

In the lobby, the stipend sheet was taped crooked beside a fundraiser flyer for academy uniforms. The woman at check-in wore cat-eye glasses and had a tiny tattoo of a rolling pin on her wrist. She stamped Anika's page and said, "Quarterfinalists get partial transit reimbursement. You'll receive it by email in three to five business days, assuming accounting isn't asleep."

"Thank you," Ravi said.

"If accounting was awake," the woman added dryly, "none of us would be here this late on Friday."

Outside, late-summer humidity grabbed them by the throat the second the glass doors opened. Anika felt sweat bloom again under her shirt within ten steps. City noise hit different after court acoustics: horns, bus brakes, one guy on a bike screaming at a rideshare, somebody's music leaking tinny bass from a backpack speaker.

Ravi and Anika walked to the subway in silence at first, and it was processing silence rather than hostile silence.

At the corner, they waited for the light with three other players and two parents discussing draw politics too loudly. Someone said Cora would cruise semis if her knee held. Someone else said Anika looked "less explosive than spring." Both things might be true. Hearing them still felt like getting pinched.

She kept her eyes on the pedestrian signal as the white walking figure changed to a red hand and then back to white again.

On the platform, a train thundered through without stopping, wind kicking wrappers and old MetroCard receipts down the tracks. Ravi stood half a step in front of her without touching, body angled to block the crowd surge in a habit from childhood that was both protective and practical.

Anika leaned back against the tiled column and did a stealth body scan like Tess had taught her: jaw, neck, shoulders, ribs, hips, shin.

Everything felt tender but stable, with no hot spike and no electric pull.

Relief came again, annoyingly pure this time.

With it came shame's twin: if you're relieved, maybe you wanted an excuse. Maybe you're soft now. Maybe Cora was right about the leash.

She shook her head once as if clearing water from her ears.

Ravi glanced over. "What?"

"Nothing."

He waited.

"I was thinking about that thing Cora said before the match," Anika admitted. "About the leash."

"She was poking," Ravi said.

"Yeah, but she wasn't totally wrong. I can feel the missing volume late." Anika stared at her shoes. "I hate that she could feel it too."

"Any good opponent will feel what you're missing," he said. "So we price it in on the sheet: one pace-endurance block, signed off, and stop at four even if you hate it. Today she paid for games two and three." He looked down the tracks, then back at her. "Next time we make game four cost her too."

The train finally came, already crowded. They squeezed into a corner by the door. Anika's shoulder touched the plexiglass map, and every station stop shifted strangers against her forearms, sweat and detergent and cheap cologne mixing into one city smell.

Across from her, a little boy in a Mets cap watched her racquet bag like it held a sword.

"Did you win?" he asked bluntly.

His mother hissed, "Mateo."

Anika surprised herself by answering. "I lost in five."

The boy considered this with solemn seriousness. "Five is a lot," he said.

"Yeah," Anika said. "That's a lot."

He nodded like that solved something and went back to pressing his face to the window.

At Queensboro Plaza, the car lurched hard and Ravi grabbed the overhead rail just before his weight pitched into her. His left ankle bent slightly. "Fuck," he said, sharp enough that the boy in the Mets cap snapped his head around. Ravi set his foot flat again and reset his stance.

"You okay?" she asked.

"Yeah," he said. "Old stupid ankle."

They rode the rest of the way mostly quiet, except for Ravi asking once if she wanted food from the cart by their station or leftovers at home.

"Cart," she said. "Egg and cheese. And one of those bad orange drinks."

"Hydration science is crying," he said, deadpan.

"Hydration science can wait."

When they got off, evening rain had started in thin diagonal needles, warm and sticky. The bus shelter was packed, so they walked the fifteen minutes to the apartment under one folding umbrella that leaked at the seam. By the time they climbed the walk-up stairs, both had wet cuffs and damp hair.

In the kitchen, the smell of mustard seed and old tea hit her first, familiar and slightly sour. Ravi put her bag by the table and went straight for the freezer pack without saying anything performative about care. He wrapped ice in a dish towel and set it within reach.

She sat, pulled up her pant leg, and laid the cold against her right shin.

Pain stayed mild, and it remained mild even as the ice bit deeper.

Ravi cracked eggs into a skillet and tore open two cheap rolls. Oil hissed. Rain tapped the fire escape outside. Their silence filled with domestic sounds that had nothing to do with winning.

After they ate, Anika opened her notebook. She chose the private ledger from chapter three, not the school one, with a bent cover and paper soft at the edges from sweat and being shoved into bags.
She wrote the date and then wrote, Regional quarterfinal, followed by Result: loss, 2-3.
She paused with the pen hovering and then wrote two new lines under the score: Body intact. Wanted more anyway.

She let the words sit there, ugly and true, no moral pinned to them.

From the sink, Ravi rinsed plates and said without turning, "Lights out by ten-thirty."

"Yeah," she said.

"And if you wake up wanting to do ghosting at midnight, wake me instead."

She almost laughed. "You'd hate that."

"I'd hate you rebreaking your leg more."

He turned then, drying his hands on a frayed towel, and met her eyes.

"We'll do this the hard way," he said. "Slow enough to keep you in one piece."

The sentence landed flat and practical. It offered a schedule, not salvation. It offered work, not trophies.

It sounded like work.

Anika looked down at the notebook line again, where she had written that she wanted more anyway.

Outside, a siren rose and faded. Upstairs, somebody dragged a chair across bare floor. The apartment felt small and humid and real.

She pressed the ice tighter to her shin until the ache dulled, then set a timer for twelve minutes and let it run all the way down.
When it chirped, she set Maya's cracked watch on the milk-crate nightstand and left the alarm off.


# Chapter 16

At 7:43 on Saturday, with the alarm still off, Anika woke anyway.

No alarm, no text from Ravi with a split time, no shoes lined by the door like an accusation. Her right shin still gave its old warning when she swung out of bed, a thin hot thread along the bone, but the pain settled after two steps. Humidity pushed through the window screen. Someone in the next building had already started frying onions, and the smell mixed with the detergent steam from the laundromat vent below.

She checked Maya's watch out of habit. The plastic strap had cracked at the third hole months ago, and she had reinforced the back with clear tape that had gone cloudy at the edges. 7:43 blinked in gray block numbers. The seconds ticked loud enough in her head to become counting, so she shut it down and sat on her hands until the urge passed.

In the kitchen, cabinet doors opened and shut in the wrong rhythm. Ravi never moved quietly when he was trying to look quiet. Metal hit metal, then the high whine of the kettle.

Anika stood in the doorway. Ravi had not shaved. He wore his depot polo untucked and a pair of old shorts, and the left ankle he used to pretend was fine had wrapped itself in a black elastic brace.

"You're up," he said, like he had not been listening for her floorboards.

"Yeah."

He held up a dented steel bowl. "I found Amma's lemon rice card. Want to do that for lunch?"

She blinked once. Twice. "Today?"

"It's Saturday." He shrugged. "We need lunch anyway."

They were work words and safe words. She recognized the move and felt herself matching it.

"We're low on curry leaves," she said. "And peanuts."

"And lemons," he said. "We'll go down to Patel's. Ten minutes."

Neither of them said no drills, no video review, no bracket talk. The omission sat between them like a third person who knew all their passwords.

They left at eight-thirty with two tote bags and exact cash folded into Ravi's wallet in stiff, sorted stacks. The stairwell smelled like floor cleaner and wet shoes. On the second-floor landing, Mrs. Alvarez was kneeling beside her potted basil, pinching dead tips with two pink clips still in her hair from the night shift.

"Look at this child, up at a human hour for once," she called, wiping her hands on her Knicks T-shirt. "My Tito slept in more than you."

Her dog, eight pounds of jaw and opinion, stood in a yellow raincoat although it was not raining and barked once at Ravi's ankle brace.

Anika crouched and let Tito sniff her fingers. "Morning, boss."

"He likes you now because you dropped chicken last week," Mrs. Alvarez said. "That's not love. That's strategy."

Ravi huffed through his nose and tugged once at the ankle brace strap. "Same as Tito's owner."

"Please. I'm pure romance and unpaid overtime." Mrs. Alvarez stood with a hand against the wall. "You making your wife's rice?"

Ravi lifted the steel bowl in a small salute.

"Use fresh lemons. Not that bottled nonsense. Maya would've cursed you out."

"I know," Ravi said quietly.

Mrs. Alvarez looked at Anika, then back at Ravi, like checking whether either of them would break if touched too hard. "If you have extra, bring me one spoon. My son still refuses turmeric like he's allergic to joy."

"We'll bring," Anika said.

Outside, the block had gone from early quiet to neighborhood loud. Two boys chalked goalposts on the sidewalk and argued over whether the curb counted as in bounds. A halal cart hissed beside the pharmacy gate. Above the 7 train tracks, pigeons shifted in synchronized waves before settling, as if an invisible hand had pressed pause.

Ravi and Anika walked without rushing. That was new enough to feel strange in her body. Her stride kept trying to lengthen, and each time she dragged it back. At the corner, the crossing light flashed white, then red hand, and she waited through the full cycle instead of darting.

At Patel's Market, the air conditioner fought a losing battle against wet produce and human breath. Mr. Patel stood at the register with a Mets cap pinned at the brim where the fabric had torn; he had fixed it with a tiny gold safety pin shaped like a fish, an old gift from his daughter. He was lining up scratch-off lottery tickets by color like they were spice jars.

"Champ and coach," he said when they came in. "No court this morning?"

Anika reached for a basket before Ravi could answer. "We're making lemon rice."

Mr. Patel put one hand over his heart. "Then you require proper lemons. Bin three, not bin one. Bin one tastes like disappointment."
