In the back aisle, Ravi chose onions by weight, pressing each one with his thumb. Anika checked the curry leaf packets for black spots. She held up two brands of peanuts and Ravi said, "Cheaper one has more salt. We rinse if needed."

"You can just buy the right one."

"I can buy both if you found oil money behind the sofa."

He said it dry, half-joke, but the debt numbers still clicked in her head: 38,400. 1,860. Late fees crawling like ants. She hated how fast her mind could find the list.

"I've got twenty from tutoring Devon in math," she said. "Use that."

Ravi stopped with an onion in his hand. "Keep your money."

"It's not my money if this is lunch for everyone."

"It's your money. Keep it."

Mr. Patel appeared at the end of the aisle, holding a crate of tomatoes against his stomach. "If you two are fighting over twenty dollars, take my cilantro free and restore peace."

"We're not fighting," Ravi said.

Mr. Patel raised his eyebrows in a way that said he had watched families argue over less and also over everything. "Then I'm giving free cilantro to celebrate your not-fight."

They took the cilantro.

At the counter, Ravi peeled bills from the correct stack and counted twice before handing them over. His thumb had a fresh split at the nail, a clean line where skin had cracked from soap and cold water and maybe from gripping steering wheels too long at night. Anika looked away before he could see her looking.

Back on the block, they passed the laundromat where Jorge, the owner, sat on an upside-down detergent bucket smoking clove cigarettes while his machines thumped behind him. Jorge had tattooed two tiny socks on his left wrist after his twins were born and still rolled his sleeve up to show people when business was slow.

"Big cooking today?" he called.

"Lemon rice," Anika said.

Jorge whistled. "Save me from my own empanadas."

"No promises," Ravi said.

At home, the kitchen shrank around them the way it always did when both tried to work at once. Counter on one side, stove on the other, just enough space between for one person and one argument. The recipe card lived in a jar by the window, thick paper soft at the edges from oil fingers. Maya had written it in blue pen with slanted letters and three underlines under "LOW HEAT."

Anika read aloud to prove she could do it without help. "'One and a half cups basmati. Wash until water runs clear. Do not bruise curry leaves when tearing.'"

Ravi placed the rice canister on the counter. "You do rice and lemon. I'll do tempering."

"I can do tempering."

"You can. I'm saying I want to."

There it was again: request dressed as instruction. She took a breath and chose not to slice it apart yet.

She poured rice into the steel bowl and ran cold water over it. The first rinse turned the water chalky white. The second was less cloudy. The third was mostly clear. She rubbed the grains lightly between her fingers, hearing Maya's voice in memory and not memory, two channels slightly out of sync: not too hard, you'll break it, this is rice not gravel.

Ravi measured oil into the kadai. When it heated, mustard seeds started to pop like static. He added chana dal, urad dal, dried red chiles snapped in half. The curry leaves hit hot oil and exploded in a sharp green crackle that made both of them jump and then laugh at themselves.

"Still sounds like gunfire," Ravi said.

"It scared Amma every time too. She'd pretend it didn't."

"It would scare her, then she'd scare me by laughing while holding hot oil."

"She did that thing with her wrist so it wouldn't splatter."

Anika copied the flicking wrist motion in the air. Ravi watched, then handed her the turmeric without speaking.

They moved around each other in short practiced arcs, shoulder almost touching, then not. Ravi cut onions too fine and Anika told him so. He said fine onions disappear better. She said disappearing was not always the goal. He gave her a look she could not read and kept cutting.

The rice went into the pressure cooker. They waited through the hiss.

While they shelled roasted peanuts, Anika's phone buzzed on the fridge. Zoe, four messages in a row.

`u alive?`
`my brother shaved one eyebrow for ten dollars`
`mrs p looked at him for thirty full seconds and said "choices are data"`
`also school rumor: vice principal might get replaced by some ex-marine`

Anika laughed before she could swallow it.

Ravi glanced up. "Good news?"

"Zoe's brother did cosmetic self-destruction for money."

"Entrepreneur."

"Dumb entrepreneur."

She typed with peanut salt on her fingers.

`alive. making lemon rice.`
`tell your brother symmetry matters`

Zoe sent back a photo of one tragic eyebrow and a proud grin.

Ravi leaned against the sink. "How's Zoe doing with the new math teacher? The one who speaks like a podcast?"

"Mr. Kline. He says 'circle back' every ten minutes. If he says synergy one more time we're staging a coup."

"Please don't get suspended while staging your coup."

"We're not that organized."

He flicked peanut salt from his fingertips into the sink. "And Devon? Still asking you for homework help?"

"Yeah. He thinks fractions are a conspiracy."

"He's right about many conspiracies, maybe not that one."

They stayed there longer than the recipe required, talking about people who had nothing to do with ladders or rankings or injury protocols. The super's daughter had painted a mural of mangoes on the back alley wall and got yelled at, then got permission when everyone liked it. The bodega switched bread suppliers and half the block had opinions. Mrs. Alvarez was threatening to report the landlord again because the hallway lights died every third night and she was done climbing with grocery bags in the dark.

It felt almost easy until Ravi said, "High school tours start in a month. We should map weekends now before they fill."

Anika's shoulders tightened before her mind caught up. Map weekends. The old script sliding in with clean shoes.

"We can do school tours," she said carefully. "I'm not mapping every weekend."

"Not every weekend. Just the key ones."

"Key for what?"

He tossed peanut skins into the trash and missed. "For the calendar: school tours, training blocks we agreed to, your rest day, my shifts."

"That's not an answer."

Ravi's jaw worked once. "For the week not swallowing us whole."

There it was. Everything used to mean squash, and squash used to mean obedience.

She set the lemon on the cutting board and pressed the knife down harder than she meant to. Juice sprayed onto her wrist, into the small old scar near her thumb.

"We signed terms," she said. "Rest day stays a rest day. No surprise sessions. No hidden sparring. No turning school into dead time between drills."

"I'm not saying that."

"You always start with 'I'm not saying that.' Then somehow we end up there."

He reached for the salt and found none because he had put it back in the wrong cabinet. He opened three doors before noticing.

"I'm trying to plan like a responsible parent," he said, voice flattening into depot tone. "Tournaments don't care about our feelings. Applications don't pause because we need emotional weather reports."

She laughed once, short and sharp. "See? That's exactly it. I said school tours and you heard tournaments."

"I heard your future."

"You heard your panic."

The cooker clicked off with a metallic sigh. Neither moved.

Ravi wiped his hands on a dish towel, folded it into a square, and pulled it open again. "Don't talk to me like I'm your opponent."

"Then stop coaching me in my kitchen."

He looked at her hard at that, at the claim inside the pronoun. My kitchen. Not his. Not the house's. The one place where Maya had let recipes matter more than outcomes.

Anika lifted the cooker lid and steam fogged her glasses. She blinked through it, eyes stinging from onion and lemon and old anger.

"I'll finish," she said.

"Anika..."

"I said I'll finish."

She expected him to argue. Instead he took his phone from the counter, unlocked it, and opened the utility payment screen. A red banner at the top read FINAL NOTICE in all caps.

"I've gotta pay this before noon cutoff," he said. "But I'm not walking out in the middle of this again."

He set the phone between them like evidence and moved back to the stove. "Finish the tempering with me."

The request scraped, but it was not a command. Anika added asafoetida, peanuts, turmeric, rice, salt, lemon juice, then tossed everything on low heat with a wooden spoon while Ravi held the pan steady. Her hands knew the order even when her chest felt too tight.

The rice changed color slowly, white to yellow to that exact bright not-neon shade Maya called festival yellow. Steam lifted lemon and curry and roasted nut. For one beat Anika was nine again, feet swinging off a chair while Maya tapped the spoon against the pan.

Taste, then adjust, Maya had said. Don't commit to a dish before tasting.

Anika tasted. Needed more salt. Needed one more lemon half. Needed less anger, which was not measurable and refused adjustment.

Ravi killed the flame and set plates on the table without sitting. "Can I ask one thing without you hearing a trap?"

"Try."

"Fall's coming. School gets harder. Invitational prep starts soon. I want to know what you want your week to look like. Not what you think I want. Yours."

She felt the old reflex rise: give numbers, give a grid, minimize blast radius. Monday ninety, Tuesday active rest, Wednesday ghosting.

Instead she said, "I want one day where my body isn't a project."

Ravi looked down.

"And I want school to be school. Not recovery between sessions." She gripped the underside of her chair hard enough to ache. "I want to see Zoe without calculating what it costs me. I want to play because I want to play, not because the debt counter is screaming."

Ravi gave a short laugh that hurt to hear. "Debt counter is screaming at me too, in case that wasn't clear."

"You think I don't see that?" She tapped the phone with the red notice, then the stack of envelopes by the fruit bowl. "You count dollars. I count you pretending you're not exhausted. Same house."

He stood very still. "I don't think you see all of it."

"Then show me without turning it into more drills."

He carried both plates to the sink in one trip, too fast, shoulder clipping the cabinet. Water ran full force, then stopped. He came back and stayed standing, one hand on the chair back like he needed it.

"When you were at Priya's," he said, "I didn't sleep in our bedroom for four nights. I sat in the kitchen chair and watched the hallway. Like you were five and might wander out looking for water."

Anika stayed still.

"I kept thinking if I'd had better rules, tighter rules, none of this would've happened. The fracture. You leaving. Everything. So I made more rules in my head. More hours, more control, more structure. Like structure could bargain with death."

The last word landed heavy and plain. He said death, not hospital complications or what happened to your mother.

He sat again because his knees seemed to decide for him. "You want plain? Here. I used your training to avoid my grief. I turned your life into timers and color-coded boxes on the fridge because if I stopped counting reps I had to count days since Maya stopped breathing, and I couldn't do that. I didn't know how."

Anika's chest tightened so fast she thought for a second she might throw up. She had wanted him to say it for months and now that he had, the room felt tilted.

Ravi kept going, voice rougher. "I told myself it was discipline, opportunity, protection. Some of it was. Some of it was me being terrified and calling it coaching. I was scared you'd break the way she broke. I was scared I'd fail you both. I was scared if you weren't in front of me every hour, something would take you too."

He pressed his thumb into the split nail until it whitened. "That's not an excuse. It's the truth."

Anika heard herself ask, "Why now?"

"Because you asked me to stop hiding behind calendars and checklists, and you were right." He gave a short laugh with no humor in it. "And because I almost said 'we should increase volume' three times today while cutting onions. I can hear myself doing it. I don't want to keep doing it."

The words should have felt like relief. Instead her stomach cinched, waiting for the correction that usually came after his soft voice.

Silence sat with them. Not the old punishment silence. This one had edges but also room.

Anika folded and unfolded her napkin until the paper edge nicked her thumb. She counted the folds out of habit: four, eight, sixteen. Counting always tried to turn fear into math. "I can't be your anti-grief plan."

Ravi swallowed. "You're not. You're my kid, and I made you carry that weight."

"You don't get to panic and make it my problem."

"I don't. If I start spinning out, I call Priya or Tess. I don't dump it on you."

"You said that before."

Her calf twitched under the table, braced for the old argument sprint.

He looked past her shoulder at the fridge clips, then back at her. "Then put conditions on me."

She looked at him for a long second. His face had gone smaller somehow, like stress had eaten whatever posture he used at work. She remembered him at the mediation table promising with clean sentences and measured breath, and she remembered his voice on the morning she left for Priya, sharp enough to use Maya like a lever. Both were true men sitting in the same chair.

"Okay," she said. "Extra training is a question. You ask once. I answer once. No sneaking it in with words like maybe or just."

He nodded. "I ask once. If you say no, it's no."

"If I say my shin hurts, we stop. You get one clarifying question, then we stop. No bargaining me out of my own leg."

The hot thread along her tibia flared at the words, phantom and real.

His fingers tightened on the chair back. "One question, then stop."

"No using Amma's name to win arguments. If you miss her, say you miss her. Don't put words in her mouth to corner me."

The split in his thumbnail reopened; a thin red line surfaced where he'd been pressing it.

"I did that," he said. "I won't do it again."

"Rest day stays rest day. Stretching is fine. Drills aren't."

Saying it made her throat tighten; she had spent too many fake rest days waiting for a knock and a surprise plan.

He set his bleeding thumb flat on the table and left it there. "Rest day is rest day."

"And no side deals. Any schedule change, sponsor ask, or extra session goes in a group text with me and Priya before it becomes real."

Ravi flinched, then steadied. "Me, you, Priya. Group text first."

She exhaled, but the hard part was still there. "If pain rules or surprise-training rules slip, I go to Priya's that night."

"For one bad sentence?"

"For pain or surprise training, yes. For words, you get one in-the-moment correction. If you push past it, I'm gone."

He stared at the table, then at her, swallowing once. "I hate that this has to be a rule."

"It has to be."

"Okay." He pressed his palm over the wet ring his glass had left, as if pinning himself in place. His voice came out scraped raw. "If I slip, you leave. No debate."

"It's not a threat. It's the plan."

"I hear you."

He leaned back and covered his eyes with the heel of his hand for three seconds, maybe four. When he lowered it, the skin under his eyes was mottled red and one tear had dried into his stubble. His next breath snagged halfway in his chest before he forced it out through his teeth.

"I don't know how to grieve correctly," he said.

Anika almost said nobody does, but the sentence felt like a poster in a counselor's office, too clean for this table.

"You could start by not scheduling over it," she said. "Like today."

He rubbed at his mouth with his wrist and let out a thin breath. "Like today."

She stood and carried the mango plate to the sink. He moved to take it.

"I got it," she said.

His hand hovered a second, then dropped.

At the sink, water ran cool over her wrists. Her hands trembled, then steadied. Ravi brought the rice pot over and stopped half a step away, waiting.

"Left side's clear," she said after a beat.

They rinsed in silence, careful with elbows, passing spoons by setting them down instead of handing them over.

After dishes, Anika packed leftovers into mismatched containers. She wrote names on blue painter's tape: `ALVAREZ`, `JORGE`, `HOME`.

Ravi reached for a lid, missed the groove, and tried again.

"That one doesn't fit," she said.

"I know." He held it there anyway for one more second. "Brain lag."

He tried a normal tone. "Zoe still planning a revolution against Mr. Kline?"

"Yeah."

"How's the revolution budget?"

Anika snapped the `HOME` lid shut hard enough to pop the corner seam. "Five dollars and bad intentions."

The line sat there without either of them smiling.

Ravi lined the containers up military straight, caught himself, and dragged the middle one crooked with two fingers.

Anika let out a breath that was almost a laugh. "You don't have to perform casual."

"I know." He took the container marked `ALVAREZ`. "Want to drop this with me?"

She considered. Leaving the apartment together could feel like pretending everything was fixed. Staying could feel like punishment. She chose movement.

"Yeah."

In the hall, the light on the third-floor landing had burned out again. They climbed by phone flashlight, shoulder to shoulder but not touching. Mrs. Alvarez opened on the second knock, wearing reading glasses at the end of her nose and holding a crossword book where she filled answers in blue gel pen and then circled clues she hated in red.

"My heroes," she said, taking the container. "If this is bland, I'm filing a complaint."

"It isn't bland," Anika said. "I policed the salt myself."

Mrs. Alvarez peered around her at Ravi. "You look less haunted today."

Ravi rubbed the back of his neck, eyes still red-rimmed. "Work in progress."

"Aren't we all." She tapped the crossword with her pen. "Tell your daughter the clue for seven down is wrong. 'Mythic bird, four letters.' They printed PHNX. That's not a word."

"Ask Zoe," Anika said. "She'll invent one and charge a consulting fee."

Mrs. Alvarez snorted. Ravi did too, brief and surprised, like the sound had escaped him.

Back downstairs, they dropped the second container at the laundromat. Jorge accepted it like a ceremonial object and swore he would trade only one shirt this week for emergency cash because "I've got nutrients now." This time Anika laughed once, then rubbed her face and went quiet again.

On the way back, Ravi ducked into the utility kiosk off 82nd while Anika waited by the pharmacy gate, kicking a cracked bottle cap with the toe of her sneaker. He came out three minutes later with a damp receipt folded in his fist.

"Paid enough to keep service on this week," he said. "It's not fixed. It's just on."

"Good," Anika said. "I've got this dumb volcano for science due Tuesday, and I'm not building that thing by flashlight."

By the time they got home, afternoon had softened toward evening. Sunlight hit the opposite building and bounced into their kitchen in a copper strip across the table. The air smelled like lemon and dish soap and the faint rubber note from the hallway mats.

Ravi took the utility receipt from his pocket and stuck it to the fridge with the old Eiffel Tower magnet Maya hated. Below it, the signed training agreement still held in place by two clips.

He tore a blank index card from the junk drawer pad and wrote in block letters:

`SUNDAY 8:00 PM CHECK-IN (ME / ANIKA / PRIYA)`
`NO EXTRA TRAINING OR SPONSOR YESSES OUTSIDE GROUP THREAD`

He taped it under the agreement and passed Anika the marker. "Add your line."

She wrote beneath his lines:

`PAIN >= 4: WE STOP.`
`SURPRISE SESSION = I GO TO PRIYA'S.`

"Even if it's only twenty minutes before school," she said. "No, especially then."

Ravi nodded, unlocked his phone, and opened a three-person text thread. "If I start that 'just one extra' speech, cut me off. If I push—if I start spinning, call Priya."

`Starting tonight: all schedule, training, and sponsor changes go here first. Weekly call Sunday 8 PM.`

Priya answered before he set the phone down.

`Seen. Sunday at 8. No freelancing.`

"Want tea?" he asked.

"Yeah."

He filled the kettle. "Strong?"

"Strong."

While water heated, she opened her notebook from chapter three, the one with columns for reps and pain and memory that had changed shape over months. She turned to a blank page and wrote the date. No split times. No weight targets. Just:

`Saturday. Lemon rice.`

She waited one breath and added:

`He said the word death.`

Then, below that:

`I stayed.`
`Group thread lit up at 8:17.`

Ravi set a mug by her elbow and did not read over her shoulder. He sat across from her, both hands around his own cup, and for several minutes they drank in the same room without pretending the hard parts were finished.

At 9:07, his rideshare alarm buzzed from the counter. A red surge map lit the cracked screen.

He muted it and started wrapping the black brace tighter around his left ankle.

"You said till eleven," Anika said.

"Till eleven," he said. "No ghost shift."

He took his keys and left with the tea barely half-finished.

The quick exit scraped at her, old fear translating any closed door into unpaid bills and pressure she would be asked to absorb.

When the door shut, Anika pressed her thumb against her shin until the dull pulse passed. On the fridge, under FINAL NOTICE, the receipt still showed $1,240.12 remaining.

The number made her jaw clench. Her brain offered the old bargain instantly: extra sessions, extra wins, extra money, as if effort could meter electricity. She kept her thumb on bone until the pulse slowed and let the bargain pass.


# Chapter 17

A week after the lemon-rice Saturday she had written down in her notebook, and a week after the group thread lit up at 8:17, Anika stepped out of the Lexington Avenue station with socks already damp from a leak in somebody else's umbrella and scorched-metal brake dust clinging to the inside of her jacket. Midtown wind pressed cold through the fabric anyway. The private club sat half a block down, all glass and brushed steel, with a doorman who held the door as if everybody coming in had earned that kind of welcome. Anika hated that she noticed it. She hated that she wanted it.

Inside, the air hit her like a refrigerator. The lobby floor had the same pale shine as the hospital corridor outside Maya's oncology wing, and she felt the old jolt before she could stop it. She looked down at her wrist. Maya's digital watch blinked 8:13 in gray block numbers. The strap had cracked at the third hole months ago, and Anika had reinforced the back with clear tape that had gone cloudy at the edges. She rubbed it once with her thumb, then clipped her bag shut.

Ravi was already at the registration table, talking to a volunteer in a tournament polo two sizes too big for him. Ravi kept shifting his weight off his left ankle, the way he always did when it stiffened in cold weather. He had worn his good navy shirt, the one he ironed with a dish towel because the iron plate stuck. The collar was still a little crooked. He turned when she came in, and he started to say something sharp and practical, then checked himself and lifted his chin toward the courts instead.

"You ate?" he asked.

"Half a bagel on the train. Banana too." She opened her bag to show the peel folded inside a sandwich bag because he still asked for evidence when he got anxious.

He gave one fast nod. "Warm-up court is three. Jonah's inside. You have twenty-two minutes. We go through length first, then front-court holds."

It would have been a command six months ago, maybe even six weeks ago. This morning it sounded like a request dressed as logistics. Anika heard the difference and kept her eyes on the court map while she answered.

"I want five minutes alone before I hit." She said it like she was reading from a list. "No talking. Just breathing and movement."

Ravi's mouth tightened. For a second she thought he would push. Then he said, "Okay. Five. Then work." He glanced at her right leg before he could stop himself. "If anything in your shin lights up, you tell me right away."

"I know the agreement." Her voice came out flatter than she meant. "I know."

At court three, Jonah Feld stood by the back wall with his ball bucket and a travel mug that said WORLD'S OKAYEST UNCLE in chipped white letters. He had replaced his usual wrist tape with bright green kinesiology strips that zigzagged up his forearm like somebody had drawn lightning on him with a marker.

"Morning," he said. "You look like you slept zero hours and one minute."

"I got four." She started lunges without waiting. "Maybe four and a half."

"Luxury." He rolled a ball underhand to her forehand. "Today you don't chase every miracle pickup. If the rally is dead, let it die. We need clean points, not movie points."

Ravi stayed outside the glass while she did her first five minutes alone. He did not wave. He just stood with his hands in his jacket pockets, rocking once on that stiff ankle, reading the draw sheet taped to the wall even though he already knew it by heart.

The draw sheet read: Semifinal, Court One, Anika Iyer versus Cora Bell.

Cora walked in three minutes later with her academy duffel, immaculate as always, knee sleeve black against her left calf. The zipper on her bag had a tiny silver charm shaped like a dachshund. Cora touched it with her index finger before she set the bag down, a quick superstitious tap that looked private enough to be embarrassing if anyone teased her for it. Through the glass she held Anika's gaze for one short nod.

"You good?" Cora asked when they crossed at the T.

"Yeah," Anika said.

"Cool. Let's not make this ugly." Cora's public voice was polished, almost bored, but her cheeks were pink with adrenaline and she kept smoothing one flyaway hair behind her ear every few seconds.

The club had filled by nine. Parents in long coats, academy kids in matching warmups, two men with sponsor lanyards and shoes that had never seen rain. A printed banner hung near court one: NORTH ATLANTIC ELITE PATHWAY SHOWCASE. Anika had memorized what came next if she won today: year-round academy slot, travel support, coaching subsidy, a room at summer camp that people in Queens talked about like it was an address in another country. She had not asked how much the package was worth. She had memorized the household totals anyway, enough to feel them in her molars.

The oncology balance sat at thirty-eight thousand four hundred. The utility arrears sat at one thousand two hundred forty dollars and twelve cents, even after the August payment. The fifteenth of every month felt like a countdown clock. If she reached the final and signed, Ravi could drop one overtime shift, maybe two. Maybe his eyes would stop looking like he had been awake for years.

The referee called two minutes. Anika bounced on the balls of her feet and listened to the court: the squeak of Cora's shoes, the distant metallic rattle from the old HVAC vents even in this polished place, the hollow thud of another match three courts over. She pressed Maya's watchband into the heel of her palm, found the white service-box line with her eyes, and matched her breath to the vent rattle until panic thinned.

First game started fast. Cora served tight to the wall, heavy and precise, trying to pin Anika deep and force lazy boasts. Anika answered with length that kissed the side wall and died near the back nick. They traded points in stretches that felt like held breath: thirty-two shots, twenty-eight, forty-one, both of them moving so hard their shirts darkened at the spine. At 6-6 Cora sent a crosscourt that sat up half an inch too high, and Anika stepped forward and carved a backhand volley drop so soft the ball looked surprised to be on the floor.

Ravi did not clap big. He pressed his knuckles once to his mouth and sat down.

At 9-8, Cora pushed a brutal rally, dragging Anika corner to corner until Anika felt her lungs go metallic. She heard Jonah in her head anyway: if the rally is dead, let it die. Cora guessed wrong on a hold, committed early to the backhand corner, and Anika flicked the ball short to the opposite front with her wrist almost at full extension for a clean winner. Then she struck a forehand drive that Cora scraped up too late. 11-8.

Anika walked to the back glass between games and put both palms against it, cool and smooth, letting the sting in her skin pin her in the present. She watched her own reflection breathe. She looked eleven in the glass, not like the hard silhouette she carried around in her head.

Ravi leaned in when she sat.

"Good width," he said. "Her middle is opening. Keep your first step forward when she floats. Don't gift her the T."

"My timing's there," Anika said. Her heart was hammering so hard she could hear blood in her ears. "My right calf is twitchy but okay."

He did not pretend not to hear that. "Zero to ten?"

"One. Maybe two. Calf, not shin."

"Track it. Tell me if it moves."

Second game was the best squash Anika had ever played in front of people who mattered. Cora raised her level and stopped missing easy balls. They built points like arguments, each shot forcing the next concession. Cora changed pace with ugly little float balls that dropped dead in the back corners, then ripped flat drives when Anika edged too far forward. Anika answered by taking the ball early, stepping in and cutting angles so sharp the crowd made that involuntary noise people made when they were trying not to cheer for one kid over another.

At 10-9, game ball Anika, Cora saved with a backhand nick that rattled the tin and rolled out to a murmur. At 10-10, Cora got her own game ball after a fifty-shot rally that left both of them bent over their rackets. Anika stared at the service box, then looked up and saw sponsor lanyards in the front row, faces blank as clipboards.

She served body line, got a weak return, and punished the loose ball to the back. 11-10. Next rally Cora attacked first and Anika absorbed, absorbed, absorbed, then lifted a desperate lob that kissed the side wall and stayed in. Cora sprinted, reached, clipped the ball high. Stroke, Anika. 12-10.

Ravi stood this time, both hands on the rail, and forgot to hide what was on his face. Pride came through him so clean it was almost frightening. Not because it was new. Because she had gone so long without seeing it outside of results sheets.

Cora reset fast in game three. She shortened points, hunted Anika's backhand volley, and started testing the front corners with perfect weight. Anika got half a step late twice and paid for it. At 3-3 she lunged deep to the forehand and felt a small live wire flick against the inside edge of her right shin. It vanished in two strides. She told herself calf. She told herself court sting. She fixed on the front wall and kept moving.

Cora took the game 11-6 with clinical patience. At 8-5 she held a drive for just a beat and sent it wrong-footing across. At 10-6 she served into the body and punished the loose return. There was no drama and no fist pump. She just turned and walked to the back, smoothing that flyaway hair behind her ear again, face blank the way academy kids learned to keep it.

Between games, Anika sat and tightened her right shoe even though it was already tight.

Ravi crouched so his face lined up with hers. "Talk."

"It's there," she said, eyes on the knot. "Right shin. Not sharp all the time. Flickers when I push off deep lunges."

"Number."

"Three sitting. Four on hard push."

He did not answer immediately. His focus flicked to the officials' table where the tournament physio sat with an iPad and an orange water bottle covered in peeling stickers. Tess Keating was not supposed to be here today; she had texted earlier that another clinician would staff the event. Then his attention returned to Anika.

"Can you keep your mechanics clean?"

"Yeah." She swallowed. "I can."

"If it jumps, we stop and call medical," he said.

She nodded, and then she hated that she had nodded because nodding made it real.

Game four started with rain drilling against the high windows and crowd noise turning thick. Anika won the first two points on length. Cora answered with a hold-and-flick that left Anika sliding. 2-1. 2-2. At 3-2 Anika attacked short and Cora scrambled, lifted, recovered. The rally stretched. Thirty shots. Thirty-five. Anika moved into the back right corner for a ball dropping tight to the wall and pushed off hard to recover the T.

Pain hit this time like a screwdriver driven straight into bone.

Her right leg buckled half an inch. She still reached the middle. She still won the rally because Cora hit the tin on the next ball. The point counted. The pain did not leave.

She walked to receive serve and every step sent a bright pulse up her shin. It was not muscle pain and it was not calf pain. Bone pain had its own personality. It was clean and mean and specific.

Cora served. Anika tried to plant and drive. Her foot landed a fraction off and the shock traveled up the same line, shin to knee to hip. She hit short. Cora punished. 3-3.

On the next rally Anika opened with decent length, then got dragged front right. She lunged, felt the edge of the world go white for a second, and bailed out with a weak boast. Cora killed the ball in two shots. 4-3 Cora.

Ravi stood up so fast his chair scraped loud enough for half the front row to turn.

Anika bounced once at the service box and bit the inside of her cheek until salt hit her tongue.

If she withdrew now, the offer packet died. She had heard enough hallway talk to know that. The sponsor wanted finalists, not stories. No sponsor meant no academy subsidy. No subsidy meant monthly dues at clubs they could not afford. It meant Ravi back on the late-night app-driving loop, drinking gas-station coffee and pretending he was not swerving with fatigue. It meant the fifteenth again and the oncology portal open on his cracked phone screen, minimum payment blinking red.

Debt totals flashed as hard facts, not comfort: thirty-eight thousand four hundred, one thousand two hundred forty and twelve cents, and bus salary at three thousand nine hundred if no shifts were missed. Overtime was variable. Everything was variable except what was owed.

Cora served again, deep to backhand. Anika got there late and framed the return high, and the ball went down. 5-3.

The referee called "Hand out" and held her in his stare a beat too long.

Ravi pressed both palms to the glass and mouthed, `Talk.`

Anika toweled off and went to the back wall. Her throat felt full of tin. She could keep playing. She could probably steal this game if she shortened points and accepted ugly squash. She could also hear Tess from months ago, clinical and maddeningly calm: when bone pain returns at impact, you don't negotiate with it.

Maya's letter from Locker 214 had one line she had read so often the paper had gone soft at the fold. I don't want your life to become a punishment you're good at.

She checked Ravi once, then faced the referee. "Medical timeout," she said, and her voice came out thin.

The court door opened. The tournament physio jogged in with a badge that read DR. ELLA MORALES. She had a crescent of turmeric stain near the cuff of her white fleece, like she had eaten between matches and wiped her hand on the wrong sleeve. Her black curls were twisted into a pencil-held bun that kept trying to collapse.

"Tell me where and when," Morales said, kneeling.

"Right tibia," Anika said. "Medial side. Pain on push-off and impact. Started as flickers game three. Sharp now."

"Scale."

"Three at rest. Seven when I lunge hard."

Morales pressed along the shin with two fingers, precise and merciless. Anika hissed at one spot halfway down.

"There?"

"Yeah."

"Hop test." Morales steadied her elbow. "Single-leg, right side. One hop."

Anika tried. The first hop landed and she sucked air through her teeth so hard she tasted metal. She could not bring herself to do a second.

Morales glanced toward the officials' table, then back to Anika. Her tone stayed level. "You have prior stress fracture history on this side, correct?"

"Yes. Spring." Anika hated how childlike the single syllable sounded.

"Based on focal tenderness and impact pain, continuing puts you at high risk of worsening a bony stress injury. I advise retirement now."

Ravi had stepped through the door and now stood one pace behind Morales, hands open at his sides as if he had forgotten what to do with them.

"Can she finish this game if she shortens movement?" he asked, too quickly.

Morales did not match his speed. She slowed down the way people in authority did when they knew someone wanted to rush them past caution.

"No," she said. "This is not about fitness discomfort. This is localized tibial pain with load intolerance in an athlete with prior fracture history. If she continues, she could turn a manageable setback into a complete fracture and a much longer shutdown."

Ravi's jaw jumped. "How long if it's a flare?"

"We image first. Could be days, could be weeks, could be worse if you gamble right now."

Cora stood near the short line, racket hanging loose in her hand, trying not to stare and failing. Her face had lost its polished boredom. She rubbed the dachshund charm once with her thumb and looked away.

Anika sat on the bench with a towel around her shoulders and watched the sponsor table through the glass. One of the lanyard men was already typing on his phone. Beside him, a woman in a camel coat and bright red sneakers spoke into a headset mic, then shook rain off her sleeve with irritated little flicks. Anika recognized her from the pre-event briefing packet Ravi had shown her: Lindsay Cho, pathway liaison.

Lindsay walked to the court door as officials conferred. She was probably thirty, maybe younger, with eyeliner sharp enough to cut paper and two tiny silver safety pins in one ear. When she smiled, it looked trained. When she stopped smiling, a crease stayed between her eyebrows as if the expression never fully left.

"Ravi, can I borrow you for a second?" she asked from the threshold. "Just outside?"

Ravi checked Anika first.

"Go," Anika said. She wrapped both hands around her water bottle because her fingers had started shaking.

Through the glass she could not hear them, but she could read enough in posture. Lindsay spoke with her palms up, professional concern face on. Ravi kept glancing back toward Anika and then down at the floor. Lindsay leaned in closer, voice lower, then tapped the sponsor badge against her chest as if to remind him what she represented.

Morales stayed by Anika's bench. She opened a small notepad and wrote with a fountain pen that scratched softly in the pauses.

"You asked for medical before total failure on that leg," she said. "That timing is in the chart."

Anika laughed once, ugly and brief. "I didn't call it yet."

"You asked for medical. That matters for the record." Morales capped the pen. "Retirement call is still yours."

"I'm not carrying it alone." Anika tracked Ravi and Lindsay through the glass. "He's carrying it too."

"Then make sure he says the same thing you said in here."

Lindsay and Ravi came back inside. Ravi's face had gone colorless except for two high spots in his cheeks. Lindsay kept the professional smile on but her jaw was set.

"Okay," Lindsay said, palms out. "If we log this as tactical pending imaging, tomorrow's review stays live--"

"No," Morales said. "I'm charting medical retirement: focal tibial pain, load intolerance, prior fracture history."

Lindsay turned to Ravi. "I'm not asking her to keep playing. I'm asking for wording that buys twenty-four hours before the board locks."

Anika heard auto-freezes and wanted to throw her bottle at the glass.

Ravi spoke before she could.

"Auto-freezes means what, exactly?" he asked.

"Officially, the offer pauses," Lindsay said. "In practice, pauses this late usually die. There are later windows."

"Sometimes," Ravi repeated.

"Yes. Sometimes." Lindsay's voice thinned. "I'm trying to keep one door unlocked if we file it tactical--"

"Do not relabel a fracture-risk presentation as strategy," Morales said.

Ravi met Anika's eyes. She had seen that look before when overdue notices came in and he pretended he had not opened them. Calculating, terrified, angry at the math itself.

"So pause means withdrawn with softer paint?" he asked.

"For this cycle, yes," Lindsay said.

Anika felt heat rise behind her eyes. She did not cry. She thumbed the ridges on the bottle cap instead: one, two, three, four, five.

"Dad," she said.

He turned to her immediately.

"If I go back in, I might break it." Her voice shook anyway. "Not maybe sore. Break it."

Lindsay opened her mouth. Morales cut in first.

"That is correct based on current signs," Morales said.

"And if I break it, I'm out months." Anika kept talking because if she stopped she would lose nerve. "Then no showcase, no season, maybe suspension if I hide symptoms again."

Ravi's fingers flexed at his sides. He took one breath, then another, like he was learning how.

"What do you want me to do?" he asked her.

The question hit harder than the pain. He had asked her preference before, about food, bus routes, even practice music. He had never asked it like this, with real authority attached.

"I want to retire," she said. "Medical. Not fake tactical." She swallowed. "I want my leg to work in December."

Ravi nodded once. "Okay."

Lindsay's smile disappeared for good. "Then I need your confirmation now for the report."

He turned toward her, and when he spoke the old bark was gone, replaced by flat finality.

"Door closes, it closes," he said. "We're done today. She's retiring on medical advice." He glanced at Anika. "Her decision."

The referee called both players to center for formal retirement procedure. Cora came forward, face sweaty and open in a way Anika had never seen from her.

"I hate winning like this," Cora said quietly, not for cameras.

"Then don't act happy," Anika said, and she tried to smile so Cora would know it was not a knife.

Cora gave a tiny snort. "I'm not that good an actor." She squeezed Anika's shoulder once, awkward and brief. "Take care of it. For real."

The referee stepped between them with the clipboard and asked, "Retirement confirmed?" Anika said yes out loud, then signed beside Morales's note while her hand shook hard enough to drag the pen line uphill. Cora's palm was damp when they shook; her grip tightened once and let go.

When the announcement came over the house mic, it cracked on Anika's name and then settled: "Bell defeats Iyer by medical retirement, fourth game, five-three." A volunteer at the sponsor table uncapped a thick black marker and drew one hard line through ANIKA IYER on the printed "Tomorrow Showcase" sheet taped to a stanchion.

Applause came in scattered patches from people who were not sure what emotion was appropriate. On the board beside court one, the score froze where she had left it: Bell d. Iyer ret., 8-11, 10-12, 11-6, 5-3.

Anika sat while Morales filled out the incident sheet. Ravi stood behind her chair with one hand on the backrest, not touching her, just there. Lindsay paced with her headset tight to one ear, voice clipped and flat.

"Yes, medical retirement," Lindsay said into the mic. "Documented by event physician. Remove her from tomorrow's board."

Ten minutes later, a local livestream guy with perfect hair and a phone gimbal drifted over for post-match comments. He held the mic at Ravi because Anika was a minor and looked ready to snap it in half.

"Mr. Iyer, rough end to a huge semifinal," he said in his bright practiced tone. "Did she call it, or did medical pull her?"

Lindsay, three feet away, gave Ravi the smallest shake of her head.

Ravi kept his eyes on the camera. "Medical."

"She finished that last rally first. Why stop after?"

"Not my kid." Ravi's jaw tightened. "Old fracture site lit up. Staff said stop. She stopped. She's eleven. No match and no package are worth her leg."

The interviewer blinked, thrown off script. "Can she get out on her own?"

"She'll walk out with us," Ravi said.

The clip was online before they left court one. Anika knew because two kids from another draw glanced at their phones, then at her, then away.

Lindsay stepped in front of Ravi with her tablet. "Need guardian acknowledgment now. It posts as medical retirement, and this cycle's offer comes off the board. November committee can still--"

"Say withdrawn," Ravi said. "No soft words."

"It's withdrawn for this cycle," Lindsay said. "November review is committee discretion if--"

"Don't sell tomorrow tonight," Ravi said. "Log medical. Leave it there."

"Already logged." She held out the screen. "Sign."

He signed without reading the rest.

Morales came back with discharge paperwork and a photocopied referral sheet. "Queens Ortho has a 3:40 opening," she said. "If you miss that, next slot is tomorrow morning. Bring prior scans, insurance card, and this note. No impact activity until imaging review."

"We're going straight there," Ravi said.

Morales handed the clipboard to Anika first. "Sign here confirming you understand the restriction."

Anika signed. The pen line jumped once at the end of her last name.

Outside, rain came down in thin needles. Ravi angled their one intact umbrella over her right side, then called the depot before they hit the subway stairs.

"I can't take the late shift tonight," he said into the phone. "Put me down unpaid."

On the platform, he opened the utility app and pushed the arrears payment out by seven days. A red late-fee line appeared before he finished blinking.

At home the apartment smelled like damp wool and fried onions from downstairs. Ravi reheated Priya's lemon rice while Anika sat at the kitchen table with her leg up and her notebook open to a blank page.

She wrote: `Semifinal retirement. 8-11, 10-12, 11-6, 5-3. Pain at medial right tibia. Called medical myself.`

She added one more line and stopped there: `Offer withdrawn.`

Ravi set the plate down, then dropped the folded sponsor packet beside the salt tin instead of in the drawer. The paper had softened at the creases from his thumb.

"How bad is tonight?" Anika asked.

He did not sit. "Urgent copay first. Utility fee Friday. Overtime gone."

She ate anyway because hunger made her hands shake. When they left for the clinic, Ravi handed her the apartment keys before he locked the door and checked twice that the imaging folder was in his bag.

At Queens Ortho they got x-rays, four angles, ten minutes total. At checkout, the clerk stapled a new problem to the discharge packet: x-rays were inconclusive, MRI required prior authorization, earliest covered slot next Thursday unless they paid nine hundred forty dollars up front tonight. Ravi signed a payment-plan form for the visit they had just had, folded the no-impact order into the imaging folder, and they walked back into the rain without a diagnosis.


# Chapter 18

The whiteboard outside Court 2 still had yesterday's handwriting on it.

Ravi had waited for her pace from the train, then peeled off toward the depot with his phone already in his hand.

The imaging folder from Queens Ortho rode in her backpack, corners gone soft while the MRI authorization sat in limbo.

BEGINNER LADDER 6-8 PM, someone had written in purple marker, and then underlined it hard enough to tear the skin of the board. Jonah erased only half of it before his shoulder gave up on the job and he tossed the marker eraser to Anika. The square of felt hit her chest and dropped to the floor.

"You want this clean, you do it," he said, already moving toward the equipment closet with his bucket of balls knocking against his knee.

He always moved like he had three places to be and only two knees left. Tape wrapped his right wrist from mid-palm to forearm, the same yellowing athletic tape he reused until the edges curled gray. His thermos, dented at the bottom, sat open on the scorer's table and smelled like strong coffee and cardamom.

Anika bent and picked up the eraser. Purple marker dust streaked her fingers and smelled chemical-sweet. Air pushed through the vents in uneven breaths, while one of the hallway floor fans clicked every fourth rotation. Under it all, an older player on the side wall kept a steady pop-pop-pop that made the floorboards answer.

On her left wrist, Maya's watch sat snug under the cuff of her long-sleeve tee. She had changed the battery two weeks ago with a tiny screwdriver from Priya's junk drawer. Now the display was crisp again, seconds stepping forward without stutter. She kept looking at it as if it might change into something else if she watched long enough.

A few minutes later the first kids drifted in, each with a parent and a different version of nerves.

A girl in oversized safety goggles arrived first, dragging a racket bag with a faded unicorn patch and one broken zipper tooth. She introduced herself to the desk volunteer without looking up.

"Rina Patel," she said. "My mom's outside parking. She says if I don't text her when we're done she'll come in and embarrass me."

She said it like it was a scientific warning. Her right shoelace was neon green and the left was plain white. Both were tied in perfect double knots.

Two minutes later a skinny boy with a swim-team parka tied around his waist leaned on the hallway wall and bounced on the balls of his feet while he waited. Chlorine seemed baked into his skin. He had black Sharpie on his hand where somebody had written a grocery list and then washed half of it off.

"Luis Ortega," he told Jonah. "Can we start with serves? I watched videos. I know where to hit."

"We're starting with feet," Jonah said.

Luis groaned like he'd been handed taxes.

Then came twins, Nia and Nikhil, who argued in whispers about whether squash balls were hollow, and a tall girl named Huda who wore a denim jacket over her athletic shirt and kept touching the silver crescent pin at her collarbone whenever anyone looked at her. She parked herself near the back wall and studied everyone like she was compiling data.

Anika stood by the front wall with a clipboard and a whistle she did not want. She had said yes to assisting the clinic in the voice she used for school attendance, flat and efficient and impossible to read. The yes had come out of her mouth last week before she could stage a better answer. Jonah had called it helpful. Priya had called it healthy. Ravi had called it strategic, because every hour she volunteered counted toward reduced court fees.

Anika had not called it anything.

She had told herself it was court time. She had told herself she'd run beginners through split-step and racket prep, then stay another hour and ghost until her calves shook, maybe another twenty minutes of star drills after that, quiet and private while Jonah locked up his ball bucket and pretended he did not see. She had planned the extra work down to sip counts and bathroom stops on the Q32 ride home. In her notebook she had drawn a tiny box next to the clinic block and left it empty, waiting.

When her watch ticked into the next quarter hour, Jonah clapped once, hard. "Circle up. If you're chewing gum, it's done. If you're scared, fine. Move anyway."

The kids made a loose circle. Rina stood with her toes exactly on a paint chip in the floor, as if balance depended on it.

"You all know Anika?" Jonah asked. "She's helping me this season. Listen when she talks."

Six pairs of eyes turned to Anika. A spark jumped in her chest, the part of her that liked being looked at as if she knew more than she did. It came with danger every time.

"Shoes tied tight," she said. "Racket up before the ball comes off the wall. Don't wait to see what happens. Move first."

Her voice came out sharper than she intended. Nia blinked. Luis stopped bouncing.

Jonah gave her a side look, not disapproving, just measuring.

They began with shadow swings, then split-step timing, then simple feed-and-return drills near the short line. The younger kids kept drifting backward toward the glass as if distance could protect them from missing. Anika corrected grips one by one, pressing fingers into place on sweaty handles.

"Thumb lower, Rina. No, lower than that."

"Like this?"

"Like you're holding a bird you don't want to crush."

Rina's forehead wrinkled. "I've never held a bird."

"Okay. Like a banana that's almost bruised."

That one worked. Rina nodded once, serious.

By the second rotation Luis had already started asking for extra repetitions.

"Can I do doubles?" he asked. "Can I do, like, twenty in a row while they rest?"

Anika set another ball on the floor with her shoe and watched him lean forward, desperate to go.

There it was. The ache she recognized before she had words for it. It showed up as hunger in the eyes, but the hunger was not for the game. It was for rules that hurt and then rewarded. It was for the clean equation where pain plus obedience equaled safety.

"No doubles," she said.

Luis frowned. "Why not?"

"Because you're skipping your feet when you're tired. Then your shoulder does weird stuff. Then you start serving with your elbow and we spend next month fixing it."

"But I can handle it."

"So can a shopping cart with one bad wheel," Jonah said from behind them. "Doesn't mean it's going straight."

A couple of kids laughed. Luis did not.

They rotated again. Huda missed two forehands and stepped back with her jaw clenched, pretending to retie a shoe that did not need tying. Nikhil took too big a backswing and hit himself in the shin; he pretended it did not hurt until his eyes watered. Nia started coaching him in a fierce whisper that sounded like their grandmother lived in her throat.

Anika moved down the line, feeding easy balls with her left hand and catching returns with her racket face. Her own body kept trying to speed up the drill. She wanted to tighten everything: less talking, more reps, shorter breaks, sharper commands. She could feel that script scrolling in her muscles, the one that said if you were not near collapse you were lying about effort.

Her watch buzzed once mid-drill, a reminder she had set this morning: water.

She ignored it.

Before she got through the next line of kids, it buzzed again, persistent now because she had set the repeat by mistake.

"Coach," Rina said, quiet but direct. "Your watch is yelling."

The kids looked at Anika's wrist.

Heat ran up Anika's neck. She hated being caught by an alarm, by an object, by anything that made her seem less in control.

"Water break," she said. "Three minutes."

Jonah did not smile, but his mouth shifted like he approved.

The kids dropped rackets and swarmed the cooler. Luis drank too fast and coughed. Nia and Nikhil argued about whose bottle had the Spider-Man sticker first. Huda stood apart and took tiny careful sips, then pulled a folded paper out of her jacket pocket and read it while she drank.

Anika glanced at the paper when Huda looked up.

"What is that?" she asked.

Huda folded it fast. "My grandma's blood pressure numbers. I've got to remind her after Maghrib to take the blue pill. I wrote it down so I don't forget if practice runs long."

She said it defensively, like she expected ridicule.

"That's smart," Anika said.

Huda looked surprised, then shrugged. "She says all sports are fine as long as I do my homework and nobody breaks my face."

"Reasonable policy," Jonah muttered.

When the break ended, they switched to conditioned rallies: one bounce only, hit crosscourt, recover to the T. It was supposed to be boring enough for fundamentals and hard enough to keep kids from wandering. Luis asked again for more.

"Can I stay after? I can do solo ghosts. My brother says if I don't grind now I'll be behind forever."

Anika's throat tightened at the word grind.

"How old's your brother?" she asked.

"Seventeen. He quit basketball because coaches were trash."

"And now he's your coach?"

Luis grinned, then saw her face and straightened. "He's... loud."

"Mine was too," Anika said before she could stop herself.

Luis waited for more. She did not give it.

Late in the footwork relay, Nikhil landed wrong and grabbed his calf. He tried to wave everyone off with a huge embarrassed smile.

"I'm fine, keep going, keep going."

The relay stalled around him.

Anika knelt so they were eye level. "Where?"

He touched the outside of his lower leg.

"Cramp or pull?"

"Cramp. I think."

"Any sharp pain?"

He hesitated. His twin watched him with a stare that could strip wallpaper.

"A little sharp."

"Then you sit."

"No, I can do one more."

"Nope."

Nikhil's face collapsed into the kind of anger kids use when fear has nowhere else to go. "I hate sitting."

"Yeah," Anika said. "Me too. Bench anyway."

Jonah tossed her a thumbs-up from across the court, quick and almost hidden.

Nia escorted her brother to the bench and sat beside him without being asked. She took out a tiny packet of gummy bears from her pocket and tore it open with her teeth.

"Don't tell Amma I gave you sugar before dinner," she whispered.

Anika heard it and looked away so they could keep the privacy.

By the final stretch, clinic energy had thinned into sweat and shouts and the squeak of soles on worn wood. Parents crowded behind the glass, checking phones, tapping weather apps, calculating traffic. The sky outside the high windows had gone pewter. Rain started, light at first, then steady enough to drum the roof.

Damp weather always found Anika's right shin, the same strip that had sent her out of the semifinal. The tenderness crept in like a wire threaded under skin and tightened turn by turn. The pain was not severe, but it was enough to remind her that body and consequence were still connected, no matter how badly she wanted to believe in pure will.

Jonah blew the whistle for final cleanup.

"Balls in the bucket. Thank your hitting partner. If you leave tape or wrappers on my floor, I'll make you run lines while I eat a sandwich and judge your life choices."

The kids laughed, which was the point.

They lined up for fist bumps. Rina bumped with absolute seriousness. "Can we do that banana grip again next week?"

"We'll do it every week," Anika said.

Huda lifted her crescent pin and smiled with half her mouth. "My grandma says thanks for not yelling. She can hear yelling through the phone and then she starts yelling too."

Luis hovered after everyone else moved away. "If I come early Saturday, can I hit before clinic?"

Anika knew the reflex. Wake earlier. Do more. Earn worth.

That reflex stood in her mouth like metal.

"Come fifteen minutes early," she said. "We'll do warm-up right. No hero stuff."

Luis looked disappointed for a second, then nodded. "Okay."

When the hallway emptied, Jonah rolled the ball bucket toward the closet. "You looked like you wanted to crawl out of your skin halfway through."

Anika stacked cones by color. "I was fine."

"Sure." He leaned the bucket against the wall. "You want a solo court for thirty?"

There it was. Permission shaped exactly like temptation.

Her chest jumped. Thirty could become sixty. Sixty could become ninety if she kept her head down and told nobody. In her head she was already ghosting ladders to ten, then diagonal bursts, then serves until her shoulder burned clean. Right behind it came the calm afterward, dangerously clean, where numbers replaced thought.

Then Nikhil's face flashed up from the relay, right at the moment she told him to sit. Luis asking to grind came right after. Last came her own voice saying no and meaning it for them but not for herself.

She pulled open her notebook, the one with Maya's grocery list still faintly visible on the inside cover. The page for today had two columns: clinic and train. In the clinic column she had written the kids' names and tiny notes: Rina stiff wrist, Huda hides confidence, Luis speed without brakes. The train column was blank.

"I can do feeding drills for the six o'clock group," she said.

Jonah raised one eyebrow. "That's not what I asked."

"I know."

"You trying to be noble?"

"No. I'm trying not to do dumb math."

For the first time all afternoon, he smiled fully. "Good answer. Court 3. Kids in ten."

The six o'clock group came in with rental goggles fogged from rain and shoelaces half-tied from a hurry downstairs. Jonah handed Anika a fresh bucket and jerked his chin toward the service boxes.

"Keep them honest," he said. "No circus."

Anika set three cones at the T. "Recover to center every shot. If your heel skids, the rep doesn't count."

A broad-shouldered seventh grader named Evan pointed to the backcourt and grinned. "Can we do ghost sprints? My cousin does fifty."

The reflex rose up fast: yes, more, now.

"No," she said. "You can do six clean recoveries, water, then six more. We train what you can repeat."

He groaned, but he moved. The set got messy by rep four, better by rep six, and clean by the second round after she shortened their swing path and made them breathe before each feed.

Near the end of the block, Evan came back, sweaty and bright-eyed. "One burnout set? Please. I'll puke after, I swear."

Her pulse jumped at the word burnout. She could hear herself saying yes, then tacking on ten hidden minutes after the kids left because the court was still warm and the lights were still on.

"No burnout," she said. "Two precise serves each and you're done."

He dragged out a dramatic sigh and obeyed. A little before closing, the last kid fist-bumped her and ran to his mother in the hall.

Court 3 emptied. One ball sat in the service box like an invitation. Anika stepped toward it, felt her calves load for the first secret ghost, then opened her notebook instead and wrote in the train column: I wanted extras and said no.

She kicked the ball back to the bucket and shut the lid.

By the time she got home, her socks were damp from rain leaking through the seam of her right shoe. The apartment stairwell smelled like bleach and stale frying oil. She unlocked the door quietly, but Ravi still heard and called from the kitchen, "You eat yet?"

"No."

"There's upma on the stove. Heat it."

His voice carried the clipped workday rhythm he used when he had ten things to do and no spare tone for any of them.

The kitchen light was too bright. Ravi had spread envelopes across the table in three stacks, each weighted down with something small so the fan from the window unit would not scatter them. One stack had the TV remote on top. One had the salt cellar. One had Maya's measuring spoon set, the quarter teaspoon missing as usual.

Red notices sat in the middle stack.

Anika did not pretend not to read. QUEENS ONCOLOGY BILLING SERVICES. ACCOUNT CURRENTLY PAST DUE. AMOUNT DUE THIS CYCLE: $640.00. NEXT REVIEW DATE: NOV 15.

The utility envelope had a yellow stripe and less ceremony: FINAL REMINDER before service interruption on outstanding balance. $1,240.12 remaining after Aug 28 payment of $620.00.

She had first seen that debt in April at the school fee-waiver desk, printed in black at the top of the hardship packet: $1,860.12. Ravi had paid $620 at the bodega bill terminal in late summer while she watched the receipt spool out and curl on itself. $1,860.12 minus $620.00 left $1,240.12. Same debt, thinner by one rent-sized bite.

Ravi stood at the counter in his dispatch uniform undershirt, tying his shoes for a second shift. This shift was app-driving, not depot dispatch. His regular lanyard hung from a cabinet knob, bus logo turned inward. He massaged his left ankle once before tightening the laces. A small hiss escaped him. He ignored it.

"You got in late," he said.

"Clinic ran long."

"Paid long?"

"No."

"Okay." He tapped the red envelope with two fingers. "Then it has to convert to something else. Goodwill, lower fees, contacts, whatever."

Anika put her bag down and opened the pot on the stove. The upma had formed a skin. She stirred it back in.

"Did you eat?" she asked.

"At the depot. Bad vending machine almonds and shame." He checked his phone. "I've got airport queue from eight to midnight if demand holds."

"And tomorrow?"

"Depot at six. Home for two hours. Then maybe one more app shift."

He said maybe the way some people said please, as if politeness might make the schedule less brutal.

Anika spooned food into a bowl. Her stomach clenched from the first smell even though she was hungry. She set the bowl down and reached for water instead.

Ravi watched without looking like he was watching.

"How was clinic?" he asked.

"Loud."

"Useful loud or chaos loud?"

"Both."

"How's the shin?"

She took two beats to answer. "It's sore in the rain, not sharp."

"Okay." He nodded once. "No heroics tonight. You still have your planned session?"

"Forty-five minutes plus mobility."

"With Jonah?"

"He'll start me. Then he has the adult league check-in."

Ravi picked up the oncology notice and folded it back along the same crease, precise and angry.

"If you need to cut a session, cut a session," he said. "We're not doing another injury cycle."

The Ravi from before would have said the opposite. The one in front of her sometimes forgot himself, but tonight he sounded like a man repeating a promise to keep it alive.

Anika leaned on the sink and stared at the wet alley beyond the window. Somebody had hung jeans on a fire escape rail; rain made them flap like flags.

"Do you ever miss it?" she asked before she could edit the question.

Ravi frowned. "Miss what?"

"The plan from before. More hours. More everything."

His laugh was short and dry. "I miss certainty. I don't miss the plan." He put both hands on the table and looked at the envelopes instead of her. "That plan was me pretending I could outwork a funeral bill."

The words landed heavy and practical, like tools dropped on tile.

"You don't have to come watch tonight," Anika said. "I can go and come back."

"I know." He checked his keys, then checked them again. "I want to watch first twenty. Then I leave for the shift."

He grabbed his light rain jacket from the chair and shrugged into it. The sleeve cuff had begun to fray. He noticed Anika looking.

"I'm not buying a new one," he said. "Before you even ask."

"I wasn't asking."

"Good."

He pulled a folded sticky note from his pocket and set it near her bowl. Two phone numbers were written in his compact block handwriting: depot desk and app support.

"If I don't answer, call twice. If it's urgent, call Priya after that."

Anika nodded. She tucked the note under the salt cellar so it would not slide.

They rode the bus to the rec center mostly in silence, knees angled away from other passengers. The windows fogged. A toddler in front of them sang one line from a cartoon theme over and over while his grandmother pretended not to hear. Ravi kept checking the time and rubbing his thumb against the corner of his phone case until the plastic squeaked.

At the courts, he stood outside the glass with his arms folded while Anika warmed up: easy drives, controlled length, short movement patterns Jonah had approved. She felt him there like weather.

Eighteen minutes into warm-up, Ravi tapped his watch and pointed to the door. He mouthed, "Call me," then left at a jog that looked painful in the first three steps and less painful after momentum took over.

Jonah stepped in for the last part of warm-up, fed her a sequence of deep rails, and then handed her the timer puck they used for interval sets.

"Plan?" he asked.

"Two blocks of six-minute movement, one block serve targets, then mobility."

"And?"

"And stop."

"At?"

"Forty-five."

He nodded. "I'll be on Court 1 with adults. If you start negotiating with yourself, don't."

"Okay."

"I'm serious, kid. You have lawyer brain when you're scared. You'll draft an exception clause by minute thirty."

"I know."

He walked away before she could say anything else.

The court felt huge with only one body in it.

Anika started the timer and moved.

Split-step, lunge, recover. Split-step, back corner, recover. Her breath found rhythm by the second minute. Sweat came easy in the wet air. The right shin hummed but held.

Twelve minutes in she switched to serves. Target towels hung from the front wall: low left, mid right. She hit six good, four long, two tins, reset. Hunger sharpened under her ribs. She could feel how little forty-five minutes was compared with what she had done before. Forty-five felt like a warm-up pretending to be discipline.

She served again and missed the towel by half a racket length.

Her brain started bargaining in a voice that sounded calm and reasonable.

Just add ten. No one will know.

You already paid the bus fare. Make it count.

Debt does not care about moderation.

She wiped her face with the bottom of her shirt and kept serving.

On the back wall, her reflection flashed with each turn: small body, damp shirt, watch face catching light at the top of her swing. The watch looked like command again if she stared at it wrong.

By minute thirty-two she felt the first pull to continue past the plan. It was not pain, and it was not fatigue. It was a cleaner, meaner sensation, compulsion in neat clothes.

She walked to the service box, bounced the ball, and looked up at the glass. No one was watching now. Jonah's adult group was arguing about lets on Court 1. Two men in business polos raised their voices while a woman in neon shoes rolled her eyes and rubbed her hamstring. Nobody cared what Anika did on Court 3.

That made it harder.

She hit one more serve, then another.

Her watch buzzed at forty minutes, the first warning.

She told herself she would stop after one clean ball, then served anyway. One, two, three, four.

On the fifth she lunged late and her right shoe slipped on sweat near the short line. A hot wire of pain jumped up her shin. She slapped her left palm to the side wall to catch herself, and the racket frame clipped her own knee on the rebound.

The sixth ball hit tin and died.

She bent over, coughing. Acid rose into her throat. Her hand shook so hard the ball rattled in her grip.

Nobody had called stop. Nobody had noticed. That freedom felt like a trap door.

The watch buzzed again at forty-five, longer. End.

She pressed stop on the timer puck and said to the empty glass, "I'm done. I'm writing it exactly."

She held her thumb over reset until it trembled.

In her mind, memory snapped open without asking: Maya in the kitchen, in the blue house shorts with tiny yellow lemons, tightening this same watch around Anika's wrist because the band was one notch too loose.

"Don't make it too tight," Maya had said. "You're growing every week. I need to leave room."

Back then Anika had wanted it tight, almost painful, so it would not slip during school recess. Maya had laughed and loosened it back.

She dropped the puck on her notebook outside the glass and wrote in the train column with a damp, cramped hand:

I ignored the 40 warning and hit 6 extra serves.
I would've kept going if I hadn't slipped.

The ink bled where her knuckles spasmed.

Heat climbed her chest and face in a rush that felt almost feverish. She could hear Luis asking for doubles and her own voice saying no hero stuff, and now the words sounded counterfeit in her mouth. She hated how quickly she had tried to hide from herself.

She tried to start mobility work, but her right calf seized on the first stretch. "Fuck. Fuck this," she said, loud enough to bounce off the back glass, and smacked the mat once with an open palm before she could stop herself. A player on Court 1 glanced over.

Jonah appeared in the doorway while she was shaking out the cramp.

"I went past the first warning," she said before he could ask. "Six shots. I slipped, shin lit up, and I logged it."

"Time?" he asked.

"Forty-five."

He held out his hand. "Timer."

She passed him the puck.

"No solo block tomorrow," he said. "You open clinic with me, feed for six o'clock, and leave when I leave."

"That's a lot of rails."

"Yeah." He pocketed the timer. "That's the point. If you ask for extra court, answer's no."

He pulled out his phone. "I'm texting your dad so we're aligned."

Anika nodded once. "Do it."

Jonah looked at her for another beat. "Rina still wants banana grip Saturday."

"I can do banana grip."

"Do it for yourself too."

"I'll try."

"Trying counts if you actually change the plan."

Outside, rain had slowed to a mist. She packed her bag, slung it over one shoulder, and stepped into the hallway where the floor smelled like wet rubber and disinfectant. A little boy from the adult class stood by the vending machine in socks, shoes off, counting quarters into his palm. He came up short and stared at the coins like betrayal had happened personally.

Anika dug in her pocket and found two dimes. She handed them to him.

"Thanks," he said. "My dad says sports drinks are fake, but he buys one if I win points."

"How many points?"

"Three."

"Brutal policy."

He grinned and fed the coins into the machine.

Anika walked to the bus stop alone. The sidewalk shone black under streetlights. Her damp hair stuck to the back of her neck. Across the avenue, a laundromat glowed with rows of spinning silver drums, shirts and towels tumbling in identical circles.

She checked her phone. Two texts from Ravi.

8:41 PM: Airport line dead. Trying Midtown hotels.

9:03 PM: Don't wait up.

A third came while she watched.

9:11 PM: How was session?

She typed: `45 total. Ignored the 40 warning and went six over. Slipped, calf locked, and I scared myself. I stopped and logged it before I could lie. Jonah took my timer for tomorrow.`

She hit send before she could edit the sentence into something cleaner.

Three dots appeared, disappeared, appeared again.

`Saw Jonah's text and yours. No solo tomorrow and no debate. Priya can pick you up after clinic so you don't hang around the courts.`

Another bubble followed.

`I'm not mad. I just won't pretend six extra is nothing. Also picked up one airport fare finally. Might cover the oncology minimum if tips aren't trash.`

She read the message twice. No solo tomorrow sat next to oncology minimum like they belonged in the same sentence.

The bus arrived with wet brakes squealing. She climbed on and took a seat near the back. The watch on her wrist ticked tiny, steady beats against her pulse.

Back home she left her shoes by the door, reheated the upma, and ate standing at the sink while steam fogged the window. The red envelopes were still on the table, waiting for morning and the next set of decisions.

Before bed she set two alarms on Maya's watch: 6:10 for school, 3:00 for clinic prep. Then she took the watch off and left it face-down on the desk, out of reach.

She lay in the dark and listened to the building settle, pipes knocking twice in the wall, somebody upstairs dragging a chair over tile. Her right calf twitched every few minutes. Twice she reached toward the desk before she was fully awake, already rehearsing how to bargain for ten more minutes.

When sleep came, it felt thin and conditional, like a truce she still had to defend in the morning.
